iv  
PREFACE	  
The	  present	  Master	  Thesis	  is	  a	  30	  ECTS	  points	  project	  submitted	  for	  the	  accomplishment	  of	  a	  
M.Sc.	  in	  Environmental	  Engineering	  at	  the	  University	  of	  Padova.	  More	  precisely,	  thanks	  to	  the	  
program	  Erasmus+	  for	  Traineeship,	  I	  had	  the	  possibility	  to	  collaborate	  with	  DTU	  Environment	  
at	  the	  Technical	  University	  of	  Denmark	  (DTU)	  for	  the	  development	  of	  this	  project.	  
	  
The	   data	   used	   in	   this	   project	   are	   obtained	   from	   the	   AMOK	   project	   conducted	   at	   the	  
wastewater	   treatment	   plant	   of	   Viby.	   The	   model	   of	   the	   catchment	   of	   Viby	   was	   used	   with	  
Matlab	  as	  an	  interface.	  
	  
This	   thesis	   is	   supervised	   by	   Assistant	   Professor	   Luca	   Vezzaro	   and	   Professor	   Peter	   Steen	  
Mikkelsen.

vii	  
ABSTRACT	  
In	   the	   last	   years,	   increased	   attention	   has	   been	   paid	   to	   water	   quality	   monitoring	   in	   sewer	  
systems	   and	   in	   particular	   in	   CSO	   structures.	   In	   fact,	   pollutant	   loads	   discharged	   into	   the	  
environment	  without	  a	  proper	  treatment	  can	  adversely	  affect	  the	  quality	  of	  receiving	  water	  
bodies.	  According	  to	  the	  Water	  Framework	  Directive,	  a	  good	  ecological	  status	  was	  supposed	  to	  
be	   achieved	   in	   the	   aquatic	   environment	   by	   the	   end	   of	   2015,	   and	   therefore	   a	   good	   level	   of	  
knowledge	   about	   water	   quality	   in	   the	   different	   components	   of	   the	   drainage	   system	   is	  
necessary.	  In	  this	  perspective	  of	  increasing	  interest	  in	  water	  quality	  monitoring,	  several	  on-­‐line	  
sensors,	   giving	   high	   time	   resolution	   measurements,	   have	   been	   installed	   at	   the	   inlet	   CSO	  
structure	  of	  the	  wastewater	  treatment	  plant	  of	  Viby.	  However,	  the	  collection	  of	  high	  quality	  
data	  from	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  is	  always	  problematic,	  partly	  due	  to	  the	  arduous	  conditions	  present	  
in	  sewer	  systems	  and	  partly	  to	  the	  lack	  of	  cleaning	  and	  maintenance,	  which	  lead	  to	  clogging	  
and	   fouling	   of	   the	   sensors.	   Therefore	   the	   level	   of	   confidence	   in	   the	   measurements	   is	  
decreased	   by	   the	   presence	   of	   some	   uncertainties.	   The	   water	   quality	   data	   about	   ammonia	  
collected	  through	  the	  project	  AMOK	  from	  June	  2013	  to	  December	  2015	  have	  been	  used	  in	  this	  
study,	  together	  with	  the	  flow	  measurements.	  	  
	  
A	  model	  of	  the	  catchment	  connected	  to	  the	  WWTP	  of	  Viby	  created	  with	  the	  software	  WEST	  
was	  used	  with	  Matlab	  as	  an	  interface,	  so	  that	  a	  calibration	  of	  the	  water	  quantity	  and	  quality	  
module	  has	  been	  performed	  for	  11	  rain	  events	  and	  a	  long	  term	  event	  on	  the	  most	  sensitive	  
parameters.	   For	   the	   water	   quality	   module	   two	   different	   calibration	   methods	   have	   been	  
analysed:	  a	  traditional	  one	  (fminsearch)	  and	  an	  uncertainty	  based	  one	  (modified	  Monte	  Carlo).	  
The	  latter	  method	  has	  been	  chosen	  in	  order	  to	  take	  into	  account	  both	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  
bounds	  and	  the	  uncertainties	  of	  each	  observed	  data	  point,	  so	  that	  a	  measure	  of	  the	  reliability	  
of	  the	  results	  can	  be	  given.	  Moreover,	  a	  calculation	  of	  the	  ammonia	  loads	  was	  performed.	  The	  
uncertainty	   based	   calibration	   turned	   out	   to	   be	   more	   trustworthy	   than	   the	   traditional	   one	  
when	   the	   quality	   of	   the	   measured	   data	   is	   low,	   i.e.	   they	   are	   neither	   precise	   nor	   accurate.	  
Therefore,	  by	  taking	  into	  account	  the	  uncertainties,	  the	  risk	  related	  to	  the	  use	  of	  low	  quality	  
data	  is	  reduced.

Figure	  2:	  schematic	  representation	  of	  the	  hierarchy	  about	  water	  regulation	  both	  in	  Europe	  and	  
Denmark	  (adapted	  from	  Elahi,	  2014).	  .....................................................................................	  6	  
Figure	   3:	   schematic	   representation	   of	   the	   concepts	   of	   accuracy	   and	   precision.	   Accurate	  
measurement	   is	   when	   the	   black	   dots	   fall	   in	   the	   bull’s-­‐eye	   (that	   represents	   the	   accepted	  
reference	  value).	  Precise	  measurement	  is	  when	  the	  black	  dots	  are	  close	  one	  to	  each	  other	  (from	  
Gutierrez-­‐Osuna,	  2001).	  .......................................................................................................	  11	  
Figure	  4:	  graphical	  representation	  of	  sensitivity	  and	  offset	  errors.	  The	  blue	  line	  reproduces	  the	  
theoretical	  output	  of	  the	  model,	  while	  the	  red	  one	  represents	  the	  measured	  response	  of	  the	  
sensor	  (adapted	  from	  Bertrand-­‐Krajewski	  et	  al.,	  2000	  –	  cited	  in	  Jézéquel,	  2014).	  ....................	  12	  
Figure	  5:	  graphical	  explanation	  of	  the	  difference	  between	  error	  and	  uncertainty.	  Uncertainty	  
range	  is	  the	  numerical	  interval	  around	  the	  measured	  value	  in	  which	  the	  true	  value	  lies	  with	  
some	  probability.	  The	  measurement	  U	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  is	  the	  half-­‐width	  of	  that	  interval	  and	  it	  
is	  always	  positive	  (adapted	  from	  https://sisu.ut.ee/).	  ............................................................	  16	  
Figure	  6:	  screenshot	  of	  the	  submodels	  for	  the	  combined	  sewer	  system	  of	  the	  Viby	  catchment.	  22

1  
1. INTRODUCTION	  
“Monitoring	   consists	   in	   observing	   something	   and	   keeping	   a	   record	   of	   it.	   It	   is	   commonly	  
achieved	   with	   sensors”	   (Beaupré,	   2010).	   The	   data	   coming	   from	   a	   monitoring	   campaign	   can	  
have	  a	  high-­‐resolution	  time	  that	  can	  be	  as	  high	  as	  1	  measurement	  per	  second.	  However,	  the	  
quality	  of	  the	  collected	  data	  has	  to	  be	  evaluated	  before	  their	  use.	  The	  action	  of	  monitoring	  is	  
particularly	   important	   while	   developing	   project	   activities,	   since	   it	   can	   help	   in	   project	  
implementation	  and	  control.	  The	  aims	  of	  monitoring	  are:	  
• 
Design	  of	  a	  future	  system,	  with	  similar	  characteristics	  to	  the	  monitored	  one;	  
• 
Optimization	  of	  existing	  systems;	  
• 
Model	  calibration	  and	  validation;	  
• 
Control	  strategies	  for	  already	  existing	  systems.	  
	  
It	  is	  within	  this	  context	  that	  the	  AMOK	  project	  (Avanceret	  Online	  Måling	  af	  Overløbskvalitet,	  
that	  in	  English	  means	  Advance	  online	  Measuring	  of	  Overflow	  Quality)	  finds	  its	  place:	  it	  was	  a	  
water	   quality	   monitoring	   campaign	   (with	   the	   use	   of	   on-­‐line	   water	   quality	   sensors	   of	   high	  
temporal	  resolution)	  at	  the	  inlet	  of	  the	  wastewater	  treatment	  plant	  of	  Viby	  (Aarhus),	  before	  an	  
overflow	  structure.	  	  The	  information	  collected	  during	  the	  measuring	  campaigns	  is	  about	  flow,	  
temperature,	  pH,	  conductivity,	  ammonium	  and	  TSS	  concentration,	  but	  only	  the	  data	  on	  flow	  
and	  ammonium	  are	  used	  for	  this	  study.	  The	  time	  interval	  between	  two	  measurements	  is	  1	  
minute	  and	  the	  campaign	  lasted	  727	  days,	  from	  June	  2013	  to	  December	  2014.	  
	  
Combined	  Sewage	  Overflow	  structures	  (CSO)	  are	  relief	  structures	  that	  divert	  the	  part	  of	  the	  
wastewater	  flow	  that	  exceeds	  the	  plant	  capacity	  during	  high	  intensity	  rain	  events,	  i.e.	  when	  
the	   flow	   entering	   the	   WWTP	   is	   higher	   than	   the	   plant	   peak	   flow	   upon	   which	   it	   has	   been	  
designed.	  	  In	  those	  cases	  serious	  environmental	  problems	  can	  be	  experienced,	  because	  the	  
exceeding	  water	  is	  directly	  discharged	  into	  a	  surface	  water	  body	  without	  a	  proper	  treatment,	  
with	   adverse	   effects	   on	   the	   environment	   and	   human	   health.	   One	   of	   these	   effects	   is	  
eutrophication	  defined	  as	  “an	  increase	  in	  the	  rate	  of	  supply	  of	  organic	  matter	  in	  an	  ecosystem”	  
(Nixon,	  1995),	  that	  promotes	  an	  excessive	  algal	  growth	  and	  the	  consequent	  oxygen	  depletion,	  
but	   also	   the	   presence	   of	   bacteria	   and	   disease-­‐causing	   pathogens	   can	   lead	   to	   restriction	   on	  
human	   recreation	   and	   on	   drinking	   water	   consumption	   (USGS,	   2015).	   The	   pollution	   loads	  
resulting	   from	   CSO	   structures	   are	   particularly	   harmful	   because	   of	   the	   so-­‐called	   “first	   flush”	  
phenomenon,	  i.e.	  the	  initial	  surface	  run-­‐off	  of	  a	  rainstorm,	  that	  implies	  the	  remobilisation	  of	  
sewer	  deposits	  and	  slime	  (resuspended	  deposits)	  (Gruber	  et	  al.,	  2005).	  
	  
In	  order	  to	  solve	  these	  problems	  one	  of	  the	  solutions	  should	  be	  to	  increase	  the	  size	  of	  the	  
sewage	  system	  and	  to	  create	  large	  storage	  volumes	  for	  the	  excess	  of	  water	  during	  CSO	  events.	  
Nevertheless,	  this	  kind	  of	  solution	  is	  often	  very	  difficult,	  and	  also	  expensive,	  in	  an	  already	  built	  
up	  zone.	  Another	  option	  could	  be	  monitoring	  the	  water	  quality	  on	  the	  existing	  sewer	  systems	  
(or	  treatment	  plants)	  using	  high	  time	  resolution	  measurements,	  which	  is	  definitely	  less	  costly	  
of	   the	   previous	   solution.	   By	   using	   this	   approach,	   a	   better	   understanding	   on	   the	   pollution	  
phenomena	  is	  possible	  and	  a	  strategy,	  for	  ensuring	  the	  objectives	  to	  be	  met,	  can	  be	  set	  up	  
(Kopecny	  at	  al.,	  1999).	  
==PAGE:17==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
2  
However,	  the	  collection	  of	  high	  accuracy	  and	  high	  precision	  data	  is	  always	  troublesome	  due	  to	  
the	  challenging	  condition	  the	  sensors	  are	  subjected	  to	  (e.g.	  clogging,	  fouling),	  and	  this	  results	  
in	   noise,	   errors	   and	   sensors	   failure,	   also	   because	   their	   cleaning	   and	   maintenance	   is	   rarely	  
performed	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  When	  this	  is	  clearly	  observed,	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  do	  a	  calibration	  of	  
the	   sensors,	   in	   order	   to	   “evaluate	   and	   correct	   these	   common	   systematic	   errors”	   (Jézéquel,	  
2014).	  The	  verification	  of	  the	  data,	  by	  comparing	  the	  sensor	  data	  and	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  
measuring	  campaigns,	  in	  the	  state	  of	  art,	  should	  be	  done	  on	  a	  regular	  basis,	  but	  doing	  this	  is	  
not	  wide	  spread	  in	  practice,	  since	  it	  requires	  significant	  time	  and	  efforts.	  The	  result	  is	  that	  the	  
data	   show	   some	   irregularities	   and	   cannot	   be	   used,	   unless	   the	   flow,	   temperature,	   pH,	  
conductivity,	   ammonium	   and	   TSS	   concentration	   time	   series	   are	   cut	   into	   separate	   events	   in	  
which	  no	  irregularities	  can	  be	  detected.	  
	  
The	  use	  of	  models	  for	  urban	  drainage	  system	  management	  has	  gained	  particular	  importance	  
over	   the	   last	   few	   years,	   because	   they	   enable	   to	   investigate	   the	   different	   components	   of	   a	  
drainage	  system:	  the	  sewer	  system,	  the	  wastewater	  treatment	  plant	  and	  the	  receiving	  water	  
body	  (RWB).	  This	  requirement	  for	  an	  integrated	  approach	  was	  introduced	  by	  the	  EU	  2000/60	  
Directive	   (WFD,	   Water	   Framework	   Directive),	   according	   to	   which	   a	   global	   analysis	   on	   the	  
quality	  of	  water	  for	  the	  whole	  system	  should	  be	  done	  to	  achieve	  good	  ecological	  status	  for	  the	  
RWB	   by	   the	   end	   of	   2015	   (Directive	   2000/60/EC,	   2000).	   Moreover,	   the	   knowledge	   of	   water	  
quality	  data	  permits	  to	  estimate	  the	  quantity	  of	  pollutants	  discharged	  during	  overflow	  events	  
and	  to	  set	  up	  a	  strategy	  for	  diverting	  the	  flow	  where	  it	  has	  the	  lowest	  effect	  on	  the	  aquatic	  
environment	  (Lacour	  and	  Schütze,	  2011).	  Therefore	  a	  holistic	  model	  may	  be	  necessary	  for	  the	  
simulation	  of	  both	  the	  hydraulic	  part	  and	  the	  water	  quality	  part	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  
	  
A	  model	  of	  the	  Viby	  catchment	  was	  already	  created	  through	  the	  software	  WEST	  by	  Olesen	  in	  
2015,	   and	   in	   this	   work	   it	   has	   been	   used	   with	   Matlab	   as	   an	   interface,	   so	   that	   two	   different	  
calibrations,	  one	  on	  the	  hydraulic	  part	  and	  one	  on	  the	  water	  quality	  part,	  of	  the	  parameters	  
were	  performed.	  In	  order	  to	  do	  the	  calibration	  of	  the	  model	  on	  the	  water	  quality	  part,	  both	  
the	  model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  and	  the	  uncertainties	  from	  each	  measured	  data	  point	  should	  be	  
taken	  into	  account,	  since	  “uncertainties	  are	  inherent	  in	  all	  models	  and	  their	  total	  elimination	  is	  
not	  possible”	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  Previous	  studies	  have	  already	  demonstrated	  the	  possibility	  
of	  finding	  trends	  and	  pattern	  for	  the	  pollutants	  through	  an	  initial	  data	  analysis,	  but	  little	  has	  
been	   done	   on	   the	   assessment	   of	   the	   uncertainties	   in	   models.	   “This	   is	   mainly	   because	   the	  
techniques	  required	  for	  this	  analysis	  are	  so	  numerous,	  highly	  complex,	  poorly	  understood,	  and	  
some	  are	  still	  highly	  underdeveloped”	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  In	  this	  thesis	  is	  therefore	  presented	  
an	  investigation	  about	  the	  various	  sources	  of	  uncertainties	  (affecting	  both	  the	  model	  and	  the	  
measured	   data),	   in	   order	   to	   examine	   the	   reliability	   of	   the	   modelling	   results	   and	   the	  
performance	  of	  the	  model.	  The	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  was	  chosen	  as	  an	  uncertainty	  
based	   calibration	   method	   because	   it	   is	   simple	   and	   easy	   to	   implement	   and	   it	   “reduces	   the	  
subjectivity	  inherent	  in	  typical	  uncertainty	  approaches”	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  	  
	  
The	  results	  coming	  from	  the	  quality	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  were	  then	  compared	  with	  
the	   results	   of	   a	   traditional	   calibration	   method	   (fminsearch),	   that	   was	   already	   performed,	  
except	   for	   the	   long	   term	   event,	   on	   the	   same	   dataset,	   by	   Olesen	   in	   2015.	   Moreover,	   a	  
calculation	  of	  the	  ammonia	  load	  that	  is	  discharged	  into	  the	  CSO	  structure	  was	  made,	  based	  on	  
==PAGE:18==

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli

the	  results	  coming	  from	  the	  use	  of	  a	  traditional	  calibration	  method	  and	  an	  uncertainty	  based	  
one.	  
1.1. OBJECTIVES	  
The	   present	   work	   starts	   with	   a	   literature	   review	   for	   investigating	   the	   state	   of	   art	   of	   water	  
quality	  monitoring	  and	  modelling,	  and	  for	  detecting	  the	  various	  methods	  for	  the	  calibration	  of	  
the	   model	   parameters	   (traditional	   or	   uncertainty	   based).	   Those	   calibration	   methods	   will	   be	  
then	  applied	  on	  the	  model	  created	  with	  WEST,	  through	  the	  use	  of	  Matlab.	  The	  objectives	  of	  
the	  thesis	  are	  therefore:	  
1) Identify	  the	  parameters’	  values	  providing	  good	  simulations	  by	  performing	  a	  traditional	  
calibration	  (fminsearch	  method)	  on	  the	  hydraulic	  part	  of	  the	  model	  for	  11	  rain	  events	  
and	   for	   a	   long	   term	   event.	   The	   calibration	   is	   only	   made	   on	   the	   most	   sensitive	  
parameters	  of	  the	  model,	  detected	  by	  Olesen	  through	  a	  sensitivity	  analysis	  in	  2015.	  
2) Identify	  the	  most	  sensitive	  water	  quality	  parameters	  of	  the	  model	  and,	  through	  the	  
use	   of	   an	   uncertainty	   based	   calibration	   method	   (modified	   Monte	   Carlo),	   find	   the	  
parameters’	  values	  that	  better	  represent	  the	  trend	  of	  the	  measurements	  made	  with	  
the	  use	  of	  online	  sensors	  for	  the	  same	  rain	  events.	  
3) Evaluate	   the	   goodness	   of	   the	   calibration	   (or	   better,	   of	   the	   identified	   parameters’	  
values)	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  Root	  Mean	  Square	  Error	  (RMSE)	  method	  for	  both	  the	  
calibration	  approaches	  and	  demonstrate	  that	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  method	  provides	  
better	  results	  than	  the	  traditional	  one.	  
4) Prove	  that	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  can	  be	  used	  on	  an	  entire	  time	  series	  of	  
ammonia	   concentration	   (long	   term	   calibration),	   despite	   the	   fact	   that	   the	   series	  
includes	   sensor	   calibration	   events	   inside.	   These	   sensor	   calibration	   events	   generally	  
compromise	  the	  possibility	  of	  using	  the	  data	  without	  cutting	  the	  series	  into	  events	  in	  
which	  the	  sensor	  did	  not	  detect	  any	  calibrations.	  The	  aim	  is	  to	  demonstrate	  that	  the	  
output	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  model	  is	  able	  to	  reproduce	  the	  same	  trend	  of	  the	  
measured	   data.	   Prove	   also	   that	   this	   is	   not	   possible	   with	   the	   use	   of	   the	   traditional	  
calibration	  method.	  
5) Demonstrate	   that	   the	   uncertainty	   based	   method	   gives	   more	   reliable	   results	   also	   in	  
terms	  of	  load	  calculation,	  since	  the	  load	  coming	  from	  the	  measured	  data	  is	  closer	  to	  
the	  one	  calculated	  with	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  than	  to	  the	  one	  calculated	  
with	  the	  fminsearch	  method	  (and	  generally	  it	  is	  within	  the	  load	  uncertainty	  bounds).	  
1.2. OUTLINE	  
This	  work	  will	  follow	  the	  structure	  presented	  below	  (Figure	  1):

Figure	  1:	  thesis	  outline 
• Here	   the	   general	   framework	   of	   this	   project	   is	   presented,	   together	   with	   the	   main	  
objecrves	  that	  should	  be	  met.	  
Introducron	  -­‐	  Chapter	  1	  
• In	  this	  chapter	  the	  theorercal	  background	  is	  provided.	  The	  importance	  of	  monitoring	  
and	  the	  way	  to	  do	  it	  are	  explained.	  Moreover	  the	  data	  quality	  evaluaron	  is	  presented,	  
and	  parrcolar	  asenron	  is	  put	  on	  the	  uncertainty	  analysis.	  At	  the	  end	  of	  the	  chapter	  
focus	  is	  put	  on	  the	  WEST	  model.	  
Literature	  review	  -­‐	  Chapter	  2	  
• Here	  a	  site	  descripron	  of	  the	  area	  of	  Viby	  and	  its	  WWTP	  is	  given	  and	  the	  AMOK	  project	  
is	  presented.	  Moreover	  the	  model	  setup	  is	  shown.	  
Case	  study	  -­‐	  Chapter	  3	  
• This	  chapter	  presents	  two	  diﬀerent	  calibraron	  methods,	  both	  used	  within	  this	  thesis.	  
The	  ﬁrst	  one	  	  is	  a	  tradironal	  method	  (fminsearch)	  and	  the	  second	  one	  is	  an	  uncertainty	  
based	  one	  (modiﬁed	  Monte	  Carlo).	  The	  diﬀerences	  between	  the	  two	  approaches	  are	  
illustrated,	   together	   with	   the	   advantages	   of	   taking	   into	   account	   the	   uncertainres	  
present	  in	  a	  model	  for	  a	  beser	  results'	  reliability.	  
Calibraron	  -­‐	  Chapter	  4	  
• The	  load	  calcularon	  methods	  for	  the	  measured	  data,	  for	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  
fminsearch	   calibraron	   and	   for	   the	   data	   coming	   from	   the	   modiﬁed	   Monte	   Carlo	  
calibraron	  are	  explained.	  
Load	  calcularon	  -­‐	  Chapter	  5	  
• The	  results	  of	  the	  diﬀerent	  calibraron	  methods	  are	  here	  presented	  and	  debated,	  for	  
11	  rain	  events	  and	  for	  a	  long	  term	  event.	  
Results	  and	  discussion	  -­‐	  Chapter	  6	  	  
• A	   conclusion	   of	   this	   project	   is	   provided,	   and	   suggesron	   for	   future	   outlooks	   are	  
provided.	  
Conclusion	  and	  further	  outlooks	  -­‐	  Chapter	  7	  and	  8

5  
2. LITERATURE	  REVIEW	  
In	   this	   section	   the	   theoretical	   background	   of	   this	   work	   is	   presented.	   Firstly,	   an	   overall	  
explanation	   about	   the	   importance	   of	   monitoring	   is	   given;	   secondly,	   two	   different	   ways	   of	  
monitoring	  are	  presented	  (grab	  sampling	   and	  on-­‐line	  sampling).	  After	  that,	  the	  data	  quality	  
evaluation	  is	  investigated,	  with	  particular	  attention	  on	  the	  uncertainty	  assessment.	  Lastly,	  an	  
overall	  explanation	  of	  the	  model	  created	  with	  WEST	  is	  given.	  
2.1. WHY	  MONITORING	  WATER	  QUALITY	  IN	  SEWER	  IS	  
IMPORTANT	  
2.1.1. What	  does	  monitoring	  mean	  and	  its	  main	  aims	  
As	   already	   mentioned	   in	   the	   introduction,	   monitoring	   means	   keeping	   under	   observation	  
something	  and	  recording	  it	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  It	  is	  particular	  important	  within	  this	  work	  because	  
it	   turns	   out	   to	   be	   essential	   for	   the	   development	   of	   the	   model	   calibration.	   Other	   aims	   of	  
monitoring	   are:	   design	   of	   future	   systems,	   optimization	   of	   the	   existing	   ones	   and	  
implementation	   of	   control	   strategies.	   Moreover,	   it	   is	   required	   by	   the	   EU	   Water	   Framework	  
Directive	  (2000/60/EC	  Directive).	  
2.1.2. Regulation	  requirements	  
Through	  the	  Urban	  Waste	  Water	  Treatment	  Directive	  (Council	  Directive	  91/271/ECC	  of	  21	  May	  
1991)	  the	  “emission	  standard”	  notion	  was	  replaced	  with	  the	  “stream	  standard”	  notion.	  The	  
first	  concept	  fixes	  discharge	  limits	  according	  to	  the	  polluting	  emission	  characteristics	  of	  each	  
substance,	   while	   the	   second	   one	   fixes	   discharge	   limits	   according	   to	   the	   self-­‐depurative	  
capacity	  of	  the	  receiving	  water	  body	  (Chave,	  2001).	  Hence,	  in	  the	  first	  case	  the	  effects	  of	  the	  
pollutants	   on	   the	   RWB	   are	   not	   taken	   into	   account,	   “with	   the	   result	   that	   in	   some	   cases	   the	  
standards	  may	  be	  unnecessarily	  stringent	  and	  in	  others	  too	  lax”	  (Chave,	  2001),	  whilst	  with	  the	  
second	  approach	  it	  is	  important	  to	  declare	  the	  uses	  to	  which	  the	  final	  receiving	  water	  bodies	  
are	   subjected	   to,	   so	   that	   the	   required	   degree	   of	   purity	   may	   be	   obtained.	   These	   two	  
approaches	  are	  better	  known	  in	  the	  European	  context	  with	  the	  names	  “Emission	  Limit	  Values”	  
(ELV)	  approach	  and	  “Water	  Quality	  Objective”	  (WQO)	  approach.	  
 
More	   recently,	   the	   EU	   Water	   Framework	   Directive	   (2000/60/EC	   Directive),	   as	   already	  
mentioned	   in	   this	   work	   before,	   recommends	   the	   achievement	   of	   environmental	   objectives	  
within	   specific	   timeframes	   (Elahi,	   2014).	   The	   WFD	   Art.	   4	   defines	   these	   objectives	   as	   “good	  
ecological	  and	  chemical	  status”	  for	  all	  water	  bodies	  in	  Europe	  by	  the	  end	  of	  2015.	  This	  means	  
that	  the	  biological	  quality	  of	  the	  receiving	  water	  bodies,	  affected	  by	  anthropogenic	  activities,	  
should	   only	   experience	   a	   slight	   departure	   from	   the	   undisturbed	   condition,	   while,	   for	   what	  
concern	  the	  concentration	  of	  chemicals	  in	  water,	  a	  good	  chemical	  status	  is	  achieved	  when	  all	  
the	   standards	   set	   by	   EU	   legislation	   are	   fulfilled	   (Vanrolleghem	   et	   al.,	   2005).	   For	   the	  
environmental	  objectives	  to	  be	  achieved,	  it	  is	  necessary	  to	  impose	  some	  environmental	  quality	  
standards	  (EQS),	  that	  in	  the	  Directive	  (Art.	  2	  and	  Annex	  V)	  are	  defined	  in	  the	  following	  way:	  
“Environmental	  quality	  standard	  means	  the	  concentration	  of	  a	  particular	  pollutant	  or	  group	  of	  
pollutants	  in	  water,	  sediment	  or	  biota,	  of	  the	  watercourse,	  which	  should	  not	  be	  exceeded	  in	  
order	  to	  protect	  human	  health	  and	  the	  environment”.	  The	  WDF	  Art.	  10	  clarifies	  the	  discharge	  
==PAGE:21==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
6  
regulation	  from	  point-­‐	  and	  diffuse	  sources	  by	  means	  of	  a	  combined	  approach,	  that	  controls	  
the	  emission	  of	  pollutants	  in	  the	  water	  environment	  through	  either	  fixed	  emission	  limit	  values	  
or,	   in	   case	   of	   diffuse	   pollution,	   through	   more	   stringent	   discharge	   limits,	   set	   down	   by	   the	  
competent	  authority	  dealing	  with	  the	  authorisation,	  so	  that	  the	  quality	  objectives	  of	  the	  RWB	  
are	  not	  exceeded	  (Elahi,	  2014).	  
	  
The	  Directive	  also	  specifies	  that	  monitoring	  actions	  are	  necessary	  and	  essential:	  for	  verifying	  
the	  water	  status,	  for	  accessing	  the	  success	  of	  remediation	  actions	  and	  in	  case	  of	  accidental	  
pollution	  (Chave,	  2001).	  
 
All	  the	  European	  Directives	  are	  translated	  into	  Danish	  law	  mainly	  through	  the	  Environmental	  
Protection	   Act	   (EPA)	   (Consolidated	   Act	   no.	   879,	   26	   June	   2010),	   which	   lays	   down	   the	  
fundamental	  environmental	  objectives	  and	  the	  means	  through	  which	  they	  should	  be	  met.	  It	  is	  
a	   framework	   act	   and	   therefore	   it	   needs	   to	   be	   integrated	   with	   guidelines	   (Djurhuus	   et	   al.,	  
2013).	  The	  EPA’s	  principal	  aim	  is	  to	  prevent	  air,	  water,	  soil	  and	  subsoil	  pollution.	  Wastewater	  
can	  be	  discharged	  into	  a	  natural	  water	  body	  only	  after	  having	  obtained	  a	  permit	  authorization	  
from	  the	  municipality	  (in	  fact	  the	  EPA	  uses	  the	  principle	  of	  decentralisation	  for	  the	  handling	  of	  
the	  discharge	  permission).	  Another	  piece	  of	  the	  wastewater	  discharge	  regulation	  is	  the	  Danish	  
Statutory	  Order	  1022/2010,	  which	  implements	  the	  WFD’s	  environmental	  objectives	  and	  the	  
resulting	  environmental	  quality	  standards,	  in	  order	  for	  the	  RWB	  to	  achieve	  a	  good	  status.	  For	  
the	  authorities	  to	  be	  able	  to	  release	  discharge	  permits,	  data	  on	  receiving	  waters,	  such	  as	  the	  
chemical,	   biological	   and	   hydrological	   characteristics,	   but	   also	   on	   the	   catchment	   area	   from	  
which	  the	  rain	  drains,	  must	  be	  given	  (Elahi,	  2014).	  These	  data	  can	  only	  results	  from	  monitoring	  
campaigns,	  which,	  therefore,	  assume	  an	  essential	  role	  in	  the	  wastewater	  discharge	  permission	  
release.	  
	  
To	   briefly	   sum	   up	   the	   regulation	   hierarchy,	   it	   can	   be	   affirmed	   that	   fixed	   environmental	  
objectives,	  in	  order	  to	  be	  achieved,	  have	  to	  be	  broken	  down	  into	  EQSs	  that	  lead	  to	  the	  laying	  
down	  of	  emission	  limit	  values	  for	  any	  discharge	  into	  a	  water	  body.	  This	  concept	  is	  summarized	  
in	  Figure	  2.

from	  Elahi,	  2014).

For	   all	   these	   reasons,	   the	   European	   Directives	   have	   been	   a	   strong	   starting	   point	   for	   the	  
implementation	   of	   water	   quality	   monitoring	   programmes	   including	   physical,	   chemical	   and	  
biological	  variables.	  
	  
From	  these	  Directives,	  and	  from	  their	  implementation	  into	  national	  laws	  in	  Denmark,	  the	  need	  
for	  an	  integrated	  view	  and	  the	  necessity	  for	  a	  holistic	  approach	  regarding	  the	  water	  quality	  
management	   were	   made	   clear.	   This	   integrated	   approach,	   therefore,	   must	   consider	   the	  
drainage	   system	   as	   composed	   by	   three	   elements	   (the	   sewer	   system,	   the	   WWTP,	   and	   the	  
receiving	  water	  body).	  Hence,	  the	  necessity	  of	  tools	  able	  to	  simulate	  the	  whole	  system	  under	  
consideration	   has	   increased.	   These	   tools	   are	   known	   as	   “integrated	   urban	   drainage	   models”	  
(Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  
	  
However,	  there	  are	  no	  precise	  guidelines	  for	  Denmark	  regarding	  the	  discharge	  of	  pollutants’	  
loads	  from	  CSO	  structures	  (Arnbjerg-­‐Nielsen	  and	  Johansen,	  2002	  −	  cited	  in	  Olesen,	  2015).	  The	  
lack	   of	   a	   uniform	   regulation	   is	   due	   to	   the	   fact	   that	   little	   has	   known	   about	   the	   effects	   of	  
untreated	  wastewater	  coming	  from	  overflow	  structures.	  Because	  of	  this,	  more	  attention	  must	  
be	  paid	  on	  the	  development	  of	  monitoring	  techniques	  to	  estimate	  the	  polluting	  loads	  from	  
overflow	  structures.	  
2.2. WHAT	  IS	  WATER	  QUALITY	  AND	  HOW	  TO	  SAMPLE	  IT	  
2.2.1. Water	  quality	  parameters	  
Water	  quality	  is	  defined	  as	  the	  physical,	  chemical	  and	  biological	  characteristics	  of	  water.	  The	  
parameters	  for	  assessing	  it	  are	  defined	  according	  to	  the	  final	  use	  to	  which	  water	  is	  subjected.	  
Environmental	  water	  quality	  parameters	  are	  all	  those	  parameters	  that	  can	  be	  used	  to	  describe	  
the	  overall	  status	  of	  rivers,	  lakes	  and	  oceans.	  These	  parameters	  are	  generally	  the	  same	  used	  
for	   the	   description	   of	   water	   quality	   in	   sewer	   systems1.	   More	   focus	   should	   be	   put	   in	   the	  
assessment	  of	  the	  behaviour	  of	  pollutants	  in	  sewer	  systems	  during	  wet	  weather,	  because	  is	  
during	  rain	  events	  that	  the	  discharge	  from	  overflow	  structures	  is	  detected.	  
 
The	   physico-­‐chemical	   parameters	   collected	   for	   the	   AMOK	   project	   are	   conductivity,	   pH	   and	  
temperature.	  Conductivity	  is	  defined	  as	  the	  ability	  of	  water	  of	  transmitting	  or	  conducting	  an	  
electrical	   current,	   which	   is	   induced	   by	   the	   presence	   of	   inorganic	   ions	   in	   water.	   pH	   is	   the	  
measure	   of	   the	   acidity	   or	   alkalinity	   of	   an	   aqueous	   solution.	   Temperature	   can	   influence	   the	  
metabolic	  rates	  and	  the	  biological	  activity	  of	  aquatic	  organisms,	  because	  some	  enzymes	  are	  
more	  active	  at	  higher	  temperatures.	  The	  wastewater	  pollution	  indicators	  investigated	  in	  the	  
project	   are	   ammonium,	   nitrate,	   total	   phosphorus,	   COD	   (chemical	   oxygen	   demand)	   and	  
turbidity.	  Nitrogen	  (in	  the	  forms	  of	  ammonium	  and	  nitrate)	  and	  phosphorus	  are	  responsible	  
for	  eutrophication,	  i.e.	  the	  excessive	  presence	  of	  nutrients	  in	  water	  that	  leads	  to	  algal	  bloom	  
and	  death	  of	  aquatic	  organisms.	  COD	  measures	  the	  potential	  consumption	  of	  oxygen	  in	  water	  
and	  it	  is	  an	  indicator	  of	  the	  organic	  matter	  content	  in	  it.	  Turbidity	  is	  the	  measure	  of	  the	  clarity	  
of	   water	   and	   it	   depends	   on	   the	   amount	   of	   suspended	   solids	   in	   water	   that	   reduce	   the	   light	  
transmission	  in	  it,	  with	  the	  consequent	  reduction	  of	  photosynthesis	  and	  loss	  of	  the	  aquatic	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
1	  It	  is	  important,	  for	  this	  work,	  to	  underline	  that	  the	  characteristics	  of	  the	  water	  discharged	  from	  CSO	  structures	  are	  
the	  same	  characteristics	  used	  for	  describing	  the	  quality	  of	  water	  in	  sewer	  networks.	  
==PAGE:23==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
8  
biodiversity	   (Mitchell	   and	   Stapp,	   1990).	   Turbidity	   is	   particularly	   important	   for	   wastewater	  
monitoring	  because	  it	  is	  often	  used	  as	  an	  indicator	  for	  TSS	  and	  COD	  concentration.	  In	  this	  work	  
focus	  will	  be	  put	  on	  ammonium.	  
Ammonia	  
Total	   nitrogen	   exists	   in	   nature	   in	   four	   main	   forms:	   organic	   nitrogen,	   ammonia,	   nitrite	   and	  
nitrate.	  Nitrogen	  is	  present	  in	  water	  in	  the	  forms	  of	  ammonia,	  nitrite	  and	  nitrate,	  which	  are	  
inorganic	  forms	  of	  nitrogen.	  It	  is	  important	  to	  reduce	  the	  concentration	  of	  dissolved	  nitrogen	  
in	  water	  bodies	  because	  of	  the	  multiple	  problems	  produced	  by	  it	  (see	  the	  previous	  paragraph	  
2.2.1).	  
	  
Ammonia	  exists	  in	  two	  forms:	  
• 
Ammonium	  ion	  NH!
!;	  
• 
Ammonia	  gas	  NH!.	  
Their	  ratio	  depends	  on	  pH	  and	  temperature.	  In	  areas	  with	  hard	  water,	  like	  Denmark,	  since	  the	  
pH	  of	  sewer	  system	  is	  between	  6.5	  and	  7.5	  (Butler	  and	  Davies,	  2011),	  the	  majority	  of	  ammonia	  
is	   present	   as	   ammonium	   ion.	   When	   the	   pH	   increases	   to	   values	   greater	   than	   8,	   then	   the	  
majority	   of	   ammonia	   will	   be	   present	   in	   the	   form	   of	   ammonia	   gas.	   Apart	   from	   this,	   no	  
distinctions	  will	  be	  made	  in	  this	  work	  between	  them	  and	  the	  term	  ammonia	  will	  be	  used.	  
 
Organic	  nitrogen	  in	  wastewater	  derives	  primarily	  from	  food	  residues	  and	  toilet	  discharges.	  Half	  
of	  the	  toilet	  products	  are	  composed	  by	  urine,	  which	  contains	  urea	  (CO(NH2)2),	  which	  is	  rapidly	  
converted	  to	  ammonia	  (Patterson,	  2003).	  The	  concentration	  of	  ammonia	  in	  strong,	  medium	  
and	  diluted	  wastewater	  is	  calculated	  to	  be	  respectively	  75,	  45	  and	  20	  mg/l	  (Henze	  et	  al.,	  2001).	  
In	   this	   study	   the	   adopted	   range	   for	   ammonia	   in	   wastewater	   was	   fixed	   between	   20	   and	   80	  
mg/l.	  The	  concentration	  of	  nitrogen	  (N)	  in	  rainwater	  is	  considered	  to	  be	  negligible	  (Göbel	  et	  
al.,	  2007).	  However,	  the	  concentration	  of	  ammonia	  in	  the	  runoff	  cannot	  be	  neglected:	  in	  fact	  
storm	  water	  runoff	  contains	  pollutants	  whose	  concentration	  depends	  on	  the	  characteristics	  of	  
the	   surface.	   The	   bandwidth	   for	   different	   EMCs	   (event	   mean	   concentrations)	   for	   different	  
surfaces	   can	   be	   found	   in	   (Göbel	   et	   al.,	   2007),	   and	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   in	   runoff	   is	  
estimated	  to	  be	  between	  0.1	  and	  6.2	  mg/l.	  The	  range	  used	  for	  this	  study	  has	  been	  set	  between	  
0.20	  and	  10	  mg/l.	  
2.2.2. Description	  of	  traditional	  and	  on-­‐line	  sampling	  methods	  and	  related	  
problems	  
Traditionally,	  two	  kinds	  of	  wastewater	  sampling	  are	  possible:	  grab	  and	  composite	  sampling.	  
With	   the	   first	   method	   all	   the	   sample	   material	   is	   collected	   at	   one	   time,	   so	   that	   the	  
measurement	  is	  time	  and	  site	  specific,	  while	  the	  second	  method	  is	  a	  mixture	  of	  grab	  samples	  
collected	  in	  the	  same	  place	  but	  at	  regular	  intervals	  over	  a	  period	  of	  time,	  generally	  24	  hours,	  
so	   that	   the	   sample	   characteristics	   can	   be	   seen	   as	   the	   average	   wastewater	   characteristics	  
during	   the	   period	   of	   collection.	   Both	   the	   methods	   can	   be	   performed	   either	   manually	   or	  
automatically.	  Standard	  methods	  for	  sampling	  were	  extensively	  described	  by	  APHA	  (American	  
Public	   Health	   Association)	   in	   1998	   and,	   more	   recently,	   some	   guidelines	   (“Regulatory	  
monitoring	   and	   testing	   –	   water	   and	   wastewater	   sampling”)	   were	   published	   by	   EPA	  
(Environmental	   Protection	   Authority),	   in	   2007.	   However,	   there	   are	   many	   limitations	  
==PAGE:24==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
9  
concerning	  these	  methods	  such	  as	  sampling	  errors	  or	  errors	  regarding	  sampling	  conservation,	  
transport	  and	  preparation	  (Gruber	  et	  al.,	  2005).	  Moreover,	  water	  quality	  sampling	  can	  only	  
give	  limited	  information	  for	  a	  fixed	  period	  of	  time.	  In	  order	  to	  have	  continuous	  information	  
over	   a	   long	   period,	   on-­‐line	   sensors	   have	   to	   be	   chosen.	   This	   is	   a	   recent	   technology	   able	   to	  
record	   water	   quality	   data	   through	   high	   time	   resolution	   measurements	   (up	   to	   one	  
measurement	   per	   second)	   for	   long	   periods	   (months	   or	   even	   years)	   in	   sewer	   networks	   and	  
surface	  water	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  For	  these	  reasons,	  sensors	  give	  information	  that	  has	  a	  higher	  
level	   of	   precision	   and	   accuracy	   (it	   is	   not	   the	   average	   over	   a	   period	   of	   time).	   However,	   the	  
quality	  of	  the	  data	  should	  be	  known	  before	  their	  use:	  indeed,	  this	  technology	  still	  presents	  
many	  practical	  problems	  that	  have	  to	  be	  detected	  with	  additional	  studies.	  One	  of	  the	  most	  
important	   problem	   to	   face	   with	   is	   the	   sensors	   calibration,	   which	   requires	   to	   do	   measuring	  
campaigns	  for	  investigating	  whether	  the	  sensors	  are	  calibrated	  or	  not,	  which	  is	  generally	  time	  
spending	  and	  requires	  many	  efforts.	  
	  
The	  main	  protocol	  existing	  for	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  is	  the	  ISO	  15839:2003	  protocol:	  Water	  quality	  –	  
On-­‐line	  sensors/analysing	  equipment	  for	  water	  –	  Specifications	  and	  performances	  tests.	  This	  
protocol	   provides	   the	   guidelines	   for	   monitoring	   under	   laboratory	   (or	   standard)	   conditions:	  
however,	  it	  is	  very	  rare	  to	  operate	  under	  such	  conditions,	  since	  the	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  are	  not	  
generally	  used	  in	  lab	  but	  under	  field	  conditions.	  For	  overcoming	  this	  problem,	  a	  section	  of	  the	  
ISO	  15839:2003	  protocol	  focuses	  on	  the	  sensors’	  performance	  during	  field	  operation.	  Anyway	  
this	   section	   is	   not	   complete,	   and	   does	   not	   aim	   to	   obtain	   reproducible	   results	   since	   the	  
performance	   characteristics	   are	   time	   and	   site	   specific.	   That	   means	   that	   a	   standardized	  
protocol	  for	  field	  conditions	  is	  needed.	  The	  most	  common	  effects	  of	  the	  disturbances	  (under	  
field	  conditions)	  to	  which	  sensors	  are	  exposed	  are	  air	  bubbles	  and	  fouling	  of	  the	  optics,	  caused	  
by	  turbidity	  (Vanrolleghem	  and	  Lee,	  2003).	  According	  to	  the	  experiments	  made	  by	  Baupré	  in	  
2010,	  in	  which	  the	  behaviour	  of	  sensors	  was	  evaluated	  under	  field	  conditions	  reproduced	  in	  
laboratory,	  the	  effects	  of	  air	  bubbles	  and	  turbidity	  are	  noise	  amplification	  and	  presence	  of	  a	  
systematic	  bias.	  He	  also	  demonstrated	  in	  his	  work	  that	  there	  is	  a	  gap	  between	  lab	  and	  field	  
conditions	  so	  that	  a	  standardized	  protocol	  is	  needed.	  	  
More	  recently,	  in	  2005,	  another	  book	  on	  this	  topic	  was	  published	  by	  IWA	  (International	  Water	  
Association),	  whose	  title	  is:	  Instrumentation,	  control	  and	  automation	  in	  wastewater	  systems.	  
In	   this	   book	   a	   chapter	   is	   particularly	   interesting	   for	   this	   work:	   Online	   sensors/analysers	   at	  
wastewater	  treatment	  plant,	  in	  which	  a	  discussion	  about	  the	  best	  measuring	  methods	  for	  the	  
most	  common	  compounds	  is	  found	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  
	  
More	  information	  about	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  can	  be	  found	  in	  (Vanrolleghem	  and	  Lee,	  2003)	  and	  in	  
(Beaupré,	   2010).	   Hereafter,	   a	   small	   description	   about	   the	   most	   common	   monitoring	  
techniques	  for	  different	  parameters	  is	  presented:	  
• 
Temperature:	  generally	  measured	  through	  a	  thermistor;	  
• 
Conductivity:	  measured	  through	  the	  application	  of	  a	  voltage	  over	  the	  electrodes;	  
• 
pH:	  through	  glass	  electrodes;	  
• 
Ammonium	  concentration:	  the	  most	  used	  methods	  are	  gas	  sensitive	  and	  ion	  sensitive	  
electrode	  methods.	  The	  first	  one	  consists	  in	  the	  increasing	  of	  the	  pH	  to	  11,	  so	  that	  all	  
the	   ammonium	   ions	   (NH4
+)	   are	   transformed	   in	   ammonia	   gas	   (NH3).	   Inside	   the	  
==PAGE:25==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
10  
electrode	  there	  is	  a	  membrane	  able	  to	  let	  pass	  the	  desired	  component	  (ammonia).	  The	  
ion	   sensitive	   electrode	   works	   with	   the	   same	   working	   principle	   of	   the	   gas	   sensitive	  
electrode,	  but	  it	  can	  be	  used	  as	  an	  in-­‐situ	  sensor,	  while	  the	  first	  one	  necessarily	  needs	  
a	  pumping	  system	  for	  pumping	  the	  water	  ex-­‐situ	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  
• 
COD	   and	   TSS:	   they	   cannot	   be	   measured	   directly,	   so	   that	   generally	   a	   measure	   of	  
turbidity	   is	   used	   instead.	   Turbidity	   is	   often	   evaluated	   through	   optical	   techniques	  
among	  which	  light	  absorbance	  and	  UV	  absorbance	  are	  the	  most	  famous	  ones.	  The	  first	  
one	  uses	  the	  so	  called	  “scattered	  light	  principle”	  that	  measures	  the	  amount	  of	  light	  
that	   is	   reflected	   by	   the	   suspended	   solids,	   whilst	   the	   second	   one	   measures	   the	  
absorption	  of	  a	  light	  source	  in	  the	  UV	  spectral	  range	  (from	  190	  to	  172	  nm),	  and	  relates	  
the	   absorption	   in	   different	   ranges	   to	   different	   types	   of	   particles	   (nitrates,	   COD	   and	  
TSS)	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  Optical	  methods	  have	  the	  advantage	  of	  being	  inexpensive	  and	  of	  
not	  requiring	  reagents	  or	  sample	  preparation	  (Vanrolleghem	  and	  Lee,	  2003).	  
For	  the	  description	  of	  the	  sensors	  installed	  in	  Viby	  see	  Chapter	  3.2.	  
	  
Monitoring	  of	  water	  quality	  is	  always	  troublesome	  due	  to	  the	  critical	  conditions	  found	  in	  sewer	  
systems	  and	  overflow	  structures.	  Fouling	  and	  clogging,	  due	  to	  the	  accumulation	  of	  particles	  on	  
the	  surface	  of	  the	  sensors,	  are	  the	  most	  common	  problems	  encountered	  that	  lead	  to	  noise,	  
errors	  and	  sensor	  failure.	  To	  avoid	  this,	  cleaning	  and	  maintenance	  should	  be	  performed	  on	  a	  
regular	  basis,	  but	  this	  is	  not	  done	  in	  practice	  because	  it	  requires	  significant	  time,	  efforts	  and	  
money.	  In	  Viby,	  the	  cleaning	  (that	  consists	  in	  the	  lifting	  of	  the	  sensors	  and	  in	  the	  removal	  of	  
the	  particles	  from	  the	  probes)	  should	  have	  been	  performed	  once	  a	  week,	  but	  this	  practice	  was	  
performed	  much	  less	  often,	  as	  it	  is	  explained	  later	  in	  Chapter	  2.3.1	  (Table	  3	  and	  Table	  4).	  
2.3. DATA	  QUALITY	  EVALUATION	  
Once	  the	  problems	  regarding	  data	  collection	  are	  overcome,	  the	  second	  step	  is	  to	  assess	  the	  
quality	   of	   the	   collected	   data.	   The	   aim	   of	   a	   measurement	   is	   to	   find	   the	   “true	   value”	   of	   a	  
measured	   quantity,	   the	   measurand.	   The	   data	   quality	   is	   always	   affected	   by	   the	   presence	   of	  
random	   errors	   and	   systematic	   errors.	   The	   first	   kind	   of	   errors	   is	   always	   present	   in	   a	  
measurement	  and	  derives	  from	  “unpredictable	  or	  stochastic	  temporal	  and	  spatial	  variation	  of	  
factors	  that	  influence	  the	  results	  of	  the	  measurement”	  (Muste	  et	  al.,	  2012),	  even	  when	  the	  
measurements	   are	   made	   under	   the	   exact	   same	   conditions.	   By	   increasing	   their	   number	   it	   is	  
possible	  to	  reduce	  the	  presence	  of	  random	  errors.	  On	  the	  other	  hand,	  systematic	  errors	  are	  
caused	  by	  an	  imperfect	  calibration	  of	  the	  measurement	  instrument	  or	  by	  an	  interference	  given	  
by	   environment	   (pressure,	   temperature	   etc.).	   If	   the	   source	   of	   a	   systematic	   error	   can	   be	  
identified,	  then	  it	  can	  be	  eliminated	  and	  the	  values	  can	  be	  corrected	  (calibration).	  	  
	  
For	   assessing	   the	   measurement	   quality,	   the	   sensor	   data	   should	   be	   compared	   with	   some	  
accepted	   reference	   values,	   which	   in	   the	   case	   of	   this	   project	   are	   the	   data	   coming	   from	   the	  
measuring	  campaigns.	  Seven	  short	  measuring	  campaigns	  were	  performed	  between	  September	  
2013	  and	  September	  2014.	  The	  grab	  samples	  were	  analysed	  and	  their	  concentration	  values	  
compared	  with	  the	  concentration	  values	  of	  the	  sensor	  data.	  In	  Table	  1	  below	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  
see	  the	  date	  and	  the	  number	  of	  samples	  analysed	  for	  each	  measuring	  campaign.	  
==PAGE:26==

An	  overview	  of	  all	  the	  measuring	  campaigns,	  containing	  day	  number	  from	  day	  1	  (01/01/2013),	  
date,	  start	  and	  end	  time,	  ammonia	  and	  TSS	  concentration,	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  A.	  
	  
In	  order	  to	  describe	  the	  accordance	  between	  two	  datasets	  the	  terms	  accuracy	  and	  precision	  
have	  to	  be	  introduced.	  Accuracy	  is	  defined	  as	  the	  closeness	  between	  a	  measurement	  value	  
and	   the	   accepted	   reference	   value,	   while	   precision	   is	   the	   degree	   of	   agreement	   between	  
different	  measurements	  under	  unchanged	  conditions	  (Gutierrez-­‐Osuna,	  2001).	  The	  precision	  is	  
affected	  by	  random	  errors,	  while	  the	  accuracy	  is	  affected	  by	  systematic	  errors.	  A	  schematic	  
explanation	  of	  this	  concept	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  3.	  
	  
Figure	  3: schematic	  representation	  of	  the	  concepts	  of	  accuracy	  and	  precision.	  Accurate	  measurement	  is	  when	  the	  
black	  dots	  fall	  in	  the	  bull’s-­‐eye	  (that	  represents	  the	  accepted	  reference	  value).	  Precise	  measurement	  is	  when	  the	  
black	  dots	  are	  close	  one	  to	  each	  other	  (from	  Gutierrez-­‐Osuna,	  2001). 
In	   Table	   2	   below	   the	   accordance	   between	   the	   sensor	   data	   and	   the	   data	   coming	   from	   the	  
measuring	  campaigns	  in	  Viby	  (for	  ammonia	  and	  TSS)	  is	  presented:

the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  last	  five	  campaigns	  have	  been	  used.

a	   visual	   assessment	   of	   the	   accordance	   between	   sensors’	   data	   and	   data	   coming	   from	   grab	  
samples	  can	  be	  easily	  performed.	  
2.3.1. On-­‐line	  sensor	  calibration	  
A	  sensor	  is	  defined	  as	  a	  good	  sensor	  if	  it	  is	  sensitive	  to	  the	  measured	  parameter	  and	  it	  is	  not	  
sensitive	  to	  all	  the	  other	  parameters.	  In	  practice,	  when	  using	  a	  sensor	  different	  errors	  can	  be	  
produced:	  
• 
Sensitivity	  error	  –	  when	  the	  observed	  output	  of	  the	  sensor	  differs	  from	  its	  theoretical	  
output	  (variation	  of	  the	  slope	  of	  the	  characteristic	  curve).	  
• 
Offset	  or	  bias	  error	  –	  when	  the	  observed	  output	  has	  the	  same	  trend	  of	  the	  theoretical	  
one,	   but	   it	   is	   not	   zero	   when	   the	   measured	   physical	   property	   is	   zero	   (shift	   of	   the	  
characteristic	  curve).	  
A	  schematic	  representation	  of	  these	  errors	  is	  presented	  in	  Figure	  4.	  
Figure	  4:	  graphical	  representation	  of	  sensitivity	  and	  offset	  errors.	  The	  blue	  line	  reproduces	  the	  theoretical	  output	  of	  
the	  model,	  while	  the	  red	  one	  represents	  the	  measured	  response	  of	  the	  sensor	  (adapted	  from	  Bertrand-­‐Krajewski	  et	  
al.,	  2000	  –	  cited	  in	  Jézéquel,	  2014).	  
==PAGE:28==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
13  
These	  errors	  (sensitivity	  and	  offset)	  can	  be	  estimated	  and	  corrected	  through	  the	  calibration	  
and	   the	   verification	   of	   the	   sensors	   (Muste,	   2012).	   The	   calibration	   of	   an	   instrument	   is	  
performed	  for	  different	  purposes:	  
• 
To	  maintain	  instrument	  accuracy;	  	  
• 
To	  ensure	  that	  the	  instrument	  readings	  are	  consistent	  with	  other	  measurements;	  
• 
To	  evaluate	  the	  reliability	  of	  the	  sensor.	  
Calibration	   consists	   in	   the	   comparison	   between	   a	   set	   of	   values	   of	   the	   measurand	   (i.e.	   the	  
supposed	   true	   value	   of	   the	   parameter	   under	   observation,	   with	   a	   determined	   level	   of	  
uncertainty)	  and	  the	  data	  measured	  by	  the	  sensor.	  In	  this	  work	  the	  values	  of	  the	  measurand	  
are	   the	   concentration	   values	   coming	   from	   the	   measuring	   campaigns.	   Once	   the	   relationship	  
between	  the	  two	  sets	  of	  data	  is	  found,	  it	  is	  possible	  either	  to	  correct	  the	  sensor’s	  parameters	  
in	  order	  to	  reduce	  sensitivity	  and	  offset	  errors	  (on-­‐line	  correction),	  or	  to	  use	  the	  calibration	  
curve	  for	  treating	  the	  data	  after	  their	  collection	  (off-­‐line	  calibration)	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  In	  the	  
AMOK	  project	  the	  first	  option	  was	  chosen	  and	  the	  sensors	  were	  corrected	  according	  to	  the	  
percentage	   of	   deviation	   between	   the	   concentration	   measured	   by	   the	   sensors	   and	   the	   one	  
measured	  through	  the	  grab	  samples.	  The	  procedure	  used	  for	  on-­‐line	  correction	  of	  the	  sensors	  
was	  the	  following:	  if	  the	  reading	  of	  the	  sensor	  and	  the	  result	  from	  the	  grab	  sample	  analysed	  in	  
laboratory	  differ	  by	  more	  than	  a	  fixed	  threshold	  (e.g.	  10	  or	  20%)	  then	  the	  test	  is	  repeated.	  If	  
the	  difference	  is	  still	  higher	  than	  the	  threshold,	  a	  correction	  of	  the	  sensor	  is	  performed.	  This	  
kind	   of	   procedure,	   also	   called	   as	   “one	   or	   two	   points	   calibration”,	   is	   made	   through	   the	  
utilization	   of	   one	   or	   two	   points	   (the	   first	   one	   is	   generally	   the	   zero	   of	   the	   sensor),	   so	   that	  
sensitivity	   and	   offset	   errors	   can	   be	   investigated	   and	   corrected,	   through	   the	   shift	   of	   the	  
calibration	  curve	  towards	  the	  theoretical	  curve.	  It	  is	  important	  to	  notice	  that,	  in	  order	  to	  do	  
that,	  two	  assumptions	  should	  be	  made:	  
• 
The	  calibration	  curve	  is	  assumed	  to	  be	  a	  straight	  line;	  
• 
Sensitivity	  and	  offset	  errors	  are	  assumed	  to	  be	  the	  only	  investigated	  errors.	  
In	  addition	  to	  this,	  a	  distinction	  between	  single	  test	  and	  multiple	  test	  should	  be	  made:	  when	  all	  
the	  measurements	  of	  a	  test	  are	  only	  made	  once	  then	  the	  test	  is	  called	  single	  test;	  when	  the	  
measurements	   of	   the	   measurand	   are	   conducted	   several	   times,	   so	   that	   for	   each	   measurand	  
there	  is	  a	  set	  of	  readings	  of	  the	  sensor,	  the	  test	  is	  called	  multiple	  test.	  In	  the	  case	  of	  AMOK	  
project,	  single	  tests	  were	  conducted.	  Moreover,	  in	  the	  analysed	  case	  study,	  verification	  was	  
performed	   through	   the	   use	   of	   one	   single	   point,	   so	   that	   the	   correction	   of	   the	   sensor	   often	  
resulted	  to	  be	  partial	  and	  inconsistent.	  
In	  Table	  3	  and	  Table	  4	  the	  dates	  of	  the	  cleaning	  and	  of	  the	  calibration	  are	  presented	  for	  both	  
ammonia	   and	   TSS	   sensors,	   together	   with	   the	   concentration	   given	   by	   the	   sensor,	   the	   grab	  
sample	  concentration	  and	  the	  deviation	  between	  the	  two.

As	   it	   can	   be	   noticed	   from	   the	   two	   above	   tables,	   the	   cleaning	   and	   the	   maintenance	   of	   the	  
sensors	   have	   not	   been	   performed	   as	   often	   as	   expected.	   For	   example,	   the	   TSS	   sensor	   was	  
cleaned	  and	  calibrated	  three	  times	  during	  the	  spring	  (March-­‐May),	  but	  only	  one	  during	  the	  
autumn	  of	  the	  same	  year.	  In	  order	  to	  reduce	  the	  noise	  and	  the	  monitoring	  errors	  coming	  from	  
this,	  the	  calibration	  of	  sensors	  has	  been	  practiced.	  	  
In	  this	  work	  only	  the	  data	  related	  to	  ammonia	  measurements	  have	  been	  used.	  
2.3.2. Uncertainty	  evaluation	  
In	   general,	   however,	   every	   value	   coming	   from	   a	   measurement,	   and	   thus	   either	   the	  
concentration	   values	   coming	   from	   sensors	   or	   the	   results	   from	   measuring	   campaigns,	   is	  
affected	  by	  uncertainty,	  whose	  value	  should	  be	  defined,	  because	  “if	  the	  level	  of	  uncertainty	  of	  
the	  data	  is	  known,	  then	  it	  is	  easy	  to	  stat	  what	  the	  reliability	  of	  the	  model	  is”	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  
Indeed,	   measurement	   uncertainties	   need	   to	   be	   assessed	   in	   order	   to	   derive	   pertinent	  
conclusions	   from	   the	   results	   of	   measurements	   (Bertrand-­‐Krajevski	   and	   Bardin,	   2002).	   A	  
distinction	   between	   error	   and	   uncertainty	   should	   be	   made:	   the	   first	   one	   is	   the	   difference	  
between	  the	  result	  of	  a	  measurement	  and	  the	  measurand,	  while	  the	  second	  is	  the	  estimate	  of	  
the	   error,	   or,	   in	   other	   words,	   the	   numerical	   estimate	   of	   the	   spread	   of	   the	   values	   that	   can	  
reasonably	   be	   attributed	   to	   a	   quantity	   (see	   Figure	   5).	   With	   high	   probability	   the	   difference	  
between	   the	   measured	   value	   and	   the	   true	   one	   is	   lower	   than	   the	   uncertainty	   range.	   “The	  
uncertainty	  of	  the	  result	  of	  a	  measurement	  reflects	  the	  lack	  of	  exact	  knowledge	  of	  the	  value	  of	  
the	   measurand”	   and	   it	   can	   be	   defined	   as	   “the	   dispersion	   of	   the	   quantity	   values	   being	  
attributed	   to	   a	   measurand”	   (Muste	   et	   al.,	   2012)2.	   It	   derives	   either	   from	   random	   errors	   or	  
systematic	   errors	   and	   there	   are	   many	   possible	   sources	   of	   uncertainties	   in	   a	   measurement,	  
among	  which	   finite	  instrument	  resolution,	  non-­‐representative	  sampling,	  not	  exact	  values	  of	  
parameters	  and	  constants,	  approximations	  and	  assumptions,	  imprecise	  value	  of	  the	  unknown	  
measurand	  and	  so	  on	  (Muste	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  It	  is	  important	  to	  notice	  that	  the	  verification	  and	  
the	  on-­‐line	  correction	  procedure	  made	  on	  sensors	  increase	  the	  level	  of	  uncertainty	  associated	  
with	  the	  measurements.	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
2	  In	  other	  words,	  uncertainty	  can	  be	  defined	  as	  the	  standard	  deviation	  associated	  with	  the	  investigated	  quantity.	  
==PAGE:31==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
16  
	  
Figure	  5:	  graphical	  explanation	  of	  the	  difference	  between	  error	  and	  uncertainty.	  Uncertainty	  range	  is	  the	  numerical	  
interval	  around	  the	  measured	  value	  in	  which	  the	  true	  value	  lies	  with	  some	  probability.	  The	  measurement	  U	  of	  the	  
uncertainty	  is	  the	  half-­‐width	  of	  that	  interval	  and	  it	  is	  always	  positive	  (adapted	  from	  https://sisu.ut.ee/).	  
“However,	  it	  is	  not	  straightforward	  to	  quantify	  the	  level	  of	  uncertainty.	  It	  is	  difficult	  to	  know	  as	  
it	  depends	  on	  what,	  where,	  how	  and	  when	  we	  are	  measuring”	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  
• 
What:	  not	  all	  the	  measurements	  need	  to	  be	  precise,	  in	  fact	  some	  parameters	  are	  used	  
only	  as	  indicators	  so	  that	  their	  quality	  can	  also	  be	  low,	  while	  some	  others	  are	  used	  for	  
control	  strategies	  and	  they	  need	  to	  be	  precise;	  
• 
Where:	  according	  to	  the	  place	  where	  the	  measurements	  take	  place,	  the	  presence	  of	  
other	   substances	   or	   the	   environmental	   conditions	   can	   interfere	   with	   the	   measuring	  
process;	  
• 
When:	  the	  uncertainty	  is	  affected	  also	  on	  when	  the	  measurement	  is	  taken,	  that	  means	  
in	  which	  part	  of	  the	  year	  (season),	  of	  the	  week	  (week	  days	  or	  weekend),	  of	  the	  day	  
(during	   the	   day	   or	   during	   the	   night);	   in	   fact	   some	   variables	   such	   as	   temperature,	  
infiltration	  rate,	  pollutants’	  concentration	  and	  flow	  have	  different	  behaviour	  according	  
to	  the	  measuring	  period;	  
• 
How:	  for	  some	  substances	  there	  are	  only	  few	  measuring	  ways,	  but	  for	  some	  others	  
there	  are	  a	  lot	  of	  them,	  each	  one	  with	  a	  different	  level	  of	  accuracy.	  (Beaupré,	  2010).	  
In	   conclusion,	   it	   is	   possible	   to	   say	   that	   a	   measurement	   is	   complete	   only	   when	   its	   value	   is	  
accompanied	   by	   the	   statement	   of	   its	   uncertainty,	   which	   can	   be	   evaluated	   through	   an	  
uncertainty	   analysis	   (UA),	   and	   which	   gives	   information	   about	   the	   reliability	   of	   the	  
measurement.	  The	  result	  of	  an	  uncertainty	  analysis	  is	  a	  range	  in	  which	  the	  true	  value	  of	  the	  
measurand	   lies	   with	   a	   certain	   degree	   of	   confidence	   (Muste	   et	   al.,	   2012).	   For	   further	  
information	   about	   UA,	   it	   is	   possible	   to	   consult	   the	   “Guide	   to	   expression	   of	   uncertainties	   in	  
==PAGE:32==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
17  
measurements”	  (GUM,	  1993),	  that	  is	  the	  first	  worldwide	  accepted	  guideline	  for	  the	  execution	  
of	  uncertainty	  analysis.	  	  	  
	  
However,	  as	  it	  will	  be	  explained	  in	  Chapter	  4.2.2,	  uncertainties	  in	  models	  does	  not	  only	  depend	  
on	   uncertainties	   in	   measurements.	   There	   are	   many	   sources	   of	   uncertainties	   (model	   input,	  
calibration	  and	  model	  structure	  uncertainties)	  within	  a	  model,	  so	  that	  an	  UA	  is	  always	  needed	  
after	  a	  model	  construction	  in	  order	  to	  evaluate	  the	  reliability	  of	  the	  model	  itself	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  
2012).	  The	  next	  subchapter	  will	  sum	  up	  the	  most	  commonly	  used	  techniques	  in	  uncertainty	  
analysis,	   for	   the	   evaluation	   of	   measurements’	   uncertainty,	   model	   structure	   uncertainty	   and	  
parameters’	  calibration	  uncertainty.	  	  
2.3.3. Uncertainty	  estimation	  
There	  are	  different	  techniques	  for	  the	  estimation	  of	  measurement	  and	  model	  uncertainties.	  A	  
summary	  of	  all	  the	  different	  methods	  for	  uncertainty	  estimation	  is	  given	  in	  Muste	  et	  al.	  (2012)	  
and,	  for	  the	  specific	  field	  of	  quantity	  and	  quality	  modelling	  of	  urban	  stormwater,	  in	  Dotto	  et	  al.	  
(2012).	  In	  particular,	  the	  GUM	  approach	  was	  considered	  to	  be	  the	  most	  reliable	  by	  Muste	  et	  al.	  
(2012).	  Their	  paper	  contains	  a	  description	  of	  the	  law	  of	  propagation	  of	  uncertainties,	  i.e.	  the	  
“determination	   of	   the	   uncertainty	   of	   the	   output	   variable	   using	   the	   functional	   relationship	  
connecting	  input	  and	  output	  variables”	  (Muste,	  2012).	  	  For	  doing	  that	  this	  method	  utilises	  the	  
Taylor	  series	  expansion.	  
The	  following	  bullet	  points	  summarize	  the	  main	  steps	  contained	  in	  the	  GUM	  approach.	  
• 
Definition	  of	  a	  measurement	  functional	  relationship.	  
If	  Y	  is	  the	  quantity	  to	  be	  estimated,	  the	  relationship	  is	  expressed	  as	  follows	  (Equation	  
1):	  
 
𝑌= 𝐹(𝑋!, 𝑋!, … , 𝑋!)	  
(1)	  
where	  𝑋!, 𝑋!, … , 𝑋!	  are	  the	  input	  quantities.	  
The	  measurement	  process	  for	  every	  input	  quantity	  should	  be	  properly	  described	  and	  
the	  possible	  sources	  of	  error	  identified.	  
• 
Evaluation	  of	  the	  standard	  uncertainty	  of	  each	  input	  estimate,	  𝒖(𝒙𝒊).	  
They	  can	  be	  evaluated	  either	  through	  the	  use	  of	  statistical	  methods	  or	  through	  other	  
methods	  (for	  more	  details	  see	  Muste	  et	  al,	  2012).	  
• 
Combination	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  for	  every	  single	  input.	  
When	   more	   sources	   of	   uncertainty	   exist,	   the	   evaluation	   of	   the	   total	   uncertainty	   is	  
made	  as	  follows	  (Equation	  2):	  
	  
𝑢(𝑥!)! =
𝑢(𝑥!)!

where	  𝑢(𝑥!)!	  is	  the	  j-­‐th	  elemental	  error	  associated	  with	  the	  variable	  𝑥!  (Muste,	  2012).	  
• 
Determination	  of	  the	  combined	  standard	  uncertainty.	  
This	  can	  be	  done	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  law	  of	  propagation	  of	  uncertainties	  (LPU),	  
built	  from	  the	  Taylor	  series	  stopped	  at	  the	  first	  order	  of	  the	  function	  f	  (Equation	  3)	  
(Bertrand-­‐Krajewaki	  and	  Bardin,	  2002):
