18  
estimated	   covariance	   associated	   with	  𝑥!	  and	  𝑥!.	   N	   is	   the	   number	   of	   input	   variables	  
(Muste,	  2012).	  
• 
Determination	  of	  the	  result.	  
The	  result	  of	  the	  measurement	  can	  be	  expressed	  as	  (Equation	  4):	  
	  
𝑌= 𝑦± 𝑘𝑢!(𝑦)	  
(4)	  
where	  k	  is	  a	  coverage	  factor	  that	  ensures	  a	  certain	  level	  of	  confidence.	  For	  example,	  
using	  k=2,	  a	  confidence	  level	  close	  to	  95%	  is	  ensured	  (Muste,	  2012).	  
	  
The	  application	  field	  of	  the	  GUM	  approach	  is	  limited	  to	  some	  conditions.	  In	  fact,	  this	  approach	  
is	  not	  applicable	  in	  the	  cases	  described	  below:	  	  
• 
The	  model	  is	  not	  linear;	  
• 
The	  probability	  density	  function	  (pdf)	  for	  the	  output	  quantity	  is	  not	  a	  Gaussian;	  
• 
The	  pdfs	  for	  the	  input	  quantities	  are	  asymmetric;	  
• 
It	  is	  difficult	  to	  calculate	  the	  partial	  derivatives	  of	  the	  model,	  as	  it	  is	  required	  by	  the	  
law	  of	  propagation	  of	  uncertainties.	  
For	  other	  cases	  in	  which	  the	  application	  of	  the	  GUM	  methodology	  results	  to	  be	  difficult	  see	  
(Muste	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  Moreover,	  since	  the	  parameters	  Y	  are	  fixed	  (even	  if	  unknown)	  it	  is	  not	  
possible	   to	   make	   any	   probability	   consideration	   (NINT/SEMATECH	   e-­‐Handbook	   of	   Statistical	  
Methods,	  2012).	  	  
 
For	  the	  above	  explained	  reasons	  many	  other	  methods,	  mostly	  based	  on	  Bayesian	  approaches	  
or	   pseudo-­‐Bayesian	   approaches,	   are	   preferred	   for	   the	   assessing	   of	   uncertainties	   in	   models,	  
since	   “there	   are	   many	   sources	   of	   uncertainties	   that	   interact	   non-­‐linearly	   in	   the	   modelling	  
process”	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012),	  so	  that	  the	  GUM	  methodology	  is	  not	  applicable.	  In	  this	  thesis	  
focus	  will	  be	  put	  on	  the	  uncertainties	  inherent	  in	  parameters’	  calibration.	  Bayesian	  techniques	  
for	  parameters	  estimation	  treat	  both	  the	  model	  and	  the	  data	  as	  priors	  by	  assigning	  different	  
degrees	  of	  uncertainty	  (Bennet	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  These	  techniques	  are	  based	  on	  the	  Bayes	  rule,	  
which	  is	  expressed	  by	  the	  following	  Equation	  (5):	  
	  
𝑃𝐴𝐵= 𝑃𝐵𝐴𝑃(𝐴)
𝑃(𝐵)
	  
(5)	  
where	  A	  and	  B	  are	  random	  variables,	  or	  events,	  P(A|B)	  is	  the	  conditional	  probability	  that	  the	  
event	   A	   will	   occur	   given	   that	   event	   B	   has	   occurred,	   and	   P(A)	   and	   P(B)	   are	   the	   marginal	  
probability	   that	   A	   and	   B	   occur.	   This	   statistical	   formula	   introduces	   the	   concepts	   of	   Bayesian	  
interference,	   that	   can	   be	   expressed	   as	   the	   conditional	   probability	   of	   a	   variable	   A	   given	   B	  
computed	  from	  the	  conditional	  probability	  of	  B	  given	  A,	  and	  of	  prior	  and	  posterior	  knowledge	  
(Eshky,	  2010).	  In	  the	  example	  P(A)	  would	  be	  the	  prior	  probability,	  i.e.	  the	  probability	  available	  
beforehand,	   and	   P(A|B)	   the	   posterior	   probability,	   i.e.	   the	   one	   obtained	   from	   the	   prior	  
probability	   and	   from	   additional	   observations	   (Eshky,	   2010).	   The	   main	   difference	   with	  
traditional	   approaches	   in	   therefore	   that	   Bayesian	   methods	   treat	   the	   variables	   as	   random	  
variables	   instead	   of	   treating	   them	   as	   fixed	   (but	   unknown)	   quantities	   (NINT/SEMATECH	   e-­‐
Handbook	  of	  Statistical	  Methods,	  2012).	  When	  dealing	  with	  parameter	  estimation,	  being	  θ	  the	  
parameter	   needed	   to	   be	   estimated	   from	   the	   available	   data	   D,	   the	   conditional	   probability	  
P(θ|D)	  is	  expressed	  as	  (Equation	  6):	  
==PAGE:34==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
19  
	  
𝑃θ 𝐷= 𝑃𝐷𝜃𝑃(𝜃)
𝑃(𝐷)
	  
(6)	  
where	   P(D|θ)	   is	   the	   probability	   model,	   or	   likelihood	   function,	   for	   the	   dataset	   D	   given	   the	  
unknown	  parameter	  θ,	  P(θ)	  is	  the	  prior	  knowledge	  about	  the	  parameter	  θ	  and	  P(θ|D)	  is	  the	  
posterior	  distribution	  model	  for	  θ	  given	  that	  the	  data	  D	  have	  been	  observed	  (NINT/SEMATECH	  
e-­‐Handbook	  of	  Statistical	  Methods,	  2012).	  
 
Bayesian	  approaches	  therefore	  need,	  just	  after	  the	  construction	  of	  a	  model,	  the	  specification	  
of	  a	  prior	  probability	  distribution	  based	  on	  old	  information	  that	  reflects	  the	  subjective	  beliefs	  
of	  the	  model	  maker	  about	  the	  unknown	  parameter	  P(θ)	  (Eshky,	  2010).	  Then	  the	  dataset	  D	  is	  
used	   for	   investigating	   the	   starting	   assessment,	   deriving	   the	   so-­‐called	   posterior	   distribution	  
model	   for	   the	   evaluated	   parameters,	   from	   which	   the	   parameter	   estimates	   and	   their	  
confidence	  intervals	  are	  derived	  (NINT/SEMATECH	  e-­‐Handbook	  of	  Statistical	  Methods,	  2012).	  
Note	   that	   when	   prior	   information	   is	   incorrect	   or	   not	   precise,	   there	   is	   an	   erroneous	  
quantification	  of	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  Pseudo-­‐Bayesian	  approaches	  are	  
those	   that	   utilise	   an	   informal	   likelihood	   function	   instead	   of	   a	   formal	   one.	   The	   latter	   is	   a	  
statistical	  likelihood,	  in	  which	  the	  error	  model	  between	  observed	  and	  modelled	  value	  should	  
be	  specified.	  On	  the	  other	  hand	  the	  concept	  of	  informal	  likelihood	  rejects	  the	  hypothesis	  of	  a	  
true	  model	  (and	  therefore	  the	  error	  model)	  for	  the	  comparison	  of	  the	  modelled	  data,	  and	  it	  
assumes	  that	  in	  the	  model	  there	  are	  many	  interacting	  sources	  of	  errors	  that	  form	  the	  total	  
error	   of	   the	   model.	   These	   sources	   of	   errors	   do	   not	   have	   a	   precise	   formulation.	   Informal	  
likelihood	  models	  are	  therefore	  based	  on	  the	  modeller’s	  judgment,	  according	  to	  his	  perception	  
of	  the	  model	  performance	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  Formal	  likelihood	  approaches	  have	  an	  
important	   limitation	   for	   they	   applicability:	   the	   methods	   for	   quantifying	   the	   errors	   in	   model	  
structure	  are	  not	  well	  developed	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  The	  formal	  methods	  assume	  that	  
“the	  model	  structure	  is	  correct	  and	  therefore	  do	  not	  account	  for	  cases	  where	  the	  parameters	  
compensate	  for	  weakness	  in	  model	  structure”	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  For	  these	  reasons,	  
an	  informal	  likelihood	  approach	  often	  result	  to	  be	  an	  attractive	  solution	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  
2009).	  Its	  major	  limitation,	  however,	  is	  indeed	  the	  fact	  that	  it	  requires	  “subjective	  decisions	  on	  
the	   likelihood	   function	   and	   cut-­‐off	   threshold	   separating	   behavioural	   from	   non-­‐behavioural	  
models,	  and	  for	  non	  implementing	  a	  statistically	  consistent	  error	  model”	  (Jin	  et	  al.,	  2010)	  
 
Among	   the	   pseudo-­‐Bayesian	   approaches,	   the	   Monte	   Carlo	   Method	   (MCM)	   is	   often	   used	  
(Muste	  et	  al.,	  2012)	  for	  the	  fact	  that	  this	  method	  is	  simple	  and	  easy	  to	  implement	  (Vezzaro	  et	  
al.,	  2013).	  This	  method	  utilises	  an	  informal	  likelihood	  approach	  that	  will	  be	  properly	  explained	  
in	  Chapter	  2.4.2.	  The	  MCM	  is	  a	  stochastic	  approach	  that	  uses	  a	  high	  number	  of	  simulations	  M	  
for	   the	   N	   input	   quantities	   Xi	   (generally	   with	   M	   =	   106	   the	   output	   solution	   is	   covered	   with	   a	  
confidence	   level	   of	   95%),	   i.e.	   it	   generates	   M	   parameter	   sets	   (Vezzaro	   et	   al.,	   2013).	   The	  
inclusion	  among	  the	  Bayesian	  methods	  derives	  from	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  determination	  of	  a	  set	  of	  
parameter	   values	   derives	   from	   a	   prior	   probability	   distribution	   (the	   prior	   range)	   of	   random	  
sampling	  of	  individual	  parameters	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  Then	  the	  output	  of	  the	  model	  (coming	  
from	  the	  estimated	  parameters’	  values)	  is	  compared	  with	  the	  available	  data.	  The	  performance	  
of	   the	   model	   is	   evaluated	   for	   each	   of	   the	   M	   simulations	   (and	   each	   of	   the	   parameter	   sets)	  
through	  a	  chosen	  performance	  criterion.	  This	  pseudo-­‐Bayesian	  approach	  utilises	  an	  informal	  
==PAGE:35==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
20  
likelihood	  method:	  in	  fact,	  the	  modeller	  has	  to	  select	  a	  proper	  “behaviorability	  threshold”,	  so	  
that,	  when	  the	  likelihood	  value	  of	  the	  parameter	  set	  is	  below	  this	  value,	  the	  parameter	  set	  can	  
be	   rejected.	   A	   marginal	   distribution	   is	   then	   considered,	   according	   to	   which	   parameter	   sets	  
with	  low	  likelihood	  weights	  (i.e.	  below	  the	  user	  selected	  threshold)	  are	  not	  considered	  and	  
classified	  as	  “non	  behavioural”.	  Then	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  are	  estimated	  with	  a	  fixed	  
percentage	  of	  confidence	  level	  by	  using	  the	  selected	  “behavioural”	  parameters	  sets	  (whose	  
likelihood	  weights	  have	  been	  conveniently	  rescaled	  so	  that	  the	  cumulative	  probability	  sum	  is	  
1),	   and	   after	   that	   the	   intersection	   between	   the	   prediction	   bounds	   and	   the	   measurement	  
values	  is	  calculated	  for	  all	  the	  M	  simulations.	  A	  big	  disadvantage	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  
method,	  and	  in	  general	  of	  all	  pseudo-­‐Bayesian	  methods,	  is	  that	  the	  acceptance	  or	  rejection	  of	  
parameter	   sets	   depends	   on	   a	   subjective	   user-­‐defined	   threshold,	   that	   is	   included	   in	   the	  
assumed	  likelihood	  function,	  which	  has	  large	  impact	  on	  results	  (Dotto	  et	  al,	  2012).	  Through	  the	  
use	  of	  a	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  approach,	  in	  this	  thesis,	  a	  reduction	  of	  the	  subjectivity	  related	  
to	  the	  user-­‐defined	  threshold	  is	  achieved.	  For	  a	  better	  explanation	  of	  the	  different	  steps	  for	  
the	  application	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  see	  Chapter	  2.4.2.	  
2.3.4. Uncertainties	  related	  to	  sensor	  performance	  
As	   already	   mentioned	   in	   this	   work	   before,	   the	   calibration	   of	   the	   sensors,	   but	   also	   the	  
conditions	  of	  installation,	  can	  account	  for	  the	  increasing	  of	  the	  uncertainties	  connected	  to	  the	  
measurements	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  	  A	  briefly	  review	  of	  the	  factors	  affecting	  the	  performance	  of	  
the	  different	  types	  of	  sensors	  is	  here	  presented3.	  
• 
Flow	  and	  volume	  measurements:	  in	  (Bertrand-­‐Krajewski	  and	  Bardin,	  2002)	  the	  relative	  
uncertainties	   connected	   with	   flow	   rates	   and	   volumes	   calculations,	   based	   on	   water	  
depth	   and	   velocity	   measurements	   out	   of	   a	   storage	   tank	   in	   the	   sewer	   system	   of	  
Vénissieux	  (France),	  are	  presented.	  They	  turn	  out	  to	  be	  around	  20%	  for	  flow	  rates	  and	  
8%	  for	  volumes.	  The	  sources	  of	  uncertainties	  for	  measurements	  and	  samples	  are	  listed	  
in	  (Bertrand-­‐Krajewski	  et	  al.,	  2003).	  
• 
Ion-­‐selective	   sensors:	   in	   (Winkler	   et	   al.,	   2004)	   the	   sources	   of	   uncertainties	   for	  
ammonium	  concentration	  measurements	  are	  presented.	  In	  particular,	  the	  membrane	  
of	  the	  electrode	  is	  generally	  ruined	  by	  some	  irreversible	  bindings	  with	  disturbance	  ions	  
(such	  as	  potassium).	  The	  lifespan	  of	  an	  ion-­‐selective	  sensor	  is	  expected	  to	  be	  of	  2-­‐3	  
months	  with	  raw	  wastewater.	  Moreover	  the	  sensor	  has	  to	  be	  installed	  in	  such	  a	  way	  
that	   it	   does	   not	   interfere	   with	   the	   wastewater	   passage	   in	   the	   sewer	   system.	   The	  
relative	  uncertainty	  for	  raw	  wastewater	  has	  been	  calculated	  to	  be	  within	  the	  20-­‐30%	  
range.	  
2.4. WATER	  QUALITY	  MODELS	  
The	  construction	  of	  a	  model	  is	  essential	  for	  the	  development	  of	  an	  integrated	  approach:	  in	  
fact,	  in	  order	  to	  detect	  the	  water	  quality,	  the	  various	  components	  of	  a	  drainage	  system	  (i.e.	  
the	  sewer	  system,	  the	  WWTP	  and	  the	  RWB)	  should	  be	  analysed.	  
 
In	   this	   project	   the	   software	   WEST	   (Wastewater	   treatment	   plant	   Engine	   for	   Simulation	   and	  
Training)	  was	  used	  for	  the	  creation	  of	  a	  model.	  This	  software	  was	  traditionally	  developed	  for	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
3	  Only	  the	  types	  of	  sensors	  whose	  measurements	  have	  been	  used	  for	  this	  thesis	  are	  analysed	  in	  this	  section.	  
==PAGE:36==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
21  
dynamic	  modelling	  and	  simulation	  of	  wastewater	  treatment	  plants,	  for	  studying	  the	  various	  
kinds	  of	  processes	  (physical,	  chemical	  and	  biological)	  that	  occur	  within	  them.	  However,	  for	  a	  
holistic	  approach	  to	  be	  developed,	  the	  product	  WESTforOPTIMIZATION	  has	  to	  be	  used.	  This	  
product	  enables	  to	  set	  up	  complex	  models	  (in	  which	  the	  three	  different	  parts	  of	  a	  drainage	  
system	   are	   included)	   and	   to	   run	   long-­‐term	   dynamic	   simulations.	   This	   can	   be	   done	   with	   a	  
relative	  short	  time	  frame	  compared	  to	  other	  detailed	  hydraulic	  models	  (DHI,	  2015).	  Therefore,	  
for	  the	  description	  of	  a	  highly	  detailed	  system	  with	  a	  short	  computational	  time,	  the	  Tornado	  
engine	  was	  introduced	  in	  the	  fourth	  version	  of	  WEST:	  it	  is	  a	  software	  kernel	  “which	  introduces	  
a	  higher	  flexibility	  and	  results	  in	  a	  better	  performance”	  (Solvi,	  2006).	  The	  software	  WEST	  is	  
based	   on	   lumped	   conceptual	   models	   and	   utilizes	   ordinary	   differential	   equations	   and	  
differential	  and	  algebraic	  equations	  (DAEs).	  Moreover,	  it	  has	  an	  open	  structure,	  so	  that	  it	  is	  
possible	  for	  the	  users	  to	  change	  existing	  models	  and	  to	  add	  new	  models	  to	  the	  already	  present	  
ones.	  In	  the	  Configuration	  Environment	  is	  possible	  to	  set	  up	  graphically	  the	  studied	  system,	  
which	   will	   be	   written	   automatically	   in	   Model	   Specification	   Language	   (MSL),	   a	   high	   level	  
oriented	   language,	   by	   the	   extraction	   of	   the	   relevant	   equations	   from	   the	   model	   base	   (Solvi,	  
2006).	  The	  model	  base	  utilized	  for	  this	  case	  study	  is	  the	  KOSIM-­‐WEST	  model	  base.	  
	  
In	   WESTforOPTIMIZATION	   an	   extensive	   library	   for	   the	   setting	   up	   of	   the	   different	   parts	   of	   a	  
model	  is	  present,	  so	  that	  an	  integrated	  model	  within	  a	  unique	  platform	  can	  be	  created	  (DHI,	  
2015).	  Within	  this	  subpart	  of	  WEST	  is	  therefore	  possible	  to	  describe	  both	  the	  water	  runoff	  and	  
the	  sewer	  system	  related	  to	  a	  specific	  catchment.	  The	  different	  submodels	  are	  connected	  to	  
each	   other	   through	   the	   use	   of	   transformer	   units,	   since	   every	   one	   of	   them	   utilizes	   different	  
variables	  (components)	  for	  describing	  the	  water	  quality	  (Vezzaro,	  2015).	  For	  the	  development	  
of	   the	   integrated	   model	   of	   the	   Viby	   catchment,	   the	   library	   IUWS	   (Integrated	   Urban	  
Wastewater	  System)	  was	  used	  and	  the	  following	  components	  were	  simulated:	  
• 
Flow;	  
• 
Pollutants:	  chemical	  oxygen	  demand	  (COD),	  phosphate,	  ammonia	  and	  total	  suspended	  
solids	  (TSS).	  
The	  input	  data	  for	  the	  model	  is	  the	  rain	  recorded	  in	  Viby	  between	  June	  2013	  and	  December	  
2014.	  The	  water	  originating	  from	  a	  catchment	  and	  entering	  the	  sewer	  system	  can	  be	  divided	  
into	  wastewater	  coming	  from	  households,	  institutions	  and	  industries	  and	  water	  generated	  by	  
the	  surface	  runoff	  (Solvi,	  2006).	  In	  combined	  sewer	  systems	  dry	  weather	  flow	  is	  estimated	  to	  
be	   between	   1	   and	   5%	   of	   the	   wet	   weather	   flow,	   so	   that	   stormwater	   results	   to	   be	   a	   very	  
important	   contribution	   for	   the	   evaluation	   of	   water	   quality.	   During	   the	   investigation	   of	  
pollutants’	  concentration	  in	  overflow	  structures,	  it	  can	  be	  assumed	  that	  the	  concentration	  is	  
comparable	  to	  the	  one	  of	  stormwater,	  because	  of	  the	  fact	  that	  overflows	  occur	  during	  intense	  
rainfall	  events	  (Arnbjerg-­‐Nielsen,	  2000	  –	  cited	  in	  Olesen,	  2015).	  It	  is	  important	  to	  note	  that	  the	  
spatial	   distribution	   of	   the	   rainfall	   is	   not	   considered	   in	   the	   WEST	   model,	   and	   the	   rainfall	   is	  
assumed	  to	  be	  uniform	  in	  the	  whole	  catchment	  (Zoppou,	  2001).	  
	  
Each	   catchment	   includes	   different	   submodels	   for	   taking	   into	   account	   the	   various	   different	  
processes	  affecting	  dry	  weather	  and	  wet	  weather	  flow	  (Figure	  6),	  that	  are:	  a	  model	  generating	  
evaporation,	  a	  model	  for	  the	  runoff	  generation,	  a	  model	  for	  the	  runoff	  concentration	  affected	  
==PAGE:37==

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
22  
by	   travelling	   time	   and	   water	   retention	   and	   a	   model	   for	   the	   DWF	   generation	   (Solvi,	   2006;	  
Vezzaro,	  2015).	  A	  detailed	  and	  complete	  description	  of	  every	  submodel	  can	  be	  found	  in	  (Solvi,	  
2006).	  	  
	  
Figure	  6:	  screenshot	  of	  the	  submodels	  for	  the	  combined	  sewer	  system	  of	  the	  Viby	  catchment.	  
In	  this	  case	  study	  a	  Tank	  is	  added	  for	  symbolizing	  the	  pipes	  in	  the	  catchment.	  The	  tank	  has	  
been	  created	  for	  the	  inclusion	  of	  the	  phenomena	  of	  deposition	  and	  resuspension	  of	  TSS	  (which	  
are	  not	  investigated	  here),	  but	  its	  size	  is	  so	  small	  that	  it	  does	  not	  have	  any	  effect	  on	  the	  flow.	  	  
	  
The	   Catchment	   and	   the	   Tank	   blocks	   represent	   the	   sewer	   system	   in	   the	   model	   under	  
consideration.	  The	  Catchment	  includes	  the	  input	  quantity	  (rain)	  and	  simulates	  the	  dry	  weather	  
flow	   and	   the	   runoff	   generation,	   while	   the	   Tank	   simulates	   the	   water	   transport	   and	   the	  
processes	  of	  sedimentation	  and	  resuspension	  within	  the	  sewers.	  The	  results	  coming	  from	  the	  
combination	  of	  the	  two	  blocks	  are	  the	  determination	  of	  the	  total	  flow	  and	  the	  calculation	  of	  
pollutants’	  loads	  and	  concentrations	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  
2.4.1. Dry	  weather	  flow	  
Within	  the	  dry	  weather	  flow	  the	  wastewater	  flow	  produced	  is	  simply	  calculated	  by	  multiplying	  
the	   number	   of	   inhabitants	   (PE)4	  for	   each	   catchment	   for	   the	   mean	   wastewater	   production	  
volume	  (Equation	  7).

𝑚!

loads	   are	   calculated	   by	   multiplying	   the	   WW	   volume	   for	   the	   mean	   pollutant	   concentration	  
value	  (Equation	  8).

pattern	   (that	   changes	   from	   place	   to	   place),	   which	   is	   included	   in	   this	   study	   through	   the	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
4	  PE	  stands	  for	  Population	  Equivalent,	  i.e.	  the	  quantity	  of	  biodegradable	  substances	  produced	  and	  discharged	  as	  
wastewater	  into	  the	  sewer	  system	  within	  one	  day	  by	  a	  person.	  For	  non-­‐domestic	  sewer,	  the	  quantity	  of	  wastewater	  
produced	  must	  be	  divided	  for	  the	  quantity	  of	  WW	  produced	  by	  an	  IE,	  so	  that	  the	  number	  of	  fictitious	  inhabitants	  
corresponding	  to	  the	  same	  amount	  of	  WW	  produced	  can	  be	  calculated.

multiplied	  for	  this	  hourly	  variation	  (Vezzaro,	  2015).	  Also	  a	  variation	  during	  weekends	  and	  a	  
seasonal	  variation	  are	  often	  detected.	  
2.4.2. Wet	  weather	  flow	  
Within	  the	  wet	  weather	  flow	  some	  processes	  occur	  that	  reduce	  the	  water	  quantity	  during	  the	  
runoff	  generation	  phenomenon.	  In	  fact	  water	  volumes	  undergo	  some	  losses	  during	  their	  paths	  
from	  the	  rain	  to	  the	  effluent:	  during	  runoff	  generation	  some	  water	  is	  lost	  due	  to	  depressions,	  
evaporation	  and	  infiltration	  so	  that	  the	  remaining	  part	  only	  derives	  from	  the	  so-­‐called	  effective	  
rain	  (Figure	  7).	  The	  quantity	  of	  runoff	  produced	  by	  a	  rain	  event	  depends	  on	  the	  area	  under	  
consideration	   connected	   with	   the	   sewer	   network	   and	   to	   the	   ratio	   between	   pervious	   and	  
impervious	  area:	  in	  fact,	  infiltration	  only	  occurs	  in	  pervious	  areas.	  Once	  the	  quantity	  of	  the	  
runoff	  has	  been	  identified,	  the	  flux	  of	  a	  pollutant	  at	  the	  time	  t	  can	  be	  calculated	  as	  follows	  
(Equation	  9):

the	  runoff	  (Solvi,	  2006).	  
	  
Figure	  7:	  representation	  of	  the	  different	  kinds	  of	  losses	  that	  transform	  total	  rainfall	  into	  effective	  rainfall,	  simulated	  
in	  each	  catchment	  of	  the	  model	  (from	  Solvi,	  2006).	  
Surface	   runoff	   in	   urban	   drainage	   systems	   is	   generally	   modelled	   using	   simplified	   approaches	  
(hydrological	  model),	  because	  of	  the	  highly	  heterogeneous	  conditions	  on	  the	  surfaces	  (Solvi,	  
2006).	  Indeed,	  the	  runoff	  in	  WEST	  is	  simulated	  by	  a	  three-­‐tanks-­‐in-­‐cascade	  method	  for	  each	  
catchment	   (see	   Figure	   8	   below).	   Each	   tank	   is	   modelled	   as	   a	   linear	   reservoir,	   described	   by	  
Equations	  12	  and	  13	  presented	  below.	  
	  
Figure	  8:	  zoom	  of	  the	  linear	  tanks	  in	  cascade	  for	  the	  simulation	  of	  the	  runoff	  (included	  in	  each	  catchment).	  
The	  sewer	  system	  (composed	  here	  by	  the	  Catchment	  and	  the	  Tank	  blocks)	  in	  hydrodynamic	  
models	  is	  generally	  described	  by	  a	  continuity	  equation	  (mass	  conservation)	  and	  a	  momentum

The	  solution	  of	  the	  2	  equation	  will	  give	  information	  about	  Q(x,t)	  and	  y(x,t).	  The	  information	  
about	  flow	  and	  water	  height	  in	  a	  certain	  position	  and	  at	  a	  certain	  time	  is	  often	  troublesome,	  
especially	   in	   the	   case	   of	   conditions	   downstream	   (structures	   or	   pipes)	   that	   change	   the	  
relationship	  between	  flux	  and	  height	  upstream	  (Solvi,	  2006).	  
	  	  
However,	  with	  the	  assumption	  of	  steady	  state	  in	  slices	  of	  pipe	  of	  a	  certain	  length,	  the	  pipe	  can	  
be	  replaced	  with	  reservoirs	  in	  series,	  i.e.	  the	  continuity	  equation	  can	  be	  simplified	  with	  the	  
following	  ‘Flow-­‐Volume’	  relationships	  (Equations	  12	  and	  13):

(12)

(13)	  
where	  𝑄!"	  and	  𝑄!"#	  are	  the	  water	  inflow	  and	  outflow,	  while	  V	  is	  the	  water	  volume	  occupied	  in	  
the	  reservoir.	  The	  number	  of	  in-­‐series-­‐tanks	  and	  the	  residence	  time	  of	  the	  water	  in	  each	  tank	  
(k)	   are	   determined	   starting	   from	   the	   physical	   properties	   of	   the	   pipe,	   through	   the	   Kalinin-­‐
Miljukov	  method.	  For	  a	  detailed	  description	  of	  the	  method	  see	  (Solvi,	  2006),	  Chapter	  3.2.2.1.	  
In	  this	  way	  the	  hydrodynamic	  model	  has	  been	  transformed	  in	  a	  more	  simplified	  hydrological	  
model	  (Solvi,	  2006).	  The	  major	  diversity	  between	  these	  two	  kinds	  of	  models	  is	  that	  the	  first	  
one	  considers	  the	  spatial	  variability	  of	  the	  process	  (Zoppou,	  2001).	  The	  linear	  reservoir	  cascade	  
method	  for	  the	  runoff	  generation	  is	  a	  special	  case	  of	  the	  unit	  hydrograph	  approach,	  which	  is	  
also	  a	  hydrological	  model.	  For	  having	  a	  complete	  explanation	  of	  the	  approach	  see	  (Zoppou,	  
2001).	  To	  sum	  up	  the	  advantages	  of	  using	  a	  hydrological	  model	  see	  Figure	  9.

Figure	  9:	  schematic	  representation	  of	  the	  advantages	  of	  using	  a	  hydrological	  model	  instead	  of	  a	  hydrodynamic	  one,	  
that	  allows	  for	  the	  achievement	  of	  a	  higher	  stability	  of	  the	  model	  (adapted	  from	  Solvi,	  2006).	  
2.4.3. Limitations	  
This	  conceptual	  model,	  however,	  cannot	  represent	  backwater	  effects,	  due	  to	  the	  exceeding	  of	  
the	  pipe	  capacity,	  that	  are	  responsible	  for	  overflows.	  Backwater	  effects	  are	  produced	  by	  the	  
decreasing	  of	  the	  slope	  of	  sewer	  systems,	  the	  increase	  of	  the	  roughness	  of	  the	  pipes,	  by	  under	  
dimensioned	  pipes	  or	  by	  pipes	  whose	  section	  has	  been	  decreased	  by	  the	  presence	  of	  some	  
obstacles	   (sediments).	   Some	   methods	   have	   been	   found	   for	   overcoming	   the	   problem,	  
described	  by	  (Solvi,	  2006)	  in	  Chapter	  3.3.2.1.	  The	  most	  efficient	  method	  seems	  to	  be	  the	  use	  of	  
a	  pump,	  which	  diverts	  the	  part	  of	  the	  flow	  exceeding	  the	  pipes’	  capacity	  to	  an	  upstream	  tank.	  
Moreover,	   CSO	   structures	   are	   not	   present	   in	   the	   basic	   WEST	   setup:	   the	   solution	   is	   to	  
implement	   a	   tank	   with	   a	   fixed	   maximum	   flow	   (at	   which	   no	   overflow	   occurs).	   When	   the	  
maximum	  flow	  is	  exceeded,	  the	  extra	  water	  is	  taken	  out	  from	  the	  sewage	  system	  (Solvi,	  2006).

3. CASE	  STUDY	  
In	  this	  section	  the	  study	  area	  of	  Viby	  (Aahrus)	  and	  its	  WWTP	  is	  presented,	  together	  with	  the	  
data	  coming	  from	  the	  AMOK	  project	  and	  a	  description	  of	  the	  sensors	  used	  for	  data	  collection5.	  
Moreover,	  the	  rainfall	  input	  data	  for	  the	  catchment	  of	  Viby	  will	  be	  described.	  At	  the	  end	  of	  the	  
section	  the	  model	  set-­‐up	  created	  with	  the	  software	  WEST	  is	  investigated.	  
3.1. SITE	  DESCRIPTION	  
Aahrus	   is	   the	   second	   biggest	   city	   in	   Denmark	   and	   the	   company	   Aahrus	   Vand	   supervises	   its	  
sewage	  system	  operation.	  The	  catchment	  of	  the	  city	  is	  divided	  into	  three	  main	  subcatchments:	  
Åby,	  Marselisborg	  and	  Viby,	  each	  one	  with	  their	  own	  WWTP.	  The	  catchment	  utilized	  for	  this	  
project	  is	  the	  one	  of	  Viby	  whose	  WWTP	  has	  been	  designed	  for	  84,000	  Population	  Equivalent.	  
The	  subcatchments	  connected	  to	  the	  WWTP	  of	  Viby	  are:	  Holme,	  Harlev,	  Stavtrup,	  Hasselager,	  
Kolt	  and	  Viby.	  The	  total	  area	  of	  the	  catchment	  is	  1450	  ha,	  subdivided	  into	  700	  ha	  of	  combined	  
sewage	  system	  and	  750	  of	  separate	  sewer	  system	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  A	  map	  of	  the	  catchment	  can	  
be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  10.	  
	  
Figure	  10:	  map	  of	  the	  subcatchments	  connected	  to	  the	  WWTP	  of	  Viby.	  The	  subcatchments	  surrounded	  by	  blue	  lines	  
are	  those	  with	  a	  separated	  or	  partly	  separated	  sewage	  system,	  while	  the	  catchment	  of	  Viby,	  surrounded	  by	  a	  red	  
line,	  is	  the	  only	  one	  with	  a	  combined	  sewage	  system.	  The	  black	  star	  represents	  the	  WWTP,	  while	  the	  main	  pumping	  
stations	  are	  indicated	  with	  black	  triangles	  (from	  Jézéquel,	  2014;	  Olesen,	  2015).	  
The	  area	  of	  interest	  of	  this	  project	  is	  the	  overflow	  structure	  situated	  at	  the	  inlet	  of	  Viby	  WWTP	  
(see	   Figure	   11).	   The	   plant	   has	   a	   maximum	   capacity	   of	   1260	   l/s;	   during	   highly	   intense	   rain	  
events,	   when	   this	   capacity	   is	   exceeded,	   the	   water	   in	   excess	   is	   diverted	   to	   the	   overflow	  
structure	  that	  behaves	  like	  a	  storage	  tank	  with	  an	  equalizing	  effect	  on	  the	  flow.	  The	  tank	  has	  a	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
5	  Only	   the	   sensors	   measuring	   flow	   and	   ammonia	   are	   here	   presented,	   since	   flow	   and	   ammonia	   are	   the	   only	  
investigated	   parameters	   in	   this	   study.	   A	   description	   of	   the	   sensors	   used	   for	   measuring	   temperature,	   pH,	  
conductivity	  and	  TSS	  concentration	  can	  be	  found	  in	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).

DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
27  
capacity	  of	  16,000	  m3	  for	  water	  storage,	  and	  the	  excess	  water	  is	  stored	  there	  until	  the	  capacity	  
of	  the	  WWTP	  allows	  it	  to	  be	  pumped	  to	  the	  treatment	  units.	  This	  overflow	  structure	  is	  able	  to	  
prevent,	  in	  the	  majority	  of	  the	  cases,	  the	  discharge	  of	  untreated	  water	  into	  the	  nearby	  river,	  
the	  Døde	  Å.	  The	  worst	  case	  of	  discharging	  into	  the	  river	  without	  any	  treatment	  is	  observed	  few	  
times	  per	  year	  (Jézéquel,	  2014;	  Olesen,	  2015).	  For	  a	  better	  understanding	  of	  the	  model	  under	  
consideration	  see	  Figure	  12.	  
	  
Figure	  11:	  position	  of	  the	  sensors	  at	  the	  inlet	  of	  the	  Viby	  WWTP	  for	  both	  in	  situ	  and	  ex	  situ	  measurements.	  
There	  are	  two	  sets	  of	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  installed	  in	  different	  positions:	  one	  is	  installed	  inside	  the	  
inlet	  pipe	  to	  the	  WWTP,	  just	  before	  the	  overflow	  structure,	  in	  situ	  measurements,	  while	  the	  
other	   one	   is	   installed	   in	   a	   container	   in	   which	   part	   of	   the	   water	   is	   pumped	   from	   the	   inlet	  
channel,	  ex	  situ	  measurements.	  The	  pump	  is	  placed	  in	  the	  same	  position	  of	  the	  in	  situ	  sensors	  
installation.	   Water	   quality	   parameters	   (temperature,	   pH,	   conductivity,	   ammonium	   and	   TSS	  
concentration)	  are	  measured	  both	  is	  situ	  and	  ex	  situ,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  hydraulic	  parameters	  (flow	  
and	   water	   level).	   Moreover	   the	   measurements	   of	   COD	   (dissolved	   and	   total)	   and	   total	  
phosphorous	  (TP)	  have	  been	  performed	  in	  the	  container.	  All	  the	  parameters	  measured	  with	  
on-­‐line	  sensors	  have	  been	  measured	  with	  a	  frequency	  of	  1	  minute	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  
	  
Additionally,	   seven	   short	   measuring	   campaigns	   have	   been	   performed	   between	   September	  
2013	  and	  October	  2014	  (for	  more	  information	  about	  start	  date,	  end	  date,	  number	  of	  samples,	  
accuracy	   and	   precision	   of	   the	   measurements	   see	   Table	   1	   and	   Table	   2).	   Water	   samples	   are	  
taken	  thanks	  to	  the	  use	  of	  a	  “peristaltic	  pump	  at	  a	  fixed	  interval	  of	  time”	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  The	  
timespan	   is	   chosen	   accordingly	   to	   meteorological	   previsions	   in	   order	   that	   it	   results	   to	   be	  
representative.	   The	   measuring	   campaigns	   were	   performed	   in	   order	   to	   compare	   the	  
measurements	   from	   on-­‐line	   sensors	   with	   the	   measurements	   from	   traditional	   samples,	   for	  
possibly	  doing	  the	  sensor	  calibration	  and	  verification	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).

Figure	  12:	  schematic	  representation	  of	  the	  catchment	  under	  consideration.	  The	  WWTP	  is	  not	  included	  in	  this	  study,	  
since	  the	  sensors	  are	  placed	  before	  it	  (from	  Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  
3.2. THE	  AMOK	  PROJECT	  
The	  AMOK	  project	  (Advanced	  monitoring	  of	  overflow	  quality6)	  has	  been	  led	  by	  the	  company	  
Aahrus	  Vand	  (the	  public	  water	  company	  of	  the	  city)	  together	  with	  DTU	  Environment,	  Aalborg	  
University,	   Krüger	   and	   DHI.	   The	   aim	   of	   the	   project	   was	   to	   help	   the	   utility	   company	   in	  
determining	   the	   pollutants’	   loads	   during	   overflow	   events,	   through	   the	   collection	   of	   a	   large	  
dataset	   of	   water	   quantity	   and	   quality	   parameters	   at	   the	   inlet	   of	   the	   WWTP.	   Collecting	   a	  
representative	  dataset	  has	  led	  to	  the	  possibility	  of	  using	  the	  data	  for	  the	  calibration	  of	  water	  
quality	  and	  quantity	  parameters	  in	  the	  model	  created	  with	  WEST,	  and	  thus	  having	  a	  better	  
understanding	   of	   the	   in-­‐sewer	   processes	   (Jézéquel,	   2014;	   Olesen,	   2015).	   The	   data	   were	  
collected	  from	  June	  2013	  to	  December	  2014	  with	  a	  frequency	  of	  1	  measurement	  per	  minute.	  
The	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  utilised	  for	  the	  AMOK	  project	  are	  many,	  but	  hereunder	  only	  the	  sensors	  
for	  the	  measurements	  of	  flow	  and	  ammonia	  are	  presented.	  
• 
Hydraulic	  parameters:	  the	  rain	  intensity	  (that	  is	  the	  input	  series	  in	  the	  WEST	  model)	  
has	   been	   measured	   through	   a	   tipping	   bucket	   rain	   gauge	   in	   the	   WWTP.	   Each	   tip	  
consists	  of	  0.2	  mm	  of	  precipitation	  and	  a	  rain	  event	  is	  defined	  as	  at	  least	  2	  tips	  in	  less	  
than	  1	  hour.	  More	  than	  60	  minutes	  between	  two	  tips	  correspond	  to	  two	  separate	  rain	  
events.	  The	  intensity	  is	  then	  defined	  through	  the	  time	  needed	  for	  the	  measuring	  gauge	  
to	  fill	  up.	  The	  flow	  is	  measured	  in	  the	  inlet	  channel	  (and	  also	  in	  the	  container)	  through	  
the	  use	  of	  an	  electromagnetic	  flowmeter	  (Jézéquel,	  2014;	  Olesen,	  2015).	  	  
• 
Water	   quality	   parameters:	   ammonia	  concentration	  is	  measured	  both	  in	  situ	  and	  ex	  
situ	  through	  the	  use	  of	  ion-­‐selective	  electrodes	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).	  
Table	   5	   gives	   a	   more	   detailed	   description	   of	   the	   above	   defined	   sensors,	   through	   the	  
explanation	  of	  the	  technique	  used	  for	  measurements,	  the	  functioning	  principle	  of	  the	  sensor	  
and	  its	  location.	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
6	  It	  is	  the	  translation	  from	  the	  Danish	  Avanceret	  Online	  Måling	  af	  Overløbskvalitet	  (Jézéquel,	  2014).

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli

Table	  5:	  details	  on	  the	  on-­‐line	  sensors	  used	  in	  Viby	  WWTP	  (adapted	  from	  Jézéquel,	  2014).	  
Sensor	  
Measured	  
parameter	  
Technique	  
Principle	  
Location	  
RIM7499	  
(Observator	  
Instruments)	  
Rainfall	  
accumulation	  
Tipping	  bucket	  
rain	  gauge	  
For	   every	   2	   mm	   of	   rain	  
collected,	  the	  bucket	  tilts	  and	  
sends	  an	  electrical	  signal	  to	  a	  
data	  logger.	  
Viby	  WWTP	  
MAGFLOW	  5000	  
(Siemens)	  
Flow	  rate	  
Electromagnetic	  
flowmeter	  
Magnetic	  
coils	  
mounted	  
diametrically	  
on	  
the	  
measuring	   pipe	   generate	   a	  
pulsed	   electromagnetic	   field.	  
The	   liquid	   flowing	   inside	   the	  
field	  induces	  a	  voltage	  that	  is	  
then	   converted	   into	   flow	  
readings.	  
Between	  the	  CSO	  
structure	  and	  Viby	  
WWTP,	  and	  in	  the	  
container	  stream	  
A-­‐ISE	  sc	  (Hach	  
Lange)	  
Ammonium	  
(NH4-­‐N),	  
potassium	  (K)	  
and	  temperature	  
Potentiometric	  
measurement	  
using	  ion-­‐selective	  
electrodes	  (ISE)	  
The	  
electrodes	  
have	  
a	  
membrane	  able	  to	  let	  adhere	  
only	   a	   specific	   kind	   of	   ion	   so	  
that	   an	   ion	   specific	   potential	  
forms	  
on	  
the	  
membrane	  
surface.	  
In	  situ	  and	  ex	  situ	  
An	   operational	   problem,	   due	   to	   the	   position	   of	   the	   two	   sensors	   (for	   flow	   and	   ammonia	  
measurements),	  is	  present:	  in	  fact	  the	  quality	  sensor	  has	  been	  placed	  at	  the	  internal	  overflow	  
structure,	   while	   the	   flow	   sensor	   at	   the	   inlet	   of	   the	   WWTP,	   i.e.	   after	   the	   CSO	   structure.	  
Therefore	  the	  flow	  measurements	  do	  no	  count	  the	  water	  stored	  in	  the	  CSO	  structure	  during	  
intense	   rain	   events,	   so	   that	   the	   flow	   readings	   will	   be	   equal	   to	   1260	   l/s,	   that	   is	   the	   plant	  
capacity,	  but	  indeed	  the	  real	  flow	  is	  higher.	  After	  the	  rain	  event,	  when	  the	  water	  from	  the	  CSO	  
structure	  is	  pumped	  back	  into	  the	  WWTP,	  the	  flow	  measurements	  will	  count	  also	  the	  water	  
coming	  from	  the	  overflow	  structure.	  Moreover,	  the	  internal	  overflow	  structure	  only	  receives	  
the	  wastewater	  coming	  from	  the	  Viby	  catchment,	  while	  the	  inlet	  of	  the	  WWTP	  receives	  water	  
from	  all	  the	  catchments	  (Holme,	  Harlev,	  Stavtrup,	  Hasselager	  and	  Kolt).	  However,	  the	  quality	  
of	  the	  water	  coming	  from	  the	  catchment	  of	  Viby	  can	  be	  considered	  as	  representative	  of	  the	  
water	  quality	  of	  all	  the	  other	  catchments	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  
3.3. MODEL	  SET-­‐UP	  
The	   software	   WEST	   was	   used	   in	   2015	   by	   Olesen	   for	   the	   creation	   of	   the	   model	   of	   the	   Viby	  
catchment.	  I	  used	  this	  model	  for	  the	  simulation	  of	  the	  system	  and	  for	  the	  calibration	  of	  both	  
the	  hydraulic	  and	  the	  water	  quality	  parameters.	  WEST	  was	  used	  with	  Matlab	  as	  an	  interface	  
for	   an	   easier	   change	   of	   the	   parameters.	   Some	   Matlab	   scripts	   are	   used	   for	   calling	   the	   code	  
behind	   the	   WEST	   model	   and	   for	   making	   the	   model	   parameters	   change.	   The	   calibration	   has	  
been	  made	  for	  11	  different	  rain	  events	  and	  for	  a	  long	  term	  event	  (60	  days),	  which	  was	  divided	  
into	   two	   shorter	   long	   term	   events	   (LT1	   and	   LT2)	   for	   having	   a	   better	   understanding	   of	   the	  
results.	   Different	   scripts	   are	   used	   according	   to	   the	   parameters	   to	   be	   changed,	   because	  
different	  calibration	  methods	  are	  used:	  
• 
For	   hydraulic	   parameters	   a	   calibration	   through	   a	   traditional	   method	   was	   chosen	  
(fminsearch);	  
==PAGE:45==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
30  
• 
For	   water	   quality	   parameters	   an	   uncertainty	   based	   calibration	   was	   used	   (modified	  
Monte	  Carlo),	  and	  the	  results	  are	  compared	  with	  the	  ones	  coming	  from	  a	  fminsearch	  
calibration	  made	  on	  the	  same	  parameters	  by	  Olesen	  (except	  for	  the	  long	  period	  for	  
which	  the	  traditional	  calibration	  has	  been	  made	  within	  this	  work).	  
The	  scripts	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  C	  for	  both	  the	  methods	  for	  the	  first	  rain	  event	  (MC3)7,	  
except	  for	  the	  scripts	  regarding	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  on	  water	  quality	  parameters	  that	  
refers	  to	  the	  long	  term	  event.	  
	  
A	  graphical	  overview	  of	  the	  elements	  of	  the	  WEST	  model	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  13.	  The	  model	  
is	  made	  up	  of	  four	  elements:	  a	  Combined	  Catchment,	  a	  Separate	  Catchment,	  a	  Combiner	  of	  
the	  flow	  and	  a	  Tank.	  In	  Table	  6	  the	  names	  of	  the	  elements	  (left	  column)	  and	  the	  names	  of	  the	  
models	  utilised	  for	  their	  representation	  in	  WEST	  (right	  column)	  are	  presented.	  
Table	  6:	  input	  series	  and	  elements	  of	  the	  WEST	  model,	  together	  with	  the	  name	  of	  the	  models	  used	  by	  WEST	  for	  the	  
representation	  of	  the	  different	  block	  elements	  (adapted	  from	  Olesen,	  2015).	  
Element	  of	  the	  Viby	  catchment	  
Model	  used	  by	  WEST	  
WetWeather	  
Rain	  series	  input	  
Combined	  Catchment	  
“Catchment_Model_Combined”	  
Separate	  Catchment

Figure	  13:	  screenshot	  of	  the	  conceptual	  model	  setup	  in	  WEST.	  
The	  parameters	  used	  in	  each	  block	  of	  the	  conceptual	  model	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  For	  
all	  the	  parameters	  default	  values	  are	  used,	  except	  for	  some	  of	  them	  that	  have	  been	  changed	  
manually	  by	  Olesen.	  Some	  clarifications	  about	  the	  conceptual	  model	  must	  be	  done:	  
• 
The	   Catchment	   was	   divided	   into	   combined	   and	   separate	   by	   Olesen.	   This	   is	   not	  
necessary	  because	  the	  parameters	  of	  the	  two	  blocks	  are	  the	  same,	  but	  this	  way	  was	  
chosen	   for	   having	   “a	   more	   realistic	   value	   of	   the	   pervious	   fraction	   of	   the	   combined	  
catchment”	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
7	  The	  scripts	  for	  the	  other	  events	  are	  exactly	  the	  same	  as	  the	  one	  presented	  in	  Appendix	  C	  with	  only	  the	  event’s	  
date	  changing	  and	  the	  index	  of	  the	  matrices,	  which	  contain	  the	  sensors’	  measurements	  (1	  per	  minute)	  for	  flow	  and	  
ammonia.	  
==PAGE:46==

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
31  
• 
The	  Tank	  represents	  the	  pipes	  in	  the	  catchments.	  The	  flow	  in	  the	  pipes,	  as	  well	  as	  the	  
flow	  in	  the	  catchment,	  is	  modelled	  as	  linear	  reservoirs	  and	  the	  Tank	  has	  been	  made	  
small	  so	  that	  it	  has	  no	  influence	  on	  the	  flow.	  The	  only	  reason	  for	  the	  addition	  of	  the	  
Tank	   block	   was	   therefore	   to	   simulate	   the	   TSS	   deposition	   and	   resuspension,	   whose	  
equations	  are	  independent	  from	  the	  size	  of	  the	  Tank	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  Since	  TSS	  are	  not	  
considered	  in	  this	  study,	  this	  part	  could	  also	  be	  eliminated.	  
	  
Some	  of	  the	  parameters	  regarding	  the	  catchment	  are	  fixed	  in	  WEST	  (they	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Table	  
7)	  in	  order	  to	  limit	  the	  correlated	  parameters	  for	  the	  calibration	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  
Table	  7:	  parameters	  of	  the	  catchment	  with	  their	  fixed	  values	  and	  units.	  
Parameter	  
Value	  and	  unit	  
Total	  area	  
1450	  ha	  
Combined	  area	  
700	  ha	  
Separate	  area	  
750	  ha	  
Wastewater	  per	  person	  
110	  l/s	  
In	   order	   to	   match	   the	   day	   input	   number	   in	   WEST,	   day	   1	   is	   considered	   to	   be	   the	   date	  
01/01/2013.	  Moreover,	  in	  order	  to	  match	  the	  sensor	  data,	  the	  output	  of	  the	  model	  was	  fixed	  
at	  one	  output	  per	  minute.	  The	  choice	  of	  the	  solver	  and	  of	  the	  time	  step	  is	  very	  important,	  
being	   WEST	   a	   numerical	   model.	   Moreover,	   a	   proper	   choice	   helps	   in	   reducing	   the	  
computational	   time.	   The	   solver	   chosen	   by	   Olesen	   was	   “the	   fourth	   order	   Runge-­‐Kutta	   with	  
variable	  time	  step	  size,	  RK4ASC,	  with	  an	  initial	  time	  step	  of	  10-­‐5	  (d),	  a	  minimum	  of	  10-­‐8	  (d)	  and	  a	  
maximum	  of	  1	  (d)”.	  
	  
It	  is	  important,	  in	  order	  to	  use	  models	  for	  management	  and	  optimization	  of	  existing	  systems,	  
to	  have	  a	  certain	  degree	  of	  confidence	  in	  their	  performance.	  For	  evaluating	  the	  goodness	  of	  a	  
model	   performance	   there	   are	   different	   techniques,	   which	   depend	   on	   the	   purpose,	  
characteristics	   of	   the	   model,	   data	   and	   available	   information.	   The	   choice	   of	   the	   best	  
performance	   criterion	   is	   therefore	   case	   specific.	   Quantitative	   tools	   are	   useful	   for	   the	  
comparison	  between	  different	  models;	  in	  fact	  they	  give	  numerical	  values	  that	  can	  be	  easily	  
compared	  (Bennet	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  The	  model	  performance	  in	  this	  project	  is	  evaluated	  through	  
the	  Root	  Mean	  Square	  Error	  (RMSE)	  method,	  since	  this	  method	  allows	  for	  a	  direct	  comparison	  
between	  the	  data	  from	  the	  sensors	  and	  the	  simulated	  data	  and	  it	  “express	  the	  error	  metric	  in	  
the	   same	   units	   as	   the	   original	   data”	   (Bennet	   et	   al.,	   2013).	   For	   ammonia	   concentration,	   the	  
RMSE	  is	  calculated	  as	  follows	  (Equation	  14):	  
	  
𝑅𝑀𝑆𝐸=
1
𝑁
(𝑥! −𝑥!)!
!
!!!
	  
(14)	  
In	   the	   formula	  𝑥! 	  represents	   the	   observed	   concentration	   at	   the	   i-­‐th	   time	   step,	   while	  𝑥!	  
represents	  the	  simulated	  concentration	  for	  the	  same	  time	  step.	  For	  ammonia,	  the	  simulated	  
concentration	  was	  both	  modelled	  with	  the	  fminsearch	  method	  and	  with	  the	  modified	  Monte	  
Carlo	   method,	   so	   that	   a	   comparison	   between	   their	   RMSE	   for	   the	   different	   rain	   events	   is	  
possible	  (for	  further	  explanation	  about	  the	  calculation	  of	  the	  RMSE	  in	  the	  case	  of	   modified	  
Monte	   Carlo	   method	   see	   Chapter	   4.2.2).	   In	   this	   work	   the	   RMSE	   is	   also	   calculated	   for	   the	  
==PAGE:47==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
32  
hydraulic	  calibration	  on	  the	  flow,	  so	  that	  in	  Equation	  14	  the	  first	  term	  is	  substituted	  with	  the	  
observed	  flow	  (Qobs)	  and	  the	  second	  term	  with	  the	  fminsearch	  simulated	  flow	  (Qsim).	  The	  same	  
was	  made	  for	  the	  load	  calculation	  for	  both	  the	  traditional	  and	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  methods.	  
As	  Equation	  14	  shows,	  the	  lower	  the	  RMSE	  is,	  the	  better	  the	  model	  performance	  is	  (Bennet	  et	  
al.,	  2013).	  The	  range	  for	  the	  RMSE	  is	  [0,	  ∞],	  with	  0	  representing	  the	  perfect	  match	  between	  
two	  datasets.	  
	  
In	  this	  project,	  instead	  of	  calculating	  one	  single	  RMSE	  value	  for	  the	  whole	  dataset	  of	  727	  days	  
(from	  June	  2013	  to	  December	  2014),	  it	  has	  been	  preferred	  to	  calculate	  it	  for	  11	  different	  rain	  
events8	  and	  for	  one	  long	  term	  event.	  This	  was	  done	  because	  using	  one	  single	  value	  for	  the	  
whole	   dataset	   often	   results	   in	   completely	   divergent	   behaviours	   over	   the	   time	   or	   over	   the	  
space.	  
	  
For	   concluding,	   it	   is	   important	   to	   notice	   that,	   as	   the	   model	   outputs	   are	   not	   the	   exact	  
representation	  of	  the	  true	  state	  of	  a	  system,	  in	  the	  same	  way	  the	  sensors’	  datasets	  are	  not.	  In	  
fact,	   as	   already	   seen	   in	   the	   previous	   chapters,	   there	   are	   a	   lot	   of	   sources	   of	   errors	   and	  
uncertainties	   affecting	   sensor	   measurements.	   This	   means	   not	   only	   that	   an	   exact	   match	  
between	   sensor	   dataset	   and	   model	   outputs	   is	   almost	   impossible,	   but	   also	   that	   this	   is	   not	  
desirable	  (Bennet	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  
8	  Since	  overflow	  events	  only	  happen	  during	  wet	  weather,	  this	  work	  only	  focuses	  on	  rain	  events.	  
==PAGE:48==

33  
4. CALIBRATION	  
In	  this	  chapter	  a	  description	  of	  the	  methods	  used	  in	  the	  project	  for	  the	  parameters’	  calibration	  
is	  presented.	  In	  fact,	  two	  different	  approaches	  for	  the	  calibration	  were	  used:	  a	  traditional	  one	  
(fminsearch)	  and	  an	  uncertainty	  based	  one	  (modified	  Monte	  Carlo).	  	  
	  
“The	  majority	  of	  urban	  drainage	  models	  require	  calibrating	  prior	  to	  use”	  (Deletic,	  2012).	  The	  
aim	  of	  a	  parameter	  calibration	  technique	  is	  to	  select	  those	  parameters’	  values	  for	  which	  the	  
error	  (here	  estimated	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  RMSE	  between	  sensor	  data	  and	  model	  outputs)	  is	  
lower	  (Bennet	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  For	  doing	  this,	  it	  is	  necessary	  “to	  enable	  an	  efficient	  search	  of	  the	  
parameter	   space,	   identifying	   those	   regions	   where	   model	   performance	   is	   considered	  
satisfactory”	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  	  	  
	  
In	  order	  to	  have	  a	  higher	  efficiency	  calibration,	  a	  sensitivity	  analysis	  of	  the	  quantity	  and	  quality	  
parameters	   was	   made	   by	   Olesen	   in	   2015.	   For	   more	   information	   on	   the	   procedure	   of	   the	  
sensitivity	   analysis,	   which	   is	   based	   on	   a	   Monte	   Carlo	   approach	   with	   the	   use	   of	   1000	  
simulations,	   see	   (Olesen,	   2015).	   Here	   only	   the	   results	   are	   presented:	   they	   were	   evaluated	  
through	  a	  visual	  assessment	  thanks	  to	  the	  use	  of	  scatter	  plots	  and	  through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  linear	  
regression	   between	   the	   parameters	   and	   the	   RMSE	   (calculated	   for	   every	   one	   of	   the	   1000	  
simulations	  of	  the	  parameter	  sets),	  for	  including	  less	  subjectivity	  in	  the	  reading	  of	  the	  results.	  
The	   use	   of	   the	   scatter	   plot	   is	   particularly	   useful	   in	   visual	   assessment	   because	   it	   gives	   two	  
different	  information,	  according	  to	  the	  shape	  of	  the	  scatter	  and	  the	  colour	  of	  the	  points:	  
• 
The	  flatter	  the	  scatter	  is,	  the	  less	  sensitive	  the	  parameter	  is	  (Sokolowsky	  and	  Banks,	  
2010).	  
• 
The	  lighter	  the	  colour	  is,	  the	  higher	  the	  RMSE	  is	  (and	  therefore	  the	  more	  sensitive	  the	  
parameter	  is)	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  sensitivity	  analysis	  show	  that	  for	  the	  flow	  the	  most	  sensitive	  parameters	  are	  
k,	  the	  linear	  reservoir	  constant	  (d-­‐1)	  for	  the	  rainfall-­‐runoff	  transformation	  model,	  also	  called	  
storage	  coefficient	  of	  the	  reservoir,	  and	  f,	  the	  fraction	  of	  the	  catchment	  that	  is	  pervious,	  while	  
for	  the	  ammonia	  the	  most	  sensitive	  parameters	  are	  NH4	  sew,	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  
wastewater	   (mg/l),	   and	   NH4	   runoff,	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   in	   runoff	   (mg/l).	   Table	   8	  
summarises	  the	  parameters	  for	  which	  the	  calibration	  within	  this	  project	  was	  made.	  The	  other	  
parameters	  that	  were	  analysed	  through	  the	  sensitivity	  analysis	  did	  not	  show	  enough	  sensitivity	  
for	  being	  included	  in	  the	  calibration.	  
Table	  8:	  summary	  of	  the	  most	  sensitive	  parameters	  used	  for	  the	  parameter	  calibration.

The	  calibration	  is	  performed,	  for	  both	  the	  two	  different	  methods,	  for	  11	  single	  rain	  events	  (five	  
of	   them	   corresponding	   to	   the	   five	   considered	   measuring	   campaigns	   with	   a	   good	   level	   of	  
precision	   and	   accuracy)	   and	   for	   one	   long	   term	   event,	   which	   was	   divided	   into	   two	   shorter	  
events	  (LT1	  and	  LT2)	  for	  having	  a	  better	  representation	  of	  the	  results.	  Table	  9	  summarises	  the	  
events	  on	  which	  the	  calibration	  was	  performed.	  In	  the	  chosen	  long	  term	  event	  (60	  days)	  two	  
sensor	   calibrations	   were	   performed	   and	   this	   period	   was	   therefore	   chosen	   for	   the	   model	  
calibration	  so	  that	  it	  has	  been	  possible	  to	  analyse	  the	  response	  of	  the	  model	  for	  initial	  data	  
affected	   by	   uncertainties.	   In	   the	   work	   of	   Olesen	   (2015),	   the	   calibration	   was	   only	   made	   for	  
periods	  in	  between	  two	  different	  sensor	  calibrations.
