4.1. QUANTITY	  CALIBRATION:	  FLOW	  
The	   calibration	   of	   the	   flow	   was	   included	   in	   this	   project	   after	   the	   first	   ammonia	   calibration	  
simulation,	  since	  it	  was	  observed	  that	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  predicted	  by	  the	  model	  was	  
not	  close	  to	  the	  observed	  data	  as	  expected.	  	  In	  fact	  the	  dependence	  of	  water	  quality	  from	  
water	  quantity	  calibration	  is	  strong,	  and,	  as	  it	  is	  explained	  in	  (Zoppou,	  2001),	  “in	  most	  water	  
quality	   models	   pollutant	   concentrations	   and	   loads	   cannot	   be	   estimated	   without	   having	  
estimated	   the	   flow”.	   This	   means	   that	   water	   quality	   modelling	   is	   not	   representing	   the	   true	  
behaviour	  of	  the	  catchment	  when	  flow	  in	  not	  adequately	  modelled	  (Zoppou,	  2001).	  
	  
For	  these	  reasons,	  an	  automatic	  calibration	  of	  the	  flow	  was	  performed	  through	  the	  use	  of	  two	  
Matlab	  scripts	  (Appendix	  C	  –	  autoCalWEST	  and	  runwest_MC_opt),	  with	  the	  use	  of	  the	  function	  
fminsearch,	   which	   is	   a	   traditional	   calibration	   method.	   The	   parameters	   subjected	   to	   flow	  
calibration	  are	  the	  reservoir	  constant	  (k,	  d-­‐1)	  and	  the	  pervious	  fraction	  (f).	  The	  linear	  reservoir	  
constant	  depends	  on	  the	  size	  of	  the	  catchment,	  in	  fact	  the	  larger	  the	  catchment	  is,	  the	  higher	  
the	  k	  is.	  There	  are	  different	  formulas	  for	  the	  calculation	  of	  k.	  In	  general,	  however,	  it	  is	  included	  
in	  the	  range	  [1.00·∙10-­‐3,	  1]	  (d-­‐1).	  The	  pervious	  fraction	  instead	  has	  the	  following	  range:	  [0.30,	  
==PAGE:50==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
35  
0.99].	  When	  using	  the	  fminsearch,	  however,	  the	  range	  should	  not	  be	  imposed,	  but	  only	  the	  
starting	  point	  of	  the	  calibration.	  	  
	  
Fminsearch	  is	  a	  Matlab	  function	  for	  finding	  a	  minimum	  of	  a	  scalar	  function	  of	  several	  variables,	  
starting	   from	   an	   initial	   estimate.	   In	   other	   words,	   it	   performs	   an	   unconstrained	   nonlinear	  
optimization.	  The	  inputs	  to	  this	  function	  are	  the	  starting	  points	  (x0)	  for	  every	  parameter	  on	  
which	  the	  calibration	  is	  performed	  and	  the	  function	  that	  has	  to	  be	  optimized	  (fun),	  while	  the	  
outputs	  (x)	  are	  the	  local	  minimizer	  for	  the	  function	  described	  in	  (fun)	  (MathWorks,	  2015).	  
	  
Fminsearch	  uses	  a	  simplex	  search	  method.	  Being	  n	  the	  number	  of	  calibrated	  parameters,	  n+1	  
are	  the	  vectors	  needed	  for	  making	  up	  the	  simplex	  vertices.	  In	  a	  2	  dimensional	  space,	  a	  simplex	  
is	  a	  triangle,	  while	  in	  a	  three	  dimensional	  space	  it	  is	  a	  pyramid.	  At	  each	  iteration,	  a	  new	  point	  
inside	   or	   close	   to	   the	   simplex	   is	   generated,	   so	   that	   the	   value	   of	   the	   objective	   function	   is	  
calculated	  for	  the	  new	  simplex	  and	  compared	  to	  the	  value	  calculated	  for	  the	  previous	  simplex.	  
Generally	   the	   new	   point	   replaces	   one	   of	   the	   vertices.	   This	   procedure	   is	   repeated	   until	   a	  
specified	  tolerance	  is	  achieved	  (MathWorks,	  2015).	  As	  objective	  function,	  the	  RMSE	  was	  used,	  
but	  it	  was	  calculated	  only	  for	  flow	  values	  higher	  than	  250	  l/s	  (it	  can	  be	  seen	  within	  the	  script	  
runwest_MC_opt),	  being	  this	  project	  interested	  in	  the	  calibration	  of	  flow	  peaks	  rather	  than	  of	  
dry	  weather	  flow.	  
	  
Fminsearch,	  however,	  has	  also	  some	  important	  limitations.	  As	  it	  is	  a	  very	  simple	  approach,	  it	  
cannot	   be	   used	   for	   complicated	   nonlinear	   systems	   (Lagarias	   et	   al.,	   1998).	   In	   fact,	   with	  
nonlinear	  systems,	  fminsearch	  can	  find	  local	  minima	  instead	  of	  the	  global	  minimum	  that	  is	  the	  
object	  of	  the	  calibration	  problem.	  A	  way	  for	  overcoming	  this	  problem	  is	  to	  run	  the	  calibration	  
several	  times	  with	  different	  starting	  points,	  as	  it	  has	  been	  done	  in	  this	  project.	  	  
	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  for	  the	  parameters’	  values	  and	  for	  the	  RMSE	  values	  of	  
the	  11	  rain	  events	  and	  the	  long	  term	  event	  (subdivided	  in	  LT1	  and	  LT2)	  are	  reported	  in	  Chapter	  
6.1,	  while	  the	  graphs	  of	  the	  model	  output	  for	  the	  chosen	  parameters’	  values	  (compared	  with	  
the	  measured	  data)	  for	  the	  rain	  events	  are	  partly	  showed	  in	  Chapter	  6.1	  and	  partly	  in	  Appendix	  
E.	  
4.2. QUALITY	  CALIBRATION:	  AMMONIA	  
For	  the	  water	  quality	  calibration	  on	  ammonia	  concentration,	  two	  different	  methods	  have	  been	  
compared.	   In	   the	   following	   subchapters	   the	   two	   approaches	   are	   presented.	   The	   traditional	  
one	  (fminsearch)	  has	  already	  been	  explained	  within	  this	  work,	  while	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  one	  
(modified	  Monte	  Carlo)	  is	  here	  analysed.	  The	  reason	  why	  it	  has	  been	  decided	  to	  compare	  two	  
different	  methods	  for	  the	  quality	  part	  of	  the	  model	  is	  that	  in	  many	  studies	  (Ashley	  et	  al.,	  1999;	  
Freni	   et	   Mannina,	   2010a;	   Harremoës,	   1988;	   Willems,	   2000	   and	   2008)	   it	   has	   been	  
demonstrated	   that	   the	   uncertainties	   related	   to	   the	   water	   quality	   module	   are	   one	   order	   of	  
magnitude	   higher	   than	   the	   uncertainties	   in	   the	   water	   quantity	   module	   (Dotto	   et	   al.,	   2012;	  
Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  	  
	  
==PAGE:51==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
36  
The	  parameters	  that	  have	  been	  investigated	  within	  the	  water	  quality	  calibration	  process	  are	  
the	   ammonia	   concentration	   in	   wastewater	   (NH4	   sew)	   and	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   in	  
runoff	  (NH4	  runoff),	  both	  expressed	  in	  (mg/l).	  The	  ranges	  for	  these	  two	  parameters	  have	  been	  
defined	  accordingly	  to	  the	  literature	  review	  of	  Chapter	  2.2.1.	  For	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  
in	  wastewater	  the	  chosen	  range	  is	  [20.00,	  80.00]	  (mg/l),	  while	  for	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  
in	   runoff	   the	   range	   is	   [0.20,	   10.00]	   (mg/l).	   The	   range	   definition	   is	   useful	   only	   for	   the	  
uncertainty	  based	  approach,	  since	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method,	  as	  already	  seen	  in	  the	  
previous	  subchapter,	  does	  not	  need	  the	  definition	  of	  a	  range	  for	  the	  parameters’	  values,	  but	  it	  
only	  needs	  the	  definition	  of	  a	  starting	  point	  x0	  for	  each	  of	  the	  analysed	  parameters.	  
	  
The	   outputs	   of	   the	   two	   calibration	   methods	   are	   compared	   through	   the	   use	   of	   the	   RMSE	  
performance	  criterion.	  The	  results	  will	  be	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  
4.2.1. Traditional	  
In	  order	  to	  do	  a	  comparison	  with	  a	  different	  calibration	  method	  (uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  
through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  approach,	  which	  is	  explained	  in	  the	  subchapter	  
4.2.2)	  the	  results	  coming	  from	  an	  fminsearch	  calibration,	  performed	  by	  Olesen	  in	  2015,	  have	  
been	  used.	  The	  fminsearch	  approach	  has	  already	  been	  explained	  in	  Chapter	  4.1.	  
	  
For	   the	   eleven	   rain	   events	   analysed,	   the	   outputs	   from	   Olesen	   were	   directly	   utilized	   for	   a	  
graphical	   overview	   and	   for	   the	   RMSE	   calculation.	   For	   the	   long	   term	   event	   LT,	   and	   for	   its	  
subdivisions	  LT1	  and	  LT2,	  however,	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  was	  performed	  within	  this	  work,	  
since	  Olesen	  did	  not	  analysed	  this	  period.	  In	  fact,	  as	  she	  explained	  within	  her	  work	  (Lumped	  
conceptual	  modelling	  of	  CSO	  pollutant	  loads	  in	  Viby,	  Aahrus)	  in	  2015,	  “to	  find	  the	  period	  the	  
model	  should	  be	  calibrated	  for,	  you	  have	  to	  evaluate	  the	  data	  and	  find	  all	  the	  periods	  in	  which	  
no	  irregularities	  are	  noticed,	  in	  order	  to	  avoid	  any	  correction	  having	  been	  performed	  on	  the	  
sensor	   in	   the	   chosen	   period”.	   In	   this	   project,	   on	   the	   contrary,	   one	   of	   the	   aims	   is	   to	  
demonstrate	   that,	   through	   the	   use	   of	   an	   uncertainty	   based	   calibration	   method,	   the	   model	  
output	  can	  approximate	  the	  sensor	  data	  in	  a	  satisfactory	  way,	  with	  the	  consequence	  that	  the	  
time	  series	  for	  ammonia	  concentration	  does	  not	  have	  to	  be	  cut	  into	  separate	  events,	  even	  if	  a	  
sensor	  calibration	  is	  detected	  in	  the	  chosen	  period.	  Therefore,	  from	  a	  comparison	  between	  the	  
two	  calibration	  methods,	  the	  aim	  is	  to	  demonstrate	  that	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  approach	  gives	  
a	   better	   representation	   of	   the	   reality	   than	   the	   traditional	   approach,	   being	   the	   calibration	  
output	  of	  the	  first	  closer	  to	  the	  sensor	  data.	  
	  
The	  scripts	  for	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  on	  water	  quality,	  performed	  on	  the	  long	  term	  event,	  
can	  be	  find	  in	  Appendix	  C	  (autoCalWESTamm	  and	  runwest_MC_optAmm).	  
4.2.2. Uncertainty	  based	  
In	   Chapter	   2	   the	   importance	   and	   the	   need	   for	   an	   integrated	   approach	   in	   the	   evaluation	   of	  
water	  quality	  have	  been	  explained.	  The	  holistic	  approach	  within	  this	  thesis	  has	  been	  achieved	  
thanks	  to	  the	  use	  of	  a	  model,	  created	  with	  the	  software	  WEST.	  “However,	  integrated	  urban	  
drainage	  models	  introduce	  several	  uncertain	  factors	  that	  are	  due	  to	  the	  high	  complexity	  level	  
of	  the	  adopted	  approaches	  as	  well	  as	  to	  the	  lack	  of	  data	  for	  a	  robust	  model	  calibration”	  (Freni	  
et	  al.,	  2009).	  In	  fact,	  complexity	  in	  models	  leads	  to	  an	  increase	  in	  the	  number	  of	  parameters	  
==PAGE:52==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
37  
that,	   as	   a	   consequence,	   decrease	   the	   error	   between	   measured	   and	   modelled	   data,	   but	  
increase	  the	  uncertainty	  linked	  to	  the	  model	  output	  (Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  Moreover,	  also	  the	  low	  
precision	  of	  field	  data	  on	  water	  quality,	  whose	  uncertainty	  has	  been	  estimated	  in	  the	  range	  of	  
30%,	  leads	  to	  over-­‐parameterization	  problems.	  The	  result	  is	  that	  the	  reliability	  in	  the	  model	  
outputs	   is	   reduced	   (Freni	   et	   al.,	   2009).	   Therefore,	   a	   measure	   of	   the	   significance	   and	   of	   the	  
accuracy	  of	  the	  results	  should	  always	  be	  presented	  when	  using	  urban	  drainage	  models.	  
	  
Uncertainties	  are	  intrinsic	  in	  every	  model	  and	  they	  derive	  from	  many	  different	  sources.	  Their	  
elimination	   is	   not	   possible,	   but	   a	   measure	   of	   their	   amplitude	   must	   be	   given	   (Deletic	   et	   al.,	  
2012).	   Uncertainty	   analysis	   is	   the	   procedure	   used	   for	   quantifying	   uncertainties	   in	   the	  
modelling	   results	   (Zoppou,	   2001).	   This	   quantification	   is	   not	   always	   elementary,	   since	   the	  
different	   uncertainty	   sources	   are	   internally	   linked	   and	   the	   measure	   of	   their	   impacts	  
propagates	  simultaneously	  through	  the	  model	  (Deletic	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  In	  general,	  when	  building	  
up	  a	  model,	  three	  sources	  of	  uncertainties	  are	  considered:	  
• 
Input	  uncertainties:	  those	  on	  the	  model	  input	  variables	  and	  data	  (both	  random	  and	  
systematic	  errors	  have	  to	  be	  considered	  but	  also	  the	  overall	  quality	  of	  the	  monitoring	  
program).	  The	  impact	  of	  input	  data	  uncertainties	  on	  model	  outputs	  is	  so	  far	  poorly	  
understood	  (Deletic,	  2012).	  Xu	  et	  al.	  (2006)	  have	  demonstrated	  that	  the	  quality	  of	  the	  
precipitation	  data	  (input	  data	  in	  the	  WEST	  model)	  influences	  both	  simulation	  errors	  
and	  calibrated	  model	  parameters	  (Jin	  et	  al.,	  2010).	  	  
• 
Parameter	   uncertainties:	   those	   on	   parameter	   values,	   which	   are	   connected	   with	   the	  
chosen	  calibration	  algorithm	  and	  with	  the	  selected	  objective	  function	  (Deletic	  et	  al.,	  
2012).	  
• 
Model	  structure	  uncertainties:	  those	  coming	  from	  an	  imperfect	  physical	  description	  of	  
the	  model	  (Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  
	  
When	  dealing	  with	  complex	  modelling	  approaches,	  traditional	  calibration	  often	  gives	  several	  
equally	  plausible	  parameter	  sets	  as	  results,	  fact	  that	  decreases	  the	  confidence	  in	  the	  obtained	  
outputs	  (Deletic	  et	  al.,	  2012;	  Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  Therefore,	  the	  theory	  of	  the	  uniqueness	  of	  a	  
parameter	  set	  which	  give	  the	  best	  fit	  between	  measured	  and	  modelled	  data	  can	  be	  replaced	  
with	   the	   concept	   of	   equifinality	   (Beven,	   2006).	   According	   to	   this	   theory,	   there	   are	   many	  
parameter	  sets	  able	  to	  give	  an	  equally	  good	  fit	  between	  simulated	  and	  measured	  data	  (Beven,	  
2006;	  Deletic	  et	  al.,	  2012;	  Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  Moreover,	  within	  this	  approach,	  the	  concept	  of	  
model	  calibration	  is	  substituted	  with	  the	  one	  of	  likelihood	  analysis	  between	  the	  two	  kinds	  of	  
data,	   while	   the	   efficiency	   of	   the	   chosen	   parameter	   set	   is	   replaced	   with	   the	   concept	   of	  
uncertainty	   analysis	   of	   the	   model	   (Freni	   et	   al.,	   2009).	   In	   fact,	   being	   every	   model	   plenty	   of	  
uncertainty	   sources	   that	   cannot	   be	   eliminated,	   it	   is	   obvious	   that	   the	   modelled	   catchment	  
cannot	   fully	   represent	   the	   complexity	   of	   the	   natural	   catchment	   and	   it	   this	   therefore,	   to	   a	  
certain	  degree,	  “wrong”	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  Consequently,	  the	  aim	  of	  the	  calibration	  
is	  to	  find	  different	  sets	  of	  parameters,	  which	  give	  a	  good	  approximation	  of	  “some	  aspects	  of	  
true	  catchment	  behaviour	  under	  some	  circumstances”	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  
	  
In	  this	  work,	  thanks	  to	  the	  use	  of	  a	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method,	  a	  high	  number	  of	  parameter	  
sets,	   that	   have	   the	   same	   likelihood	   of	   being	   accepted,	   are	   generated	   through	   a	   uniform	  
==PAGE:53==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
38  
sampling	  of	  the	  space	  defined	  by	  the	  parameter	  ranges	  (which	  are	  imposed	  by	  the	  modeller)	  
(Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  The	  model	  then	  processes	  each	  parameter	  set	  and	  the	  output	  is	  compared	  
with	  the	  measured	  dataset.	  A	  likelihood	  measure	  is	  then	  associated	  to	  every	  parameter	  set	  
through	   the	   use	   of	   a	   performance	   criterion	   (RMSE),	   and	   those	   parameter	   sets	   that	   do	   not	  
reach	  a	  minimum	  likelihood	  threshold	  are	  classified	  as	  “non-­‐behavioural”	  and	  hence	  rejected.	  
On	  the	  other	  hand,	  the	  parameter	  sets	  that	  reach	  the	  threshold	  are	  classified	  as	  “behavioural”	  
and	  their	  likelihood	  weights	  are	  rescaled	  so	  that	  their	  cumulative	  sum	  gives	  one	  as	  a	  result	  
(Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  The	   uncertainty	   based	   procedure	   therefore	   transforms	   the	   search	   for	   a	  
single	   parameter	   set	   that	   minimizes	   the	   problem	   (typical	   of	   the	   traditional	   calibration	  
methods)	  into	  the	  search	  for	  different	  parameter	  sets	  that	  are	  evaluated	  through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  
likelihood	  measure.	  This	  is	  done	  according	  to	  the	  belief	  that	  one	  single	  parameter	  set	  cannot	  
represent	  the	  observed	  reality,	  given	  the	  different	  sources	  of	  errors	  that	  affect	  a	  modelling	  
problem	  (Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  
	  
The	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  utilised	  in	  this	  work	  can	  be	  classified	  as	  a	  pseudo-­‐Bayesian	  
approach,	   since	   it	   is	   based	   on	   an	   informal	   likelihood	   measure	   for	   the	   determination	   of	   a	  
“behaviorability	  threshold”,	  because	  of	  the	  impossibility	  of	  creating	  a	  true	  model,	  due	  to	  the	  
different	  intrinsic	  error	  sources	  of	  a	  model	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009).	  One	  limitation	  of	  the	  
formal	  approach,	  in	  fact,	  is	  that	  it	  considers	  the	  model	  structure	  to	  be	  correct,	  and	  therefore	  it	  
does	   not	   consider	   the	   case	   in	   which	   the	   parameters	   compensate	   for	   the	   weakness	   of	   the	  
model	  structure	  (McMillan	  and	  Clark,	  2009),	  which	  is	  indeed	  the	  case	  of	  this	  study,	  as	  it	  can	  be	  
seen	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  	  
	  
The	  chosen	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  approach	  differs	  from	  other	  Monte	  Carlo	  methods	  since	  “it	  
reduces	   the	   subjectivity	   inherent	   in	   typical	   uncertainty	   approaches	   (e.g.	   cut-­‐off	   threshold)”	  
(Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013)	  and	  it	  evaluates	  the	  performance	  of	  the	  model	  by	  taking	  into	  account	  the	  
uncertainty	  in	  each	  measured	  datapoint	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  This	  allows	  for	  a	  comparison	  
between	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  and	  the	  uncertainty	  related	  to	  the	  measured	  dataset	  
through	  the	  use	  of	  an	  intersection	  measure	  λ	  (%)	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  
	  
Hereafter	  a	  detailed	  description	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  utilised	  for	  the	  ammonia	  
calibration	  is	  given.	  The	  explanation	  is	  subdivided	  in	  8	  steps,	  based	  on	  the	  steps	  developed	  by	  
Vezzaro	   et	   al.	   in	   2013	   (Urban	   drainage	   models	   –	   Simplifying	   uncertainty	   analysis	   for	  
practitioners).	  The	  same	  steps	  are	  used	  in	  the	  Matlab	  scripts	  coverage_analysisMaxAmm	  and	  
coverage_analysis	  that	  can	  be	  consulted	  in	  Appendix	  C.	  A	  graphical	  overview	  of	  the	  8	  steps	  of	  
the	  process	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  14.	  
• 
Step	  1	  –	  Generate	  N	  parameter	  sets	  
First	  of	  all,	  the	  prior	  distribution	  (ranges)	  for	  the	  investigated	  parameters	  should	  be	  
defined.	  The	  chosen	  ranges	  for	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  wastewater	  and	  ammonia	  
concentration	   in	   runoff	   are	   those	   given	   in	   Chapter	   4.2.	   Then	   N	   parameter	   sets	   are	  
generated.	  For	  this	  study	  N	  has	  been	  set	  equal	  to	  1000.	   
	  
The	  number	  of	  simulations	  was	  chosen	  according	  to	  the	  procedure	  described	  in	  (Dotto	  
et	  al.,	  2012),	  and	  explained	  later	  in	  this	  work.	  Also	  the	  computational	  time	  should	  be	  
==PAGE:54==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
39  
taken	  into	  account	  in	  the	  decision	  of	  the	  simulations’	  number,	  since	  it	  does	  not	  have	  to	  
be	   too	   long.	   However,	   the	   number	   should	   be	   large	   enough	   to	   ensure	   a	   complete	  
exploration	  of	  the	  parameter	  space.	  To	  find	  the	  parameters’	  values	  within	  the	  given	  
range	   a	   uniform	   distribution	   sampling	   method	   was	   chosen,	   the	   latin	   hypercube.	  
Therefore,	  with	  this	  function,	  a	  sample	  (parSample),	  containing	  nSim	  values	  (1000)	  for	  
each	  of	  the	  parameters,	  is	  generated.	  The	  distribution	  for	  each	  parameter	  is	  divided	  
into	   sections	   with	   equal	   probability:	   (0,	   1/n),	   (1/n,	   2/n),	   …,	   (1-­‐1/n,	   1),	   so	   that	   one	  
random	  number	  is	  selected	  from	  each	  section.	  After	  that	  the	  section	  is	  excluded	  from	  
the	  analysis	  (Hammonds	  et	  al.,	  1994).	  
• 
Step	  2	  –	  Run	  the	  model	  with	  the	  first	  parameter	  set	  and	  evaluate	  the	  performance	  
The	  performance	  is	  evaluated	  through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  performance	  criterion,	  which,	  in	  
this	   case,	   is	   the	   RMSE.	   This	   criterion	   was	   chosen	   because	   it	   allows	   a	   comparison	  
between	  the	  measured	  and	  the	  modelled	  data.	  
• 
Step	  3	  –	  Run	  the	  model	  for	  the	  remaining	  N-­‐1	  parameter	  sets	  
Step	  2	  is	  repeated	  for	  the	  remaining	  N-­‐1	  parameter	  sets;	  in	  this	  case	  study	  the	  number	  
of	  parameter	  sets	  was	  fixed	  at	  1000.	  A	  value	  of	  the	  performance	  criterion	  (RMSE)	  is	  
calculated	  for	  each	  set	  of	  parameters.	  	  
• 
Step	  4	  –	  Define	  uncertainty	  intervals	  for	  each	  observed	  datapoint	  
For	   each	   of	   the	   observed	   datapoints	   an	   uncertainty	   of	   ±	   30%	   is	   added,	   in	   order	   to	  
include,	  in	  the	  evaluation	  of	  the	  performance	  of	  the	  model,	  the	  uncertainty	  related	  to	  
measured	  data.	  The	  measure	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  has	  been	  derived	  from	  the	  article	  of	  
(Freni	  at	  al.,	  2009)	  (Uncertainty	  assessment	  of	  an	  integrated	  urban	  drainage	  model),	  in	  
which	  it	  is	  specified	  that	  field	  data	  have	  generally	  a	  poor	  quality	  and	  their	  uncertainty	  
is	  in	  the	  range	  of	  ±	  30%.	  	  
• 
Step	  5	  –	  Rank	  the	  N	  simulations	  and	  then	  estimate	  prediction	  bounds	  for	  the	  first	  
two	  sets	  
A	  ranking	  with	  all	  the	  performance	  criterion	  values	  for	  every	  evaluated	  set	  is	  created,	  
from	   the	   best	   to	   the	   worst	   one,	   so	   that	   k=1	   is	   the	   parameter	   set	   for	   which	   the	  
simulated	  data	  are	  the	  closest	  to	  the	  measured	  ones	  (it	  has	  the	  lowest	  RMSE)	  and	  k=N	  
is	  the	  one	  for	  which	  the	  simulated	  data	  are	  the	  farthest	  from	  the	  measured	  ones	  (it	  
has	  the	  highest	  RMSE)	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  Then	  the	  best	  two	  model	  simulations	  are	  
selected	  and	  the	  prediction	  bounds	  at	  each	  time	  step	  j	  are	  created.	  
• 
Step	  6	  –	  Estimate	  intersection	  
The	   intersection	   λ	   (%)	   between	   the	   model	   bounds	   (simmin	   and	   simmax)	   and	   the	  
observation	   error	   interval	   (defined	   by	   obsmin	   and	   obsmax)	   is	   estimated	   through	   the	  
following	  Equation	  15:	  
	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  𝜆=
!
!
𝐼!
!
!!!
	  
𝑤ℎ𝑒𝑟𝑒:	  
          𝐼! = 0  𝑓𝑜𝑟  𝑜𝑏𝑠!"# > 𝑠𝑖𝑚!"#  𝑜𝑟  𝑜𝑏𝑠!"# > 𝑠𝑖𝑚!"#	  
            𝐼! = 1  𝑓𝑜𝑟  𝑜𝑏𝑠!"# ≤𝑠𝑖𝑚!"#  𝑎𝑛𝑑  𝑜𝑏𝑠!"# ≥𝑠𝑖𝑚!"#	  
(15)	  
==PAGE:55==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
40  
where	  J	  is	  the	  number	  of	  observations	  and	  the	  value	  of	  Ij	  indicates	  whether	  the	  model	  
prediction	   bounds	   for	   the	   j-­‐th	   observation	   are	   overlapping	   the	   observation	   error	  
interval	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  
	  
Through	  step	  6	  is	  therefore	  possible	  to	  include	  the	  uncertainty	  in	  observational	  data	  
within	  the	  uncertainty	  analysis	  of	  the	  model.	  
• 
Step	  7	  –	  Evaluation	  of	  all	  the	  other	  candidates	  
Steps	  5	  and	  6	  are	  repeated	  for	  the	  different	  values	  of	  k.	  The	  first	  case	  examined	  in	  
steps	   5	   and	   6	   was	   considering	   just	   the	   two	   best	   parameter	   sets	   (i.e.	   k=2)	   and	   an	  
estimate	  of	  λ	  was	  given,	  then	  the	  second	  case	  will	  include	  the	  top	  three	  parameter	  
sets	  (i.e.	  k=3)	  and	  the	  value	  of	  the	  intersection	  λ	  will	  be	  given	  and	  so	  on	  until	  all	  the	  
parameter	   sets	   (k=N)	   will	   be	   included	   and	   a	   final	   estimate	   of	   λ	   will	   be	   provided	  
(Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  Ideally,	  a	  coverage	  of	  100%	  between	  simulated	  and	  observed	  
data	   should	   be	   experienced,	   but	   this	   is	   never	   observed	   because	   of	   the	   different	  
uncertainty	  sources	  affecting	  the	  problem	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  
	  
At	   this	   point	   a	   plot	   of	   λ	   against	   the	   number	   of	   simulations	   can	   be	   useful	   for	  
understanding	   how	   many	   simulations	   should	   be	   included	   in	   the	   construction	   of	   the	  
model	   prediction	   bounds.	   In	   fact,	   the	   plot	   should	   show	   a	   clear	   peak	   or	   at	   least	   a	  
decreasing	  behaviour	  after	  a	  certain	  simulation	  number,	  so	  that	  the	  user	  can	  define	  
the	  acceptance	  threshold	  γ	  in	  an	  objective	  way	  (informal	  likelihood	  approach)	  (Vezzaro	  
et	  al.,	  2013).	  This	  step	  represents	  the	  biggest	  advantage	  of	  this	  procedure	  compared	  to	  
other	  procedures	  in	  which	  the	  threshold	  is	  defined	  a	  priori	  by	  the	  user,	  since	  a	  more	  
tangible	  definition	  of	  the	  threshold	  is	  possible	  (Vezzaro	  et	  al.,	  2013).	  In	  fact,	  through	  
this	  approach,	  the	  threshold	  is	  set	  in	  such	  a	  way	  that	  it	  represents	  the	  best	  possible	  
cover	  between	  the	  observation	  error	  interval	  and	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds.	  The	  
graphs	  of	  the	  plot	  of	  λ	  against	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  for	  the	  chosen	  events	  are	  
reported	  partly	  in	  Chapter	  6.2	  and	  partly	  in	  Appendix	  F,	  while	  the	  defined	  threshold	  
for	  each	  of	  the	  chosen	  events	  is	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  Once	  it	  has	  been	  defined	  for	  
all	  the	  rain	  events	  under	  observation,	  the	  threshold	  should	  be	  given	  as	  an	  input	  in	  the	  
script	  coverage_analysis	  for	  the	  calculation	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  (Step	  8). 
• 
Step	  8	  –	  Estimate	  bounds	  until	  λ	  equals	  γ;	  create	  parameter	  distributions	  
At	  this	  point,	  the	  5th	  and	  95th	  model	  prediction	  bounds,	  with	  the	  k	  simulations	  needed	  
by	  λ	  for	  reaching	  the	  acceptance	  threshold	  γ,	  are	  created.	  The	  behavioural	  parameter	  
sets	  selected	  for	  the	  creation	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  are	  saved	  in	  the	  Matlab	  
Workspace	  (with	  the	  name	  behPar),	  so	  that	  they	  can	  be	  used	  for	  the	  plot	  against	  the	  
RMSE	  values	  in	  order	  to	  investigate	  the	  uncertainty	  of	  the	  model	  parameters	  and	  the	  
possible	  interaction	  between	  different	  parameters.	  
	  
The	  final	  graph	  is	  then	  given	  thanks	  to	  the	  script	  OverlappingPlot	  that	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  
Appendix	   C.	   The	   graphs	   of	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   (with	   the	   outputs	   for	   both	   the	  
approaches)	  are	  presented	  partly	  in	  Chapter	  6.2	  and	  partly	  in	  Appendix	  F.	  
==PAGE:56==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
41  
	  
Figure	  14:	  graphical	  overview	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method	  through	  an	  8	  steps	  subdivision	  (from	  Vezzaro	  et	  
al.,	  2013).	  
The	  number	  of	  simulations	  was	  chosen	  according	  to	  the	  procedure	  described	  in	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  
2012).	  The	  uncertainty	  analysis	  was	  performed	  with	  a	  variable	  number	  of	  simulations	  (in	  this	  
project:	   50,	   100,	   250,	   500,	   750,	   1000).	   At	   each	   step,	   the	   ‘intersection	   λ	   (%)	   -­‐	   number	   of	  
simulation’	  graph	  was	  compared	  with	  the	  one	  obtained	  in	  the	  previous	  step.	  The	  final	  number	  
of	  simulations	  was	  obtained	  when,	  between	  two	  consecutive	  graphs,	  the	  difference	  was	  not	  
appreciable.	  In	  this	  way,	  a	  complete	  exploration	  of	  the	  parameter	  space	  is	  ensured	  (Vezzaro	  et	  
al.,	  2013).	  An	  example	  for	  the	  first	  rain	  event	  analysed	  in	  this	  project	  (Measuring	  Campaign	  3)	  
is	  given	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  
	  
As	  already	  explained	  in	  this	  work,	  the	  chosen	  performance	  criterion	  is	  the	  RMSE	  (the	  formula	  
can	   be	   seen	   in	   Equation	   14).	   For	   the	   water	   quality	   module,	   it	   was	   calculated	   for	   both	   the	  
traditional	   calibration	   method	   (fminsearch)	   and	   for	   the	   uncertainty	   based	   one	   (modified	  
Monte	  Carlo).	  In	  the	  first	  case	  the	  error	  is	  evaluated	  as	  the	  distance	  between	  the	  observed	  
data	  and	  the	  output	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration,	  while	  in	  the	  second	  case	  the	  error	  is	  the	  
distance	   between	   the	   observed	   data	   and	   the	   median	   of	   the	   model	   prediction	   bounds.	   The	  
reason	  for	  using	  the	  median	  rather	  than	  the	  average	  lies	  in	  the	  will	  of	  reducing	  the	  effect	  of	  
possible	  outlier	  parameter	  values	  (Olesen,	  2015).	  The	  scripts	  for	  the	  calculation	  of	  the	  RMSE	  
==PAGE:57==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
42  
with	  the	  2	  different	  methods	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  C	  with	  the	  names	  Comparison1	  (for	  the	  
fminsearch	  method)	  and	  Comparison2	  (for	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method)	  for	  the	  first	  rain	  
event	  analysed	  (MC3).	  
	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  RMSE	  calculation	  for	  both	  the	  methods	  are	  showed	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  	  
	  
The	   behavioural	   parameters	   for	   both	   parameter	   1	   and	   2	   (ammonia	   concentration	   in	  
wastewater	  and	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  runoff)	  obtained	  in	  Step	  8	  are	  plotted	  against	  the	  
RMSE,	  and	  also	  the	  scatter	  plotmatrix	  between	  the	  behavioural	  parameters	  and	  the	  RMSE	  was	  
made.	  These	  graphs	  are	  given	  partly	  in	  Chapter	  6.2	  and	  partly	  in	  Appendix	  F.	  Moreover,	  a	  table	  
containing	  the	  number	  of	  behavioural	  parameters,	  the	  minimum	  behavioural	  parameter	  set,	  
the	  maximum	  one	  and	  the	  median	  one	  is	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.	  
4.2.3. Uncertainty	  based	  with	  a	  different	  intersection	  method	  
The	   intersection	   λ	   (%)	   between	   the	   model	   prediction	   bounds	   (simmin	   and	   simmax)	   and	   the	  
observation	  error	  interval	  (defined	  by	  obsmin	  and	  obsmax)	  with	  the	  estimation	  method	  explained	  
in	  Step	  6	  of	  the	  previous	  Chapter	  4.2.2	  (through	  the	  use	  of	  Equation	  15)	  can	  be	  considered	  to	  
not	  represent	  the	  reality	  in	  a	  satisfactory	  way.	  In	  fact	  the	  intersection	  value	  is	  set	  equal	  to	  1	  
even	   when	   a	   partial	   intersection	   between	   the	   bounds	   is	   observed.	   This	   can	   lead	   to	   an	  
extremely	  lax	  evaluation	  of	  the	  simulated	  ammonia	  concentration	  compared	  to	  the	  measured	  
data.	  In	  order	  to	  verify	  whether	  this	  is	  true	  or	  not,	  the	  ammonia	  calibration	  through	  the	  use	  of	  
the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   method	   was	   repeated	   for	   a	   second	   time,	   with	   a	   changed	  
intersection	  criterion,	  on	  two	  rain	  events	  (MC3	  and	  RF1).	  With	  the	  use	  of	  this	  different	  method	  
for	  the	  intersection	  estimation,	  the	  score	  is	  calculated	  as:	  
• 
1	  -­‐	  only	  when	  there	  is	  complete	  intersection,	  i.e.	  both	  the	  model	  intersection	  bounds	  
(simmin	   and	   simmax)	   are	   within	   the	   observation	   error	   interval	   (defined	   by	   obsmin	   and	  
obsmax).	  
• 
a	  value	  between	  0	  and	  1	  -­‐	  when	  only	  one	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  is	  within	  the	  
observation	  error	  interval.	  	  
• 
0	  -­‐	  when	  both	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  are	  outside	  the	  observation	  error	  interval,	  
i.e.	  there	  is	  no	  intersection.	  
This	  can	  be	  done	  by	  replacing	  the	  script	  Intersection	  with	  the	  script	  Intersection1	  (presented	  in	  
Appendix	   C),	   which	   has	   to	   be	   called	   by	   the	   scripts	   coverage_analysisMaxAmm	   and	  
coverage_analysis.	  	  
 
Therefore,	  by	  giving	  a	  value	  lower	  than	  1	  for	  the	  partial	  intersection	  between	  model	  prediction	  
bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   a	   stricter	   condition	   is	   posed.	   All	   the	   results	   are	  
presented	  and	  discussed	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.1.

5. LOAD	  CALCULATION	  
The	  ammonia	  loads	  are	  then	  calculated	  for	  the	  analysed	  events	  for	  the	  measured	  data,	  for	  the	  
simulated	   data	   coming	   from	   the	   fminsearch	   calibration	   and	   for	   the	   simulated	   data	   coming	  
from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration,	  in	  order	  to	  do	  a	  comparison	  between	  the	  resulting	  
loads.	  Equation	  16	  has	  been	  used	  for	  the	  calculation	  of	  the	  loads	  in	  the	  three	  cases:	  
	  
𝐿𝑜𝑎𝑑   𝑚𝑔= 𝐶𝑜𝑛𝑐!"!   𝑚𝑔
𝑙
∗𝑄!!
𝑙
𝑚𝑖𝑛∗1𝑚𝑖𝑛	  
(16)	  
where	  1	  minute	  is	  the	  time	  step	  between	  each	  sensor	  measurement.	  A	  summation	  over	  every	  
minute	  of	  the	  event	  is	  then	  made	  in	  order	  to	  have	  the	  total	  load	  concerning	  the	  considered	  
event.	  	  
• 
The	  calculation	  of	  the	  loads	  for	  the	  measured	  data	  is	  presented	  in	  Appendix	  D	  through	  
the	   script	   LoadCalcDataMeasured.	   In	   order	   to	   be	   coherent	   with	   the	   calibration	  
approach,	  an	  uncertainty	  of	  ±	  30%	  is	  applied	  to	  the	  total	  load.	  The	  results	  for	  every	  
event	  for	  the	  total	  load	  and	  for	  the	  ±	  30%	  of	  the	  total	  load	  are	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  
6.3.	  
• 
The	   calculation	   of	   the	   loads	   for	   the	   simulated	   data	   coming	   from	   the	   fminsearch	  
calibration	  is	  made	  by	  simply	  applying	  the	  formula	  of	  Equation	  16	  at	  each	  time	  step	  of	  
the	  event	  under	  consideration.	  A	  summation	  over	  every	  minute	  of	  the	  event	  is	  then	  
performed.	  The	  RMSE	  is	  also	  calculated	  as	  the	  distance	  between	  the	  total	  load	  coming	  
from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  and	  the	  total	  load	  coming	  from	  measured	  data.	  The	  
results	  are	  summarised	  in	  Chapter	  6.3.	  
• 
The	  calculation	  of	  the	  loads	  for	  the	  simulated	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  
Carlo	  calibration	  is	  showed	  in	  Appendix	  D	  through	  the	  script	  LoadCalcModMC.	  In	  order	  
to	   be	   coherent	   with	   the	   calibration	   method,	   the	   load	   is	   calculated	   for	   each	   of	   the	  
behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  selected	  and	  the	  results	  are	  plotted	  in	  Matlab	  through	  the	  
function	  boxplot.	  The	  median	  is	  then	  calculated,	  together	  with	  the	  minimum	  and	  the	  
maximum	  of	  the	  load	  resulting	  from	  the	  selected	  parameter	  sets.	  The	  RMSE	  is	  also	  
calculated	  as	  the	  distance	  between	  the	  median	  value	  and	  the	  total	  load	  coming	  from	  
measured	  data.	  The	  results	  are	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  6.3.	  
A	   graphical	   comparison	   between	   the	   total	   load	   coming	   from	   measured	   data	   and	   the	   total	  
loads	   coming	   from	   the	   two	   calibration	   methods	   is	   given	   partly	   in	   Chapter	   6.3	   and	   partly	   in	  
Appendix	  G.

6. RESULTS	  AND	  DISCUSSION	  
In	   this	   chapter	   the	   results	   are	   presented	   and	   discussed.	   The	   subdivision	   of	   the	   results’	  
presentation	  will	  follow	  the	  outline	  of	  Chapter	  5,	  so	  that	  first	  the	  results	  of	  the	  flow	  calibration	  
are	  illustrated,	  then	  the	  ones	  of	  the	  quality	  calibration	  for	  the	  two	  different	  methods	  and	  at	  
the	   end	   the	   ones	   of	   the	   load	   calculation.	   In	   order	   not	   to	   be	   repetitive,	   only	   the	   most	  
representative	   results	   are	   here	   shown,	   while	   the	   others	   are	   reported	   in	   the	   Appendices	  
(Appendix	  E,	  Appendix	  F,	  Appendix	  G).	  
6.1. QUANTITY	  CALIBRATION:	  FLOW	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  flow	  calibration	  (performed	  through	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method)	  are	  
here	  presented.	  Table	  10	  summarizes	  the	  values	  obtained	  for	  the	  two	  investigated	  parameters	  
and	  the	  values	  of	  the	  RMSE	  calculated	  between	  the	  measured	  and	  the	  simulated	  data	  for	  the	  
11	  rain	  events	  and	  for	  the	  long	  term	  event	  (further	  subdivided	  into	  two	  long	  term	  events	  LT1	  
and	  LT2).	  Based	  on	  the	  sensitivity	  analysis	  performed	  by	  Olesen	  in	  2015,	  the	  two	  investigated	  
parameters	  for	  the	  quantity	  calibration	  are	  k,	  linear	  reservoir	  constant	  (d-­‐1),	  and	  f,	  the	  fraction	  
of	  the	  catchment	  that	  is	  pervious.	  The	  RMSE	  is	  calculated	  only	  for	  flows	  grater	  than	  250	  l/s,	  in	  
order	  to	  have	  a	  better	  approximation	  of	  peak	  events.

parameters	  are	  presented,	  together	  with	  the	  RMSE	  value.

DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
46  
Generally	  it	  can	  be	  affirmed	  that	  the	  calibration	  is	  quite	  good,	  since	  the	  model	  calibrates	  the	  
flow	  in	  an	  accurate	  way.	  However,	  some	  specifications	  should	  be	  done.	  In	  fact	  the	  system	  is	  
strongly	  influenced	  by	  the	  pumping	  activity,	  which	  starts	  after	  that	  the	  maximum	  capacity	  of	  
the	  WWTP	  (1260	  l/s)	  is	  reached.	  The	  model	  cannot	  reproduce	  the	  activity	  of	  the	  pump	  so	  that	  
often	  the	  flow	  coming	  from	  the	  model	  is	  underestimated.	  This	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Figure	  17	  around	  
the	  day	  30/08/14.	  Another	  consideration	  can	  be	  made	  from	  the	  observation	  of	  Figure	  15,	  in	  
which	  there	  is	  a	  clear	  peak	  at	  the	  beginning	  of	  the	  rain	  event,	  followed	  by	  a	  stagnant	  peak	  that	  
reproduces	  the	  action	  of	  the	  pump.	  Being	  the	  model	  calibrated	  for	  obtaining	  the	  lowest	  RMSE,	  
the	  simulated	  output	  reproduces	  the	  overall	  peak,	  through	  the	  use	  of	  a	  higher	  k,	  which	  gives	  a	  
wider	  peak	  that	  includes	  the	  stagnant	  one.	  The	  last	  debate	  concerns	  the	  presence	  of	  different	  
peaks	  given	  by	  different	  rain	  events,	  as	  the	  case	  of	  Figure	  16.	  The	  model	  tends	  to	  overestimate	  
the	  first	  peak	  and	  underestimate	  the	  following	  ones.	  This	  can	  be	  explained	  by	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  
reservoir	  constant	  should	  be	  able	  to	  simulate	  different	  rain	  events	  in	  a	  linear	  system,	  which	  is	  
never	  the	  case	  of	  the	  reality.	  Another	  explanation	  for	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  second	  and	  the	  third	  
peaks	   given	   by	   the	   measured	   data	   are	   higher	   than	   the	   modelled	   ones	   could	   be	   that	   the	  
pervious	  area,	  after	  being	  saturated,	  starts	  to	  act	  like	  an	  impervious	  area,	  contributing	  to	  the	  
runoff.	  In	  the	  WEST	  model,	  on	  the	  other	  hand,	  this	  has	  not	  been	  considered.	  
6.2. QUALITY	  CALIBRATION:	  AMMONIA	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  ammonia	  calibration	  are	  given	  in	  this	  section.	  
	  
The	  outputs	  for	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  have	  been	  taken	  from	  the	  project	  of	  (Olesen,	  2015),	  
except	  for	  the	  ones	  of	  the	  long	  term	  events	  (LT,	  LT1,	  LT2),	  for	  which	  the	  calibration	  has	  been	  
performed	  within	  this	  thesis.	  These	  outputs	  are	  then	  compared	  with	  the	  ones	  coming	  from	  the	  
modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method,	  both	  graphically	  and	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  RMSE.	  	  
	  
The	   order	   of	   presentation	   of	   the	   results	   for	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	   method	  
follows	  the	  scheme	  illustrated	  in	  Chapter	  4.2.2.	  A	  brief	  summary	  of	  the	  calibration	  procedure	  is	  
here	   presented	   so	   that	   the	   reader	   can	   better	   understand	   the	   order	   of	   presentation	   of	   the	  
results.	  The	  first	  step	  (1)	  of	  the	  procedure	  is	  to	  generate	  N	  parameters’	  sets	  (in	  this	  case	  the	  
selected	  number	  of	  simulations,	  and	  therefore	  the	  number	  of	  parameters’	  sets,	  is	  1000).	  Then	  
the	  model	  has	  been	  run	  for	  all	  the	  N	  parameters	  sets	  (2	  and	  3)	  and	  a	  value	  of	  the	  RMSE	  is	  
given	  for	  each	  one	  of	  the	  simulations.	  At	  this	  point	  the	  uncertainty	  bounds	  of	  the	  measured	  
data	   are	   defined	   (±	   30%	   on	   each	   datapoint)	   (4).	   The	   N	   simulations	   are	   then	   sorted	   in	  
ascending	  order	  according	  to	  their	  RMSE	  value	  and	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  for	  the	  two	  
best	  candidates	  are	  calculated	  at	  each	  time	  step	  (5),	  so	  that	  the	  intersection	  between	  them	  
and	   the	   observation	   error	   interval	   can	   be	   estimated	   (6).	   Step	   (5)	   and	   step	   (6)	   are	   then	  
repeated	  for	  all	  the	  remaining	  (N-­‐1)	  candidates,	  so	  that	  the	  5th	  and	  95th	  prediction	  bounds	  are	  
created	  and	  a	  measure	  of	  the	  intersection	  can	  be	  given	  for	  all	  the	  candidates	  (7).	  At	  this	  point	  
the	  script	  coverage_analysisMaxAmm	  produces	  a	  plot	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  against	  
the	  intersection	  percentage	  λ	  (%).	  The	  plot	  is	  useful	  for	  understanding	  how	  many	  simulations	  
should	  be	  included	  in	  the	  creation	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds,	  since	  it	  generally	  should	  
show	   a	   clear	   peak,	   which	   can	   be	   used	   as	   an	   acceptance	   threshold	   (γ).	   In	   this	   way	   a	   more	  
tangible	   definition	   of	   the	   threshold	   is	   given,	   that	   represents	   the	   best	   possible	   coverage	  
==PAGE:62==

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
47  
between	   model	   prediction	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval.	   The	   threshold	   definition	  
leads	   to	   the	   determination	   of	   the	   parameters’	   sets	   to	   be	   included	   in	   the	   model	   prediction	  
bounds	  construction.	  In	  some	  cases,	  however,	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  
does	  not	  show	  any	  peaks,	  but	  instead	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour	  from	  the	  beginning	  (from	  the	  
first	   simulation	   number).	   This	   means	   that	   the	   intersection	   between	   measured	   data	   and	  
simulated	  ones	  is	  decreasing	  with	  the	  increasing	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations.	  This	  probably	  
depends	  on	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  quality	  of	  measured	  data	  is	  low	  in	  that	  period,	  and	  therefore	  the	  
model	  output	  is	  far	  from	  them.	  More	  considerations	  will	  be	  presented	  moving	  forward	  with	  
the	   discussion.	   In	   Table	   11	   the	   chosen	   thresholds	   for	   the	   different	   analysed	   events	   are	  
presented,	  together	  with	  an	  indication	  about	  whether	  the	  plot	  shows	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour	  
or	  not.	  
Table	  11:	  threshold	  values	  for	  every	  analysed	  event.	  The	  threshold	  values	  are	  chosen	  according	  to	  the	  maximum	  
percentage	  of	  coverage	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  observation	  error	  interval,	  which	  is	  given	  by	  the	  plot	  
of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  against	  the	  intersection	  λ	  (%).	  Moreover	  an	  indication	  about	  the	  behaviour	  of	  the	  curve	  	  
(decreasing	  or	  not)	  is	  given.

Figure	  18:	  plot	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  against	  the	  percentage	  of	  coverage	  between	  model	  simulation	  bounds	  
and	  observation	  error	  interval	  for	  measuring	  campaign	  3	  (MC3).	  
	  
Figure	  19:	  plot	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  against	  the	  percentage	  of	  coverage	  between	  model	  simulation	  bounds	  
and	  observation	  error	  interval	  for	  rain	  event	  RF1.	  
As	  already	  explained,	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  of	  this	  project	  has	  been	  set	  equal	  to	  1000.	  
The	  number	  of	  simulations	  was	  chosen	  according	  to	  the	  following	  procedure:	  the	  uncertainty	  
analysis	  has	  been	  performed	  with	  a	  variable	  number	  of	  simulations	  (50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  
1000).	  At	  each	  step	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  was	  compared	  with	  the	  
one	  obtained	  in	  the	  previous	  step	  and	  when	  two	  following	  graphs	  do	  not	  have	  an	  appreciable	  
difference,	  the	  highest	  simulation	  number	  among	  the	  two	  was	  chosen.	  The	  different	  graphs	  
are	  hereunder	  shown	  for	  the	  rain	  event	  MC3	  (Figure	  20).

Figure	   20:	   plot	   of	   the	   number	   of	   simulations	   against	   the	   percentage	   of	   the	   coverage	   between	   model	   simulation	  
bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval	   for	   rain	   event	   MC3	   for	   a	   different	   maximum	   number	   of	   simulations	  
(respectively	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000).	  
As	   it	   can	   be	   seen	   from	   the	   last	   two	   graphs	   (simulation	   number=750	   and	   simulation	  
number=1000,	   respectively),	   there	   is	   not	   an	   appreciable	   difference	   in	   the	   maximum	  
intersection	   percentage	   (close	   to	   78%	   in	   both	   cases)	   and	   in	   the	   corresponding	   number	   of	  
simulations	  (around	  500).	  Therefore	  1000	  simulations	  were	  chosen.	  This	  procedure	  was	  made	  
for	  all	  the	  considered	  rain	  events,	  and	  it	  turned	  out	  that	  1000	  simulations	  represent	  a	  good	  
choice	  for	  every	  considered	  event.

May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
50  
At	  this	  point	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  procedure,	  the	  chosen	  threshold	  for	  each	  rain	  event	  
was	   given	   as	   an	   input	   in	   the	   script	   coverage_analysis,	   which	   performs	   the	   step	   (8)	   of	   the	  
procedure.	  It	  was	  therefore	  possible	  for	  the	  model	  to	  select	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  
and	  to	  create	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  only	  with	  the	  output	  coming	  from	  the	  selected	  sets	  
of	  parameter.	   
	  
The	  graphs	  coming	  from	  the	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  all	  the	  analysed	  events	  are	  presented	  in	  
Appendix	  F,	  while	  the	  result	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  the	  rain	  event	  MC3	  is	  presented	  in	  
Figure	  21.	  The	  red	  line	  represents	  the	  measured	  data,	  while	  the	  pink	  bounds	  represent	  the	  
observation	   error	   interval.	   The	   light	   blue	   bounds	   are	   the	   model	   uncertainty	   bounds,	   which	  
become	  purple	  when	  an	  intersection	  with	  the	  observation	  error	  interval	  is	  observed.	  The	  blue	  
line	  represents	  the	  output	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method.	  The	  brown	  diamonds	  are	  the	  
grab	  samples.	  
	  
However,	  this	  makes	  sense	  and	  is	  useful	  only	  in	  the	  case	  of	  a	  clear	  peak	  shape	  in	  the	  ‘no	  of	  
simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  graph,	  since	  the	  command	  ‘while lambda<thr’	  in	  the	  script	  
coverage_analysis is	  telling	  to	  take	  all	  the	  simulations	  for	  which	  the	  intersection	  is	  lower	  than	  
the	   fixed	   threshold.	   When	   a	   decreasing	   trend	   is	   observed,	   through	   the	   command	   ‘while 
lambda<thr’, only	  one	  case	  is	  selected	  (the	  first	  one).	  Therefore,	  when	  a	  decreasing	  trend	  is	  
detected,	   a	   less	   severe	   condition	   is	   needed,	   and	   the	   command	   ‘while lambda<thr’ is	  
substituted	  with	  the	  following	  one:	  ‘for k=3:5’,	  where	  5	  is	  only	  a	  temporary	  number	  and	  it	  
is	  replaced	  with	  others	  for	  analysing	  several	  different	  cases	  (10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  
1000).	  The	  second	  number	  in	  the	  command	  indicates	  the	  number	  of	  the	  parameters	  sets	  that	  
are	  used	  for	  the	  creation	  of	  the	  model	  simulation	  bounds. Increasing	  that	  number,	  therefore,	  
means	   that	   the	   number	   of	   parameter	   sets	   taken	   into	   account	   is	   increasing	   and	   hence	   an	  
enlargement	   of	   the	   model	   simulation	   bounds	   is	   expected	   (as	   it	   can	   be	   seen,	   for	   RF1,	   from	  
Figure	  22,	  in	  which	  only	  the	  cases	  for	  k=250,	  500,	  750,	  1000	  are	  presented,	  since	  for	  the	  other	  
cases	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  are	  too	  tight).	  
	  
Figure	  21:	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  MC3.	  Red	  line=measured	  data,	  pink	  bounds=	  observation	  error	  interval	  (±30%),	  
light	   blue	   bounds=model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   (output	   of	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration),	   purple	  
bounds=intersection	   between	   model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   blue	   line=output	   of	   the	  
fminsearch	  calibration,	  brown	  diamonds=grab	  samples.

Figure	  22:	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  RF1,	  for	  k=250,	  k=500,	  k=750	  and	  k=1000.	  Red	  line=measured	  data,	  pink	  bounds=	  
observation	  error	  interval	  (±30%),	  light	  blue	  bounds=model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  (output	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  
calibration),	   purple	   bounds=intersection	   between	   model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   blue	  
line=output	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration.	  
As	  it	  can	  be	  observed	  in	  Figure	  22,	  by	  changing	  the	  number	  of	  parameter	  sets	  included	  in	  the	  
construction	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds,	  only	  the	  amplitude	  (width)	  of	  the	  bounds	  will	  
change,	  but	  not	  the	  intersection	  percentage.	  The	  very	  low	  coverage	  between	  model	  prediction	  
bounds	  and	  observation	  error	  interval	  can	  be	  explained	  through	  the	  following	  bullet	  points:	  
• 
Not	  all	  the	  uncertainty	  sources	  have	  been	  included	  in	  the	  study.	  The	  uncertainties	  in	  
models	  concern	  the	  model	  parameters,	  the	  input	  data,	  the	  calibration	  data,	  the	  model	  
structure	  and	  their	  links	  (Deletic	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  For	  example,	  the	  uncertainties	  in	  the	  
model	  structure	  have	  not	  been	  considered	  in	  this	  analysis	  and	  it	  is	  possible	  that	  for	  an	  
ill-­‐posed	   model,	   the	   uncertainty	   calibration	   method	   cannot	   focus	   around	   the	   best	  
parameter	   sets.	   Other	   examples	   are	   that	   the	   error	   on	   measured	   data	   has	   been	  
calculated	  as	  the	  30%	  of	  each	  measured	  datapoint,	  but	  this	  is	  just	  an	  approximation	  
since	  the	  error	  increases	  with	  time,	  or	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  choice	  of	  different	  calibration	  
algorithms	  can	  lead	  to	  the	  determination	  of	  different	  sets	  of	  parameters.	  Therefore,	  
the	  modelling	  approach	  should	  be	  deeply	  questioned	  (Dotto	  et	  al.,	  2012).	  
• 
The	  measured	  data	  are	  not	  precise,	  affected	  by	  both	  random	  and	  systematic	  effects.	  
Moreover,	  the	  cleaning	  of	  the	  sensors	  should	  have	  been	  done	  once	  per	  week	  during

M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
52  
the	   monitoring	   period,	   but	   this	   was	   not	   done	   systematically,	   and	   it	   resulted	   in	   low	  
precision	  and	  accuracy	  between	  measured	  data	  and	  grab	  samples	  (as	  it	  can	  especially	  
be	  observed	  for	  measuring	  campaigns	  6	  and	  7	  in	  Appendix	  B).	  A	  clear	  example	  of	  the	  
low	   quality	   of	   the	   measured	   data	   can	   be	   seen	   in	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   for	  
measuring	  campaign	  6	  (Figure	  23).	  
	  
Figure	  23:	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  MC6.	  Red	  line=measured	  data,	  pink	  bounds=	  observation	  error	  interval	  (±30%),	  
light	   blue	   bounds=model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   (output	   of	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration),	   purple	  
bounds=intersection	   between	   model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   blue	   line=output	   of	   the	  
fminsearch	  calibration,	  brown	  diamonds=grab	  samples.	  
In	  fact,	  from	  the	  above	  figure,	  it	  is	  evident	  that	  the	  measurements	  coming	  from	  the	  sensor	  are	  
totally	   wrong	   compared	   with	   the	   grab	   sample	   concentration	   values.	   The	   model	   prediction	  
bounds	  are	  instead	  able	  to	  approximate	  the	  concentration	  values	  obtained	  through	  the	  grab	  
samples.	  However,	  by	  considering	  the	  value	  of	  the	  RMSE,	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  output	  
will	  have	  a	  lower	  value	  than	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  output.	  In	  fact	  the	  RMSE	  is	  
calculated	  as	  the	  distance	  between	  the	  output	  of	  the	  calibration	  and	  the	  measured	  data.	  The	  
output	  of	  the	  calibration	  in	  the	  case	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method	  is	  the	  blue	  line	  of	  
the	   ammonia	   calibration	   graphs,	   while	   in	   the	   case	   of	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	  
method,	   it	   is	   the	   median	   of	   the	   model	   prediction	   bounds,	   i.e.	   the	   median	   of	   the	   light	   blue	  
bounds	   of	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   graphs.	   This	   means	   that	   the	   RMSE	   values	   are	   not	  
representative	   in	   case	   of	   neither	   precise	   nor	   accurate	   measured	   data.	   Unfortunately	   the	  
goodness	  of	  the	  measured	  data	  can	  only	  be	  assessed	  in	  the	  measuring	  campaign	  rain	  events,	  
where	  a	  comparison	  between	  two	  data	  sources	  can	  be	  done.	  To	  conclude,	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  
fminsearch	  output	  is	  far	  from	  the	  grab	  samples	  indicates	  that,	  for	  low	  quality	  measured	  data,	  
the	  output	  obtained	  through	  the	  traditional	  calibration	  method	  is	  not	  reliable.	  	  
	  
The	   RMSE	   values	   for	   both	   the	   calibration	   methods	   are	   reported	   in	   Table	   12.	   They	   were	  
calculated	  through	  the	  scripts	  Comparison1	  (for	  fminsearch	  calibration)	  and	  Comparison2	  (for	  
modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration),	   which	   can	   be	   seen	   in	   Appendix	   C.	   The	   number	   of	  
parameters	  sets	  considered	  for	  the	  construction	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  are	  reported	  
in	   the	   second	   column	   of	   Table	   12.	   They	   were	   determined	   through	   the	   condition	   ‘while 
lambda<thr’	  for	  the	  cases	  in	  which	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  resulted

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli

to	   have	   a	   peak.	   The	   rain	   events	   for	   which	   the	   plot	   ‘no	   of	   simulation	   –	   intersection	   λ	   (%)’	  
resulted	  to	  have	  a	  decreasing	  trend	  are	  coloured	  in	  red	  in	  the	  table.	  For	  the	  event	  MC7	  the	  
RMSE	  value	  in	  the	  case	  of	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  was	  calculated	  for	  as	  many	  cases	  
as	  the	  considered	  cases	  in	  the	  calibration	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000).	  Since	  the	  
difference	  in	  the	  RMSE	  values	  between	  the	  different	  cases	  is	  low,	  this	  was	  done	  only	  for	  the	  
rain	  event	  MC7.	  For	  the	  other	  events	  in	  which	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  
resulted	   to	   have	   a	   decreasing	   behaviour,	   the	   RMSE	   value	   reported	   in	   the	   table	   for	   the	  
uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  is	  the	  one	  of	  the	  case	  k=1000.

As	   it	   can	   be	   seen	   from	   the	   above	   table,	   the	   RMSE	   values	   of	   the	   fminsearch	   calibration	   are	  
almost	   always	   lower	   than	   the	   ones	   of	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration.	   However	   the	  
difference	   is	   not	   big	   and,	   as	   it	   is	   explained	   above,	   this	   measure	   of	   the	   error	   is	   not	   very	  
representative	   when	   the	   quality	   of	   the	   measured	   data	   is	   not	   good.	   Moreover,	   by	   doing	   a	  
comparison	  between	  Table	  11	  and	  Table	  12,	  it	  is	  possible	  to	  see	  that	  the	  events	  that	  present	  a	  
lower	   RMSE	   value	   (mg/l)	   are	   those	   that	   show	   a	   higher	   intersection	   percentage	   (%),	   i.e.	   for	  
those	  events	  the	  model	  calibration	  gives	  a	  good	  approximation	  of	  the	  measured	  data.	  These	  
events	  are	  for	  example:	  MC3,	  MC4,	  MC5,	  MC6,	  LT	  and	  LT1.	  
	  
Some	  other	  considerations	  have	  to	  be	  done	  on	  the	  long	  term	  event	  calibration.	  The	  aim	  of	  
considering	  the	  long	  term	  event	  is	  to	  prove	  that	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  can	  be	  used	  
on	  an	  entire	  time	  series	  of	  ammonia	  concentration	  that	  contains	  sensor	  calibrations	  in	  it.	  This

fact	  generally	  compromises	  the	  possibility	  of	  using	  the	  series	  without	  cutting	  it	  into	  separate	  
events	  in	  which	  no	  sensor	  calibration	  has	  been	  detected.	  The	  chosen	  period	  for	  the	  long	  term	  
calibration	  (14/08/2014	  –	  13/10/2014)	  contains	  two	  sensor	  calibrations	  inside,	  the	  first	  one	  
made	  on	  the	  29th	  of	  August	  and	  the	  second	  one	  on	  the	  29th	  of	  September.	  The	  choice	  of	  doing	  
a	  sensor	  calibration	  has	  been	  justified	  by	  the	  comparison	  between	  the	  data	  measured	  by	  the	  
sensor	   and	   the	   data	   coming	   from	   the	   grab	   samples	   of	   measuring	   campaigns	   6	   and	   7.	   The	  
deviation	   between	   the	   two	   datasets	   is	   reported	   in	   Table	   3,	   both	   in	   (mg/l)	   and	   in	   (%).	   The	  
sensor	  calibration	  method	  used	  was	  a	  single	  point	  calibration	  method,	  so	  that	  the	  correction	  
of	  the	  sensor	  resulted	  to	  be	  partial	  and	  not	  consistent.	  The	  graph	  for	  the	  ammonia	  calibration	  
for	  the	  long	  term	  period	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  F	  (together	  with	  the	  graphs	  of	  the	  events	  LT1	  
and	  LT2).	  Here	  only	  a	  zoom	  in	  on	  the	  areas	  of	  the	  sensor	  calibrations	  is	  presented	  for	  the	  long	  
term	   events	   LT1	   and	   LT2,	   which	   correspond	   to	   the	   period	   of	   the	   6th	   and	   the	   7th	   measuring	  
campaigns	  (Figure	  24	  and	  25).

Figure	  25:	  zoom	  in	  on	  the	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  LT2	  (which	  contains	  MC7).	  Red	  line=measured	  data,	  pink	  bounds=	  
observation	  error	  interval	  (±30%),	  light	  blue	  bounds=model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  (output	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  
calibration),	   purple	   bounds=intersection	   between	   model	   uncertainty	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   blue	  
line=output	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration,	  brown	  diamonds=grab	  samples,	  green	  line=sensor	  calibration	  date.	  
As	   it	   can	   be	   easily	   seen	   from	   the	   two	   figures,	   the	   sensor	   calibration	   is	   neither	   precise	   nor	  
accurate,	  being	  in	  the	  first	  case	  (LT1)	  the	  measured	  data	  much	  lower	  that	  the	  grab	  sample	  
concentration	   values,	   and	   in	   the	   second	   case	   (LT2)	   much	   higher.	   Moreover,	   the	   model	  
uncertainty	  bounds	  give	  a	  better	  approximation	  of	  the	  grab	  sample	  concentration	  values	  than	  
the	  fminsearch	  output	  (especially	  in	  the	  case	  of	  LT2).	  Therefore,	  once	  again	  the	  RMSE	  values	  
given	   in	   Table	   12	   for	   the	   two	   calibration	   methods	   cannot	   be	   considered	   as	   representative	  
(19.96	  vs.	  19.10	  for	  LT1	  and	  29.13	  vs.	  34.06	  for	  LT2).	  As	  the	  aim	  of	  the	  long	  term	  calibration	  
was	  to	  demonstrate	  that	  the	  output	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  is	  able	  to	  reproduce	  
the	  same	  trend	  of	  the	  measured	  data,	  it	  can	  be	  affirmed	  that	  this	  is	  not	  possible	  when	  the	  
measured	  data	  are	  far	  from	  the	  concentration	  values	  coming	  from	  the	  grab	  samples.	  However,	  
the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  method	  are	  able	  to	  intercept	  
the	  grab	  samples	  concentration	  values	  and	  therefore	  they	  can	  be	  considered	  as	  reliable	  for	  the	  
ammonia	  calibration.	  On	  the	  other	  hand,	  the	  output	  coming	  from	  the	  traditional	  calibration	  
method	  is	  closer	  to	  the	  measured	  data	  (that	  are	  inconsistent)	  and	  farther	  from	  the	  ammonia	  
grab	  sample	  concentration	  values.	  Therefore	  it	  can	  be	  affirmed	  that	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  
calibration	  method,	  in	  the	  case	  of	  neither	  precise	  nor	  accurate	  measured	  data,	  gives	  a	  better	  
and	  more	  reliable	  representation	  of	  the	  real	  ammonia	  concentration	  values	  (the	  ones	  coming	  
from	  the	  grab	  samples)	  than	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method.	  
	  
All	   the	   graphs	   for	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   that	   are	   not	   presented	   here	   can	   be	   seen	   in	  
Appendix	  F.	  
	  
Now	   some	   considerations	   about	   the	   selected	   behavioural	   parameter	   sets	   are	   made.	   The	  
fminsearch	  calibration	  method	  does	  not	  have	  any	  constraints	  for	  the	  parameters’	  values	  and	  it	  
gives	  as	  a	  result	  only	  one	  parameter	  set,	  which	  is	  the	  minimum	  for	  the	  given	  problem.	  On	  the	  
other	   hand,	   with	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   method,	   a	   range	   is	   given	   for	   the	   parameters’	  
values,	   according	   to	   the	   physical	   meaning	   of	   the	   parameters,	   so	   that	   values	   that	   have	   no

physical	  sense	  are	  avoided.	  The	  ranges	  for	  the	  ammonia	  parameters	  investigated	  within	  the

As	  it	  can	  be	  observed	  from	  the	  above	  table,	  many	  values	  are	  out	  of	  the	  physical	  acceptable	  
range	   for	   the	   two	   analysed	   parameters.	   This	   can	   be	   explained	   by	   the	   fact	   that	   other	  
uncertainty	  sources,	  which	  are	  not	  taken	  into	  account	  in	  this	  study,	  are	  present	  in	  the	  model.	  
The	  parameter	  values	  try	  therefore	  to	  compensate	  for	  this	  lack	  of	  information.	  Therefore	  the	  
values	   tend	   to	   move	   out	   of	   the	   given	   range,	   and	   in	   doing	   this	   they	   become	   abstract,	  
conceptual.	  
	  
Table	  14	  contains	  a	  summary	  of	  the	  results	  coming	  from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  
method.	   Since	   the	   parameter	   sets	   with	   this	   approach	   are	   many,	   only	   the	   minimum,	   the	  
median	  and	  the	  maximum	  values	  of	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  are	  given.	  	  For	  the	  cases	  in	  
which	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour	  of	  the	  plot	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  is	  observed,	  the	  
values	   of	   the	   minimum,	   median	   and	   maximum	   behavioural	   parameter	   sets	   have	   been	  
calculated	  by	  considering	  fixed	  numbers	  of	  parameter	  sets	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  
1000).	  In	  Table	  14	  only	  the	  values	  for	  the	  case	  k=1000	  are	  presented	  (coloured	  in	  red),	  while	  all	  
the	   other	   cases	   are	   presented	   in	   Appendix	   F.	   In	   Electronic	   Appendix	   1	   the	   values	   of	   every	  
behavioural	  parameter	  set	  are	  given	  for	  each	  one	  of	  the	  analysed	  rain	  events	  and	  also	  for	  the	  
variable	  k	  values	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000)	  in	  case	  of	  decreasing	  behaviour	  of	  
the	  plot	  ‘no	  of	  simulations	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’.
