For	  having	  a	  more	  clear	  idea	  about	  the	  meaning	  of	  the	  values	  of	  the	  parameter	  sets	  presented	  
in	  Table	  14,	  the	  plot	  of	  the	  values	  of	  the	  two	  parameters	  against	  the	  RMSE	  value	  is	  hereunder	  
presented	  for	  the	  cases	  MC3	  and	  RF1,	  together	  with	  the	  scatter	  plotmatrix	  between	  the	  two	  
parameters	  and	  the	  RMSE.	  Each	  dot	  in	  the	  graphs	  represents	  one	  run	  of	  the	  model.	  In	  the	  plot	  
of	  the	  values	  of	  the	  parameters	  against	  the	  RMSE	  value,	  all	  the	  parameter	  values	  selected	  by	  
the	  latin	  hypercube	  function	  are	  given	  (grey	  points),	  and	  the	  behavioural	  ones	  are	  coloured	  in	  
blue	  for	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  wastewater	  (parameter	  1,	  Figure	  26)	  and	  in	  red	  for	  the	  
ammonia	  concentration	  in	  runoff	  (parameter	  2,	  Figure	  27).	  The	  plotmatrix,	  instead,	  was	  made	  
only	  with	  the	  values	  of	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  (Figure	  28).

Figure	  27: plot	  of	  the	  parameter	  values	  for	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  runoff,	  selected	  through	  the	  latin	  hypercube	  
function,	   against	   the	   RMSE	   values	   (mg/l)	   for	   measuring	   campaign	   3	   (MC3).	   The	   behavioural	   parameters	   are	  
coloured	  in	  red.	  
If	  the	  plot	  of	  the	  two	  investigated	  parameters	  against	  the	  RMSE	  shows	  that	  the	  behavioural	  
parameter	   values	   identify	   a	   minimum	   in	   the	   considered	   range,	   then	   it	   means	   that	   a	   good	  
approximation	   is	   achieved	   (it	   is	   the	   case	   of	   Figure	   26).	   The	   case	   in	   which	   the	   selected	  
behavioural	  parameter	  values	  do	  not	  reach	  a	  minimum	  in	  the	  given	  range	  (Figure	  27)	  means	  
that	  there	  are	  other	  sources	  of	  uncertainty	  not	  considered	  in	  this	  study:	  the	  parameter	  values	  
tent	  to	  compensate	  for	  this	  lack	  of	  information	  in	  order	  to	  give	  a	  lower	  RMSE,	  but	  they	  lose	  
their	  physical	  meaning	  by	  going	  out	  of	  the	  given	  range.	  Here,	  probably,	  the	  main	  uncertainties	  
concern	   the	   measured	   data.	   As	   it	   can	   be	   seen	   from	   the	   graphs	   ‘NH4	   runoff	   concentration	  
(mg/l)	   –	   RMSE	   (mg/l)’	   of	   all	   the	   other	   considered	   events	   (Appendix	   F),	   the	   ammonia	  
concentration	  in	  runoff	  never	  reaches	  a	  minimum	  in	  the	  given	  range.	  	  
	  
Figure	   28	   shows	   the	   scatter	   plotmatrix	   between	   the	   two	   investigated	   parameters	   and	   the	  
RMSE	  values	  for	  the	  rain	  event	  MC3.

wastewater	  and	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  runoff)	  and	  the	  RMSE	  values	  for	  measuring	  campaign	  3	  (MC3).	  Along	  the	  
diagonal	  the	  histogram	  plots	  of	  the	  data	  are	  shown.

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli

Along	  the	  diagonal	  of	  the	  scatter	  plotmatrix	  there	  are	  the	  histogram	  plots	  of	  the	  data,	  which	  
give	   an	   indication	   of	   the	   distribution	   of	   the	   parameter	   values.	   With	   other	   words,	   the	  
histograms	  on	  the	  diagonal	  represent	  the	  density	  function	  of	  the	  model	  efficiency	  (the	  one	  in	  
the	  right	  lower	  corner)	  and	  the	  likelihood	  density	  of	  the	  parameters	  (the	  one	  in	  the	  left	  upper	  
corner	   and	   the	   one	   in	   the	   middle)	   (Dotto	   et	   al.,	   2012).	   In	   this	   case	   (rain	   event	   MC3),	   for	  
example,	  it	  can	  be	  seen	  that	  for	  the	  ammonia	  wastewater	  concentration	  the	  majority	  of	  the	  
behavioural	   parameter	   values	   are	   in	   the	   middle	   of	   the	   given	   range,	   while	   for	  the	   ammonia	  
concentration	   in	   runoff	   the	   majority	   of	   the	   behavioural	   parameter	   values	   are	   on	   the	   right	  
border	  of	  the	  given	  range,	  which	  corresponds	  to	  the	  case	  in	  which	  the	  parameter	  values	  try	  to	  
compensate	  for	  the	  inefficiencies	  of	  the	  model.	  The	  plot	  of	  parameter	  1	  against	  parameter	  2	  
gives	  an	  indication	  of	  a	  possible	  correlation	  between	  the	  parameters	  (if	  a	  clear	  trend	  can	  be	  
identified).	   In	   all	   the	   analysed	   cases,	   no	   correlation	   can	   be	   observed	   between	   the	   two	  
parameters	  (the	  graph	  for	  all	  the	  remaining	  rain	  events	  can	  be	  seen	  in	  Appendix	  F).	  
Now	  the	  same	  graphs	  are	  presented	  for	  the	  event	  RF1.	  This	  event,	  as	  already	  discussed	  in	  this	  
work	  before	  (Figure	  19),	  shows	  a	  decreasing	  trend	  in	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  
λ	  (%)’,	  which	  means	  that	  the	  intersection	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  observation	  
error	  interval	  decreases	  with	  the	  increasing	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations.	  This	  is	  probably	  due	  
to	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  measured	  data	  are	  not	  good,	  so	  that	  the	  simulated	  data	  are	  not	  able	  to	  
reproduce	   their	   trend.	   This	   theory	   is	   also	   supported	   from	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   graph	  
(Figure	   22),	   in	   which	   it	   can	   be	   seen	   that	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   in	   the	   case	   of	   the	  
simulated	   data	   is	   much	   lower	   than	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   of	   the	   measured	   data.	  
Therefore,	   from	   the	   plot	   of	   the	   RMSE	   value	   against	   the	   values	   of	   the	   two	   parameters	  
(ammonia	  concentration	  in	  wastewater	  and	  in	  runoff,	  Figure	  29	  and	  Figure	  30)	  it	  is	  expected	  
that	   a	   minimum	   is	   not	   reached	   within	   the	   given	   range,	   since	   the	   calibration	   of	   the	   two	  
parameters	  tries	  to	  compensate	  for	  this	  problem	  by	  increasing	  their	  concentration	  values.	  In	  
other	  words,	  the	  calibration	  is	  using	  the	  parameter	  values	  to	  compensate	  for	  the	  structural	  
inefficiencies	  of	  the	  model	  or	  of	  the	  input	  data.	  Here,	  probably,	  are	  the	  measured	  data	  to	  be	  
totally	  incorrect.	  In	  the	  case	  of	  this	  rain	  event,	  the	  plot	  of	  the	  two	  parameters	  versus	  the	  RMSE	  
only	   contains	   the	   behavioural	   parameters,	   since	   their	   number	   has	   been	   imposed	   equal	   to	  
1000,	  in	  order	  to	  take	  the	  less	  strict	  case.	  	  
In	  Figure	  31	  the	  scatter	  plotmatrix	  for	  the	  event	  RF1	  is	  shown.	  
==PAGE:75==

Figure	   31:	   scatter	   plotmatrix	   between	   the	   two	   ammonia	   parameters	   considered	   (ammonia	   concentration	   in	  
wastewater	  and	  ammonia	  concentration	  in	  runoff)	  and	  the	  RMSE	  values	  for	  rain	  event	  RF1.	  Along	  the	  diagonal	  the	  
histogram	  plots	  of	  the	  data	  are	  shown.	  
The	  graphs	  for	  all	  the	  others	  rain	  events	  considered	  in	  this	  study	  (except	  for	  MC3	  and	  RF1)	  are	  
given	  in	  Appendix	  F.	  
6.2.1. Different	  intersection	  method	  
The	  intersection	  condition	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  observation	  error	  interval	  
has	   been	   changed	   during	   the	   development	   of	   this	   work,	   since	   it	   was	   considered	   that	   an	  
intersection	  condition	  equal	  to	  1,	  even	  when	  a	  partial	  intersection	  is	  observed,	  could	  be	  too	  
lax.	  This	  can	  lead	  to	  a	  not	  representative	  evaluation	  of	  the	  simulated	  ammonia	  concentration	  
compared	  to	  the	  measured	  data.	  Therefore,	  the	  ammonia	  calibration,	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  
modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   method,	   has	   been	   repeated	   for	   a	   second	   time,	   with	   a	   changed	  
intersection	  criterion,	  on	  two	  rain	  events	  (MC3	  and	  RF1).	  For	  a	  more	  detailed	  description	  of	  
the	  chosen	  different	  intersection	  method	  see	  section	  4.2.3.	  It	  is	  important	  to	  notice	  that,	  by	  
giving	  a	  value	  lower	  than	  1	  for	  the	  partial	  intersection	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  
observation	   error	   interval,	   a	   stricter	   condition	   is	   posed.	   The	   given	   condition	   therefore	   is	   a	  
measure	  of	  the	  intersection	  percentage	  of	  the	  area	  between	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  
simulation	   error	   interval.	   The	   results	   are	   hereunder	   presented	   for	   the	   two	   rain	   events	  
analyzed.	  
	  
Firstly,	  the	  graphs	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  were	  evaluated	  and	  compared	  with	  
the	  same	  graphs	  obtained	  with	  the	  previous	  intersection	  condition	  (partial	  intersection	  equal	  
to	  1).	  In	  Figure	  32	  and	  Figure	  33	  the	  comparison	  is	  graphically	  presented	  for	  MC3	  and	  RF1	  (for	  
k=1000	  simulations).

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
62  
	  
Figure	   32:	   plot	   of	   the	   number	   of	   simulations	   against	   the	   percentage	   of	   the	   coverage	   between	   model	   simulation	  
bounds	  and	  observation	  error	  interval	  for	  rain	  event	  MC3	  for	  the	  different	  intersection	  conditions.	  The	  graph	  on	  the	  
left	  represents	  the	  condition	  of	  partial	  intersection	  equal	  to	  1,	  while	  the	  one	  on	  the	  right	  represents	  the	  condition	  of	  
total	  intersection	  only	  equal	  to	  1.	  	  
	  
Figure	  33:	  plot	  of	  the	  number	  of	  simulations	  against	  the	  percentage	  of	  coverage	  between	  model	  simulation	  bounds	  
and	   observation	   error	   interval	   for	   rain	   event	   RF1	   for	   the	   different	   intersection	   conditions.	   The	   graph	   on	   the	   left	  
represents	  the	  condition	  of	  partial	  intersection	  equal	  to	  1,	  while	  the	  one	  on	  the	  right	  represents	  the	  condition	  of	  total	  
intersection	  only	  equal	  to	  1.	  	  	  
As	   it	   can	   be	   easily	   assessed	   from	   the	   above	   figures,	   in	   both	   cases	   the	   new	   intersection	  
condition	  resulted	  in	  a	  lower	  intersection	  percentage	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  
observation	  error	  interval,	  as	  it	  was	  expected.	  Since	  both	  the	  graphs	  with	  the	  new	  intersection	  
condition	   have	   showed	   a	   decreasing	   behavior,	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   was	   made	   at	   fixed	  
interval	  of	  the	  considered	  behavioral	  parameters	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000).	  
Here,	  only	  the	  case	  for	  k=1000	  is	  presented,	  since	  it	  has	  been	  demonstrated	  that	  the	  number	  
of	  selected	  behavioral	  parameters	  only	  influences	  the	  width	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds.	  
Figure	  34	  and	  Figure	  35	  show	  a	  comparison	  between	  the	  ammonia	  calibration	  with	  the	  two	  
intersection	  conditions	  for	  rain	  events	  MC3	  and	  RF1.	  It	  is	  expected	  that	  the	  graphs	  with	  the	  
new	  intersection	  condition	  (the	  ones	  on	  the	  right)	  are	  equal	  to	  the	  graphs	  with	  the	  previous	  
intersection	  condition,	  since	  the	  coverage	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  observation	  
error	  interval	  does	  not	  vary,	  it	  is	  only	  the	  way	  of	  evaluating	  it	  that	  has	  been	  changed.	  In	  fact,	  
while	  with	  the	  previous	  condition	  a	  partial	  coverage	  was	  accounted	  as	  an	  intersection,	  with	  
the	  new	  condition	  only	  the	  total	  coverage	  is	  considered	  as	  an	  intersection	  (value	  equal	  to	  1)

and	  the	  partial	  coverage	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  observation	  error	  interval	  is

coverage	  between	  the	  area	  of	  the	  model	  simulation	  bounds	  and	  the	  simulation	  error	  interval,	  
but	  not	  the	  percentage	  of	  coverage	  on	  the	  event	  time	  steps	  (as	  in	  the	  previous	  case).	  	  
Figure	  34:	  uncertainty	  analysis	  for	  MC3	  for	  the	  two	  intersection	  conditions.	  The	  graph	  on	  the	  left	  represents	  the

uncertainty	   bounds	   and	   observation	   error	   interval,	   blue	   line=output	   of	   the	   fminsearch	   calibration,	   brown	  
diamonds=grab	  samples.	  
	  
Figure	   35:	   uncertainty	   analysis	   for	   RF1	   for	   the	   two	   intersection	   conditions.	   The	   graph	   on	   the	   left	   represents	   the	  
condition	  of	  partial	  intersection	  equal	  to	  1,	  while	  the	  one	  on	  the	  right	  represents	  the	  condition	  of	  total	  intersection	  
equal	   to	   1.	   Red	   line=measured	   data,	   pink	   bounds=	   observation	   error	   interval	   (±30%),	   light	   blue	   bounds=model	  
uncertainty	  bounds	  (output	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration),	  purple	  bounds=intersection	  between	  model

As	   already	   specified,	   the	   two	   graphs	   showing	   the	   ammonia	   calibration	   with	   the	   two	  
intersection	  conditions	  should	  be	  equal:	  this	  is	  true	  for	  RF1,	  but	  not	  for	  MC3.	  This	  is	  because,

k=1000	  was	  imposed.	  For	  the	  event	  RF1,	  the	  graphs	  are	  exactly	  the	  same,	  since	  in	  both	  cases

the	   model	   prediction	   bounds	   were	   constructed	   using	   k=1000	   parameter	   sets.	   The	   only

prediction	  bounds	  and	  the	  area	  of	  the	  observation	  error	  interval	  overlap	  for	  the	  2%	  
(Figure	  33,	  right	  side).

Monte	   Carlo)	   and	   for	   the	   two	   intersection	   criteria	   are	   presented	   (for	   rain	   events	   MC3	   and	  
RF1).	  
Table	  15:	  number	  of	  parameter	  sets	  considered	  for	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  construction	  and	  RMSE	  values	  (for	  
both	  the	  fminsearch	  and	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  approaches)	  for	  the	  two	  intersection	  methods	  for	  
MC3	   and	   RF1.	   Intersection	   criterion	   1	   refers	   to	   the	   method	   in	   which	   partial	   intersection	   is	   equal	   to	   1,	   while	  
Intersection	   criterion	   2	   refers	   to	   the	   method	   in	   which	   the	   value	   1	   is	   given	   only	   when	   complete	   intersection	   is	  
observed.

The	   same	   considerations	   made	   before	   can	   be	   applied	   to	   this	   case	   of	   different	   intersection	  
criterion.	  In	  fact,	  also	  in	  this	  case,	  the	  RMSE	  values	  for	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  are	  lower	  
than	  the	  ones	  for	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration.	  Moreover,	  by	  increasing	  the	  number	  
of	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  included	  in	  the	  construction	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds,	  an	  
increase	  in	  the	  width	  of	  the	  bounds	  is	  observed,	  with	  a	  slight	  increase	  of	  the	  RMSE	  values.	  To	  
conclude,	   the	   RMSE	   values	   for	   the	   uncertainty	   based	   calibration	   of	   the	   two	   different	  
==PAGE:80==

intersection	  criteria	  do	  not	  show	  an	  appreciable	  difference,	  as	  it	  can	  be	  observed	  also	  from	  
Figure	  34	  and	  Figure	  35.	  
	  
By	   continuing	   the	   analysis	   for	   the	   case	   of	   different	   intersection	   criterion,	   the	   behavioural	  
parameter	  sets	  have	  to	  be	  taken	  into	  account.	  Table	  16	  summarises	  the	  minimum,	  the	  median	  
and	   the	   maximum	   obtained	   parameter	   values	   for	   the	   event	   MC3	   and	   RF1	   in	   the	   case	   of	  
different	  intersection	  criterion,	  for	  a	  number	  of	  considered	  parameter	  sets	  equal	  to	  1000.	  The	  
other	  cases	  for	  different	  values	  of	  the	  considered	  parameter	  sets	  can	  be	  found	  in	  Appendix	  F.	  
In	  Electronic	  Appendix	  1	  the	  values	  of	  every	  behavioural	  parameter	  set	  are	  given	  for	  every	  k	  
value	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000)	  for	  the	  different	  intersection	  criterion.

In	   Table	   17	   a	   comparison	   between	   the	   different	   parameter	   values	   obtained	   with	   the	  
traditional	  calibration	  method	  and	  with	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  method	  is	  given.	  The	  
results	   for	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	   method	   have	   been	   further	   subdivided	   for	  
taking	  into	  account	  the	  two	  intersection	  criteria	  adopted.	  Intersection	  criterion	  1	  is	  the	  one	  of	  
partial	  intersection	  equal	  to	  1,	  while	  intersection	  criterion	  2	  is	  the	  one	  of	  total	  intersection	  
only	  equal	  to	  1.	  It	  can	  be	  observed	  from	  the	  table	  that	  the	  greatest	  difference	  comes	  from	  the	  
comparison	  between	  the	  values	  of	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  and	  the	  ones	  of	  the	  modified	  
Monte	  Carlo	  (whether	  intersection	  criterion	  1	  or	  2	  is	  considered),	  especially	  for	  rain	  event	  RF1.	  
This	  is	  because	  the	  values	  obtained	  for	  this	  event,	  through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  traditional	  calibration	  
method,	  are	  not	  physically	  acceptable	  since	  they	  are	  out	  of	  the	  given	  physical	  range.	  It	  means	  
that	  some	  other	  uncertainties	  are	  present,	  which	  are	  not	  accounted	  in	  this	  study.

presented	  in	  Appendix	  F.	  
6.3. LOAD	  CALCULATION	  
In	  this	  section	  the	  results	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  are	  presented	  for	  the	  measured	  data,	  for	  the	  
data	  coming	  from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  and	  for	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  
Carlo	  calibration.	  All	  the	  load	  results	  are	  expressed	  in	  kilograms	  (kg).	  It	  is	  important	  to	  notice	  
that,	   since	   a	   measure	   of	   the	   flow	   coming	   from	   the	   CSO	   structure	   is	   not	   available,	   the	  
calculated	  loads	  are	  only	  used	  for	  having	  a	  qualitative	  comparison	  between	  the	  loads	  coming	  
from	  the	  two	  ammonia	  calibration	  methods	  and	  the	  loads	  coming	  from	  the	  measured	  data,	  so	  
that	  a	  measure	  of	  their	  goodness	  can	  be	  given.	   The	  explanation	  of	  the	  different	  calculation	  
techniques	  for	  the	  three	  cases	  is	  given	  in	  Chapter	  5	  (Load	  calculation).	  	  
	  
The	  results	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  for	  the	  measured	  data	  are	  presented	  in	  Table	  18.	  Since	  an	  
uncertainty	   of	   ±	   30%	   was	   applied	   to	   the	   total	   load,	   for	   being	   coherent	   with	   the	   ammonia	  
calibration,	  the	  results	  for	  every	  event	  include	  the	  total	  load	  and	  ±	  30%	  of	  the	  total	  load.

analysed	  events.

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
68  
As	  it	  can	  be	  easily	  accessed	  from	  Table	  19	  and	  Table	  20,	  the	  RMSE	  value	  for	  all	  the	  events	  is	  
lower	   in	   the	   case	   of	   fminsearch	   calibration	   than	   in	   the	   case	   of	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	  
calibration.	   The	   explanation	   is	   that	   probably	   the	   measured	   data	   are	   not	   good,	   so	   that	   the	  
resulting	  RMSE	  is	  not	  representative.	  	  
	  
A	  graphical	  comparison	  between	  the	  total	  load	  coming	  from	  the	  measured	  data	  and	  the	  total	  
loads	  coming	  from	  the	  two	  calibration	  methods	  is	  given	  hereunder	  for	  rain	  events	  MC3	  and	  
RF1	  (Figure	  36	  and	  Figure	  37),	  and	  in	  Appendix	  G	  for	  all	  the	  other	  events.	  
	  
In	  each	  boxplot	  resulting	  from	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  the	  central	  red	  line	  indicates	  
the	  median,	  while	  the	  bottom	  and	  the	  top	  edges	  of	  the	  box	  are	  the	  25th	  and	  75th	  percentiles,	  
respectively.	  The	  bottom	  and	  the	  top	  whiskers	  represent	  the	  extreme	  values	  (minimum	  and	  
maximum	  values)	  (MathWorks,	  2015). 
	  
Figure	  36:	  graphical	  comparison	  of	  the	  total	  loads	  (kg)	  calculated	  for	  the	  measured	  data	  and	  for	  the	  two	  ammonia	  
calibration	  methods	  (fminsearch	  and	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo)	  for	  the	  event	  MC3.	  
	  
	  
Figure	  37:	  graphical	  comparison	  of	  the	  total	  loads	  (kg)	  calculated	  for	  the	  measured	  data	  and	  for	  the	  two	  ammonia	  
calibration	  methods	  (fminsearch	  and	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo)	  for	  the	  event	  RF1	  (k=1000).

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
69  
The	  graphical	  overview	  of	  the	  loads	  for	  the	  two	  events	  reflects	  the	  results	  coming	  from	  the	  
graphs	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  and	  from	  the	  graphs	  of	  the	  ammonia	  calibration.	  
In	  fact,	  for	  the	  event	  MC3	  the	  intersection	  between	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  simulation	  
error	  interval	  resulted	  to	  be	  77%,	  with	  a	  clear	  peak	  around	  400	  simulations.	  Within	  the	  load	  
calculation,	   the	   load	   uncertainty	   bounds	   coming	   from	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	  
succeeded	  in	  covering	  the	  observation	  error	  interval	  of	  the	  load	  coming	  from	  the	  measured	  
data.	   The	   load	   coming	   from	   the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  is	  a	  bit	  lower	   compared	  to	  the	  load	  
interval	  coming	  from	  the	  measured	  data.	  On	  the	  other	  hand,	  for	  the	  event	  RF1,	  the	  graph	  ‘no	  
of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  resulted	  to	  have	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour,	  with	  a	  coverage	  
percentage	  of	  10%	  for	  k=1000	  simulations.	  Within	  the	  load	  calculation,	  the	  coverage	  between	  
the	   load	   interval	   coming	   from	   the	   measured	   data	   and	   the	   load	   uncertainty	   bounds	   coming	  
from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  is	  zero,	  i.e.	  even	  worst	  than	  the	  10%	  estimated	  for	  
the	  intersection	  of	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  and	  the	  observation	  error	  interval.	  This	  is	  due	  to	  
the	  fact	  that,	  for	  the	  load	  calculation,	  the	  uncertainties	  on	  ammonia	  concentration	  (estimated	  
through	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   method)	   are	   multiplied	   for	   the	   uncertainties	   on	   flow	  
(estimated	   through	   the	   fminsearch	   method).	   Also	   in	   this	   event,	   moreover,	   the	   load	   coming	  
from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  is	  a	  bit	  lower	  compared	  to	  the	  load	  interval	  coming	  from	  the	  
measured	  data.	  
6.3.1. Different	  intersection	  method	  
The	  load	  calculation	  has	  been	  made	  also	  for	  the	  events	  analysed	  with	  a	  different	  intersection	  
criterion	  (MC3	  and	  RF1).	  The	  results	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  for	  the	  simulated	  data	  coming	  from	  
the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  are	  presented	  in	  Table	  21,	  but	  only	  for	  the	  case	  in	  which	  
k=1000	  (all	  the	  parameter	  sets	  are	  considered	  behavioural).	  The	  results	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  
for	  the	  measured	  data	  and	  for	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  do	  not	  change	  
from	  the	  previous	  case.	  The	  results	  (minimum,	  maximum	  and	  median	  values)	  coming	  from	  the	  
selection	  of	  a	  different	  number	  of	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  are	  shown	  in	  Appendix	  G.	  The	  
values	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  for	  each	  parameter	  set	  with	  different	  k	  values	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  
100,	   250,	   500,	   750,	   1000)	   for	   the	   different	   intersection	   method	   are	   given	   in	   Electronic	  
Appendix	  2,	  for	  rain	  events	  MC3	  and	  RF1.	  
Table	  21:	  results	  of	  the	  load	  calculation	  for	  the	  simulated	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  for	  
the	  minimum,	  maximum	  and	  median	  value	  of	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  (k=1000),	  together	  with	  the	  RMSE	  
value	  for	  the	  events	  analysed	  with	  the	  different	  intersection	  criterion.

loads	  coming	  from	  the	  two	  calibration	  methods	  is	  given	  hereunder	  for	  the	  rain	  event	  MC3	  and

71  
7. CONCLUSIONS	  
The	  aim	  of	  this	  thesis	  was	  to	  calibrate	  the	  quantity	  (flow)	  and	  the	  quality	  (ammonia)	  part	  of	  a	  
lumped	  conceptual	  model	  created	  through	  the	  software	  WEST.	  The	  model	  should	  be	  able	  to	  
reproduce	   the	   data	   coming	   from	   the	   AMOK	   project,	   which	   provided,	   through	   the	   use	   of	  
sensors,	   an	   extensive	   dataset	   for	   flow	   and	   ammonia	   at	   the	   inlet	   of	   a	   CSO	   structure	   of	   the	  
WWTP	   of	   Viby.	   The	   monitoring	   project	   was	   characterized	   by	   high	   time	   resolution	  
measurements	   (one	   measurement	   per	   minute)	   and	   it	   lasted	   727	   days,	   from	   June	   2013	   to	  
December	   2014.	   The	   aim	   of	   the	   AMOK	   project	   was	   to	   determine	   the	   pollutant	   loads	  
discharged,	  without	  a	  proper	  treatment,	  into	  the	  environment	  during	  overflow	  events.	  
	  
The	  collection	  of	  high	  accuracy	  and	  high	  precision	  data	  resulted	  to	  be	  troublesome,	  both	  for	  
the	   challenging	   condition	   the	   sensor	   are	   subjected	   to	   (fouling,	   clogging)	   and	   for	   the	   rarely	  
performed	  cleaning	  and	  maintenance	  of	  the	  sensors.	  Through	  the	  comparison	  between	  sensor	  
data	  and	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  7	  measuring	  campaigns	  performed	  within	  the	  AMOK	  project,	  it	  
was	  possible	  to	  correct	  the	  investigated	  irregularities	  by	  performing	  a	  sensor	  calibration	  (that,	  
however,	  was	  a	  one	  point	  calibration	  and	  therefore	  often	  the	  correction	  resulted	  to	  be	  partial	  
and	  inconsistent).	  
	  
By	  using	  the	  WEST	  model	  with	  Matlab	  as	  an	  interface,	  it	  has	  been	  possible	  to	  perform	  two	  
different	  calibrations	  on	  the	  model	  parameters:	  one	  on	  the	  water	  quantity	  module	  (flow)	  and	  
one	   on	   the	   water	   quality	   module	   (ammonia).	   The	   water	   quantity	   module	   was	   investigated	  
through	  a	  traditional	  calibration	  method	  (fminsearch),	  while	  the	  water	  quality	  module	  with	  a	  
traditional	  one	  (fminsearch)	  and	  an	  uncertainty	  based	  one	  (modified	  Monte	  Carlo).	  Both	  the	  
flow	  and	  ammonia	  calibrations	  have	  been	  performed	  on	  11	  rain	  events	  and	  on	  one	  long	  term	  
event.	   By	   using	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	   method,	   both	   the	   model	   uncertainty	  
bounds	   and	   the	   uncertainties	   coming	   from	   each	   measured	   datapoint	   have	   been	   taken	   into	  
account.	  Through	  a	  quantification	  of	  the	  uncertainties	  affecting	  the	  model	  and	  the	  sensor	  data	  
it	   was	   possible	   to	   have	   a	   measure	   of	   the	   reliability	   of	   the	   calibration	   results.	   Moreover,	   a	  
calculation	   of	   the	   ammonia	   loads	   was	   performed,	   with	   the	   ammonia	   concentration	   values	  
coming	  from	  the	  traditional	  and	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  methods.	  	  
	  
The	  results	  obtained	  are	  summarised	  through	  the	  following	  bullet	  points:	  
1) The	   parameters’	   values	   resulting	   from	   the	   fminsearch	   calibration	   on	   the	   water	  
quantity	   module	   were	   identified,	   together	   with	   their	   RMSE	   values.	   The	   calibration	  
turned	  out	  to	  be	  quite	  accurate.	  Being	  the	  fminsearch	  a	  tool	  for	  finding	  the	  minimum	  
of	  a	  scalar	  function,	  only	  one	  value	  for	  each	  parameter	  has	  been	  identified	  for	  each	  
investigated	  event.	  
2) The	   parameters’	   values	   resulting	   from	   the	   fminsearch	   calibration	   and	   from	   the	  
modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	   on	   the	   water	   quality	   module	   were	   identified,	  
together	  with	  their	  RMSE	  values.	  Through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  based	  calibration	  
method	  the	  concept	  of	  equifinality	  has	  been	  introduced:	  there	  are	  many	  parameter	  
sets	  able	  to	  give	  an	  equally	  good	  fit	  between	  simulated	  and	  measured	  data	  (Deletic	  et	  
al.,	  2012;	  Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  
==PAGE:87==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
72  
3) By	  comparing	  the	  two	  quality	  calibration	  methods,	  it	  was	  found	  that	  the	  fminsearch	  
gives	   lower	   RMSE	   values	   than	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo.	   However,	   these	   results	  
turned	   out	   not	   to	   be	   representative	   of	   the	   goodness	   of	   the	   calibration	   since	   the	  
measured	   data	   are	   not	   precise,	   as	   it	   can	   be	   seen	   through	   the	   comparison	   between	  
sensor	  data	  and	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  measuring	  campaigns.	  Moreover,	  in	  many	  of	  
the	  analysed	  events,	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds	  were	  able	  to	  overlap	  the	  measures	  
of	  ammonia	  concentration	  coming	  from	  the	  grab	  samples	  (which	  are	  considered	  to	  be	  
the	   most	   reliable	   measurements	   of	   the	   ammonia	   concentration),	   while	   the	   sensor	  
measurements	   and	   the	   fminsearch	   output	   were	   far	   from	   them.	   In	   other	   words,	   by	  
taking	   the	   uncertainties	   (both	   in	   the	   model	   output	   and	   in	   the	   measured	   data)	   into	  
consideration,	   the	   risk	   related	   to	   the	   use	   of	   low	   quality	   data	   is	   reduced	   and	   the	  
likelihood	  of	  intercepting	  the	  grab	  sample	  measurements	  is	  increased.	  
4) The	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   method	   can	   be	   used	   for	   a	   long	   period	   calibration	   on	   an	  
entire	  time	  series	  of	  ammonia	  concentration,	  despite	  the	  fact	  that	  the	  series	  contains	  
sensor	   calibration	   events	   inside.	   This	   fact	   generally	   compromises	   the	   possibility	   of	  
using	  the	  data,	  unless	  the	  series	  is	  cut	  into	  separate	  events	  in	  which	  the	  sensor	  did	  not	  
detect	  any	  calibration.	  A	  long	  period	  has	  been	  properly	  selected,	  in	  order	  to	  have	  two	  
sensor	  calibrations	  inside,	  which	  were	  performed	  by	  using	  a	  single	  point	  calibration	  
method	  that	  resulted	  in	  a	  partial	  and	  not	  consistent	  sensor	  correction	  and	  therefore,	  
once	   again,	   in	  a	  low	  quality	   of	   the	  measured	  data.	  Also	  in	  this	  case	  the	   fminsearch	  
output	  turned	  out	  to	  be	  closer	  to	  the	  measured	  data	  than	  the	  median	  of	  the	  model	  
prediction	  bounds,	  but	  the	  model	  uncertainty	  bounds	  were	  able	  to	  intercept	  the	  grab	  
sample	  concentration	  values.	  	  	  
5) The	   calculation	   of	   the	   ammonia	   loads	   has	   been	   performed	   with	   the	   ammonia	  
concentration	   values	   coming	   from	   the	   outputs	   of	   the	   two	   calibration	   methods,	   and	  
their	  RMSE	  value	  has	  been	  calculated.	  The	  same	  considerations	  made	  until	  now	  can	  be	  
applied	   also	   to	   the	   load	   calculation:	   the	   RMSE	   value	   is	   always	   lower	   for	   the	   loads	  
coming	  from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  than	  for	  the	  ones	  coming	  from	  the	  modified	  
Monte	  Carlo	  calibration,	  but	  this	  is	  probably	  due	  to	  the	  low	  quality	  of	  the	  measured	  
data.	  
	  
Therefore,	  in	  general,	  it	  can	  be	  affirmed	  that	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  method,	  in	  
case	   of	   neither	   precise	   nor	   accurate	   measured	   data,	   gives	   a	   better	   and	   more	   reliable	  
representation	   of	   the	   real	   ammonia	   concentration	   values	   (the	   ones	   coming	   from	   the	   grab	  
samples)	  than	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  method	  and	  it	  reduces	  the	  risks	  associated	  with	  the	  
use	  of	  low	  quality	  measured	  data.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:88==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
73  
8. FURTHER	  OUTLOOKS	  
Since	  the	  elimination	  of	  model	  uncertainties	  is	  not	  possible,	  further	  works	  should	  try	  to	  reduce	  
them	   as	   much	   as	   possible.	   There	   are	   three	   sources	   of	   uncertainty	   in	   models	   (input	   or	  
measurement,	  parameter	  and	  model	  structure	  uncertainties),	  so	  that	  focus	  should	  be	  put	  on	  
each	  of	  them	  for	  their	  reduction:	  
• 
Measurement	  uncertainty	  
Since	  it	  has	  been	  demonstrated	  the	  importance	  of	  having	  high	  quality	  data	  from	  the	  
sensors,	   further	   works	   should	   focus	   on	   reducing	   the	   uncertainties	   linked	   to	   sensor	  
measurements,	   through	   the	   actuation	   of	   frequent	   cleaning	   and	   maintenance	  
campaigns	  on	  the	  sensors.	  To	  avoid	  wrong	  sensor	  correction,	  moreover,	  a	  two	  point	  
calibration	   should	   be	   performed.	   Additionally,	   more	   data	   validation	   should	   also	   be	  
performed	  for	  pollutant	  concentration	  values,	  and	  this	  can	  be	  achieved	  by	  increasing	  
the	   number	   of	   measuring	   campaigns.	   Another	   way	   for	   improving	   the	   quality	   of	   the	  
AMOK	  project	  would	  be	  to	  put	  an	  additional	  flow	  sensor	  at	  the	  CSO	  structure,	  where	  
the	  quality	  sensors	  have	  been	  placed.	  
	  
Through	  the	  use	  of	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  method,	  a	  value	  of	  the	  uncertainty	  
related	   to	   the	   measured	   data	   has	   been	   provided	   (±   30%	   for	   each	   observed	   datapoint).	  
However,	   this	   is	   just	   a	   simplification,	   since	   the	   uncertainties	   have	   a	   growing	   tendency	   over	  
time.	  A	  different	  way	  for	  taking	  the	  time	  into	  account	  in	  the	  uncertainty	  propagation	  has	  to	  be	  
investigated.	  	  	  
• 
Parameter	  uncertainty	  
These	  uncertainties	  are	  those	  regarding	  the	  choice	  of	  the	  calibration	  algorithm	  and	  of	  
the	  objective	  function.	  To	  reduce	  them,	  more	  than	  one	  calibration	  algorithm	  should	  
always	   be	   used,	   for	   comparing	   the	   parameter	   values	   coming	   from	   two	   different	  
calibration	  techniques.	  Additionally,	  more	  than	  one	  performance	  criterion	  should	  be	  
used,	  in	  order	  to	  assess	  the	  model	  reliability	  under	  different	  aspects.	  In	  this	  project	  
only	  the	  RMSE	  has	  been	  used.	  	  
	  
Since	  the	  development	  of	  a	  model	  is	  an	  iterative	  process,	  when	  the	  results	  of	  the	  performance	  
evaluation	   are	   not	   satisfactory,	   additional	   studies	   must	   be	   done:	   by	   using	   different	   data,	  
different	  calibration	  procedures	  or	  by	  modifying	  the	  model	  structure	  (Bennet	  at	  al,	  2013).	  
• 
Model	  structure	  uncertainty	  
These	   uncertainties	   are	   those	   coming	   from	   an	   imperfect	   physical	   description	   of	   the	  
model	  (Freni	  et	  al.,	  2009).	  An	  example	  of	  this	  is	  that	  in	  the	  WEST	  model	  the	  spatial	  
variability	   of	   the	   rainfall	   was	   not	   taken	   into	   account.	   “Tools	   that	   can	   accurately	  
interpolate	  both	  spatial	  and	  temporal	  variability	  in	  rainfall	  would	  provide	  more	  reliable	  
estimates	   of	   point	   rainfall	   and	   consequently	   runoff”	   (Zoppou,	   2001).	   Another	  
improvement	  could	  be	  to	  include	  the	  pump	  activity	  in	  the	  model,	  since	  its	  absence	  has	  
caused	  problems	  in	  the	  flow	  calibration.	  
	  
==PAGE:89==
DTU	  Environment	  
M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
74  
Some	  more	  aspects	  regarding	  the	  uncertainty	  calibration	  should	  be	  further	  examined.	  Other	  
uncertainty	  investigation	  methods	  should	  be	  used	  on	  the	  same	  dataset,	  in	  order	  to	  identify	  
which	  method	  gives	  a	  lower	  error	  and	  a	  higher	  coverage.	  It	  is	  important	  to	  underline	  that	  “the	  
most	   appropriate	   method	   for	   uncertainty	   estimation	   is	   a	   trade-­‐off	   between	   the	   need	   for	   a	  
strong	  theory-­‐based	  description	  of	  uncertainty,	  simplicity	  and	  computational	  efficiency”	  (Dotto	  
et	   al.,	   2012).	   Furthermore,	   when	   the	   aim	   of	   the	   calibration	   is	   the	   water	   quality	   part,	   a	  
simultaneous	  calibration	  of	  flow	  and	  pollutants	  can	  give	  as	  a	  result	  a	  lower	  RMSE	  value.	  
	  
To	  conclude,	  for	  the	  water	  quality	  part	  only	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  has	  been	  taken	  into	  
account	  in	  this	  project.	  Everything	  made	  in	  this	  work	  can	  be	  repeated	  using	  the	  values	  of	  TSS	  
concentration	  instead	  of	  the	  ones	  of	  ammonia,	  since	  a	  turbidity	  sensor	  has	  been	  installed	  at	  
the	  CSO	  structure	  of	  the	  Viby	  WWTP	  and	  therefore	  the	  TSS	  concentration	  measurements	  are	  
available	  for	  the	  same	  time	  period.	  
	  
The	   recommendations	   given	   in	   this	   chapter	   can	   lead	   to	   an	   increased	   reliability	   on	   the	  
calibration	   outputs,	   so	   that	   a	   more	   accurate	   and	   precise	   measure	   of	   the	   pollutants’	   loads	  
discharged	  from	  the	  CSO	  structure	  into	  the	  environment	  without	  a	  proper	  treatment	  can	  be	  
obtained.	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
	  
==PAGE:90==

M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016

APPENDIX	  B	  
Seven	  measuring	  campaigns	  have	  been	  performed	  from	  September	  2013	  to	  October	  2014.	  The	  
first	   and	   the	   second	   campaigns	   show	   to	   have	   low	   accuracy	   and	   low	   precision	   and	   for	   this	  
reason	  they	  are	  not	  used	  in	  this	  study.	  A	  graphical	  overview	  of	  the	  results	  from	  measuring	  
campaigns	  3,	  4,	  5,	  6	  and	  7	  is	  here	  presented.	  The	  graphs	  show	  the	  ammonia	  concentration	  
(mg/l)	  measured	  with	  the	  sensor	  (red	  line)	  and	  with	  grab	  samples	  (brown	  diamonds).

APPENDIX	  C	  
Here	  the	  Matlab	  scripts	  used	  for	  the	  calibration	  of	  the	  parameters	  of	  the	  model	  created	  with	  
WEST	  are	  presented.	  The	  scripts	  are	  subdivided	  according	  to	  the	  type	  of	  calibration	  method	  
used	  (fminsearch	  or	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo),	  and	  each	  type	  of	  calibration	  method	  has	  another	  
subdivision	   for	   the	   type	   of	   parameters	   investigated	   (hydraulic	   parameters	   or	   water	   quality	  
parameters).	  The	  scripts	  here	  presented	  refer	  to	  a	  specific	  rain	  event	  (that	  is	  specified	  at	  the	  
beginning	  of	  each	  subdivision),	  but	  they	  can	  be	  applied	  to	  every	  rain	  event	  as	  long	  as	  the	  event	  
date	  within	  the	  script	  is	  changed.	  At	  the	  end	  of	  this	  appendix	  two	  scripts	  (Comparison1	  and	  
Comparison	   2)	   for	   the	   calculation	   of	   the	   RMSE	   in	   the	   case	   of	   fminsearch	   calibration	   and	  
modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  calibration	  are	  shown.	  
Ø fminsearch	  calibration	  
• 
For	  the	  hydraulic	  part	  
The	   two	   scripts	   presented	   here	   (one	   called	   autoCalWEST	   and	   the	   other	   one	   called	  
runwest_MC_opt)	  refers	  to	  the	  first	  investigated	  rain	  event	  (MC3).	  
autoCalWEST	  
clear all 
close all 
 
global dataToCal 
 
%% load files of measurements 
load('VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV.mat') %Inflow Viby 
 
% declare which period of data should be used for calibration 
(the matrix indexes correspond to the period from 10/05/14 to 
12/05/14, measuring campaign 3) 
dataToCal=[VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV(486608:489488,1)-datenum('01-Jan-
2013'),VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV(486608:489488,2)]; 
 
% define starting point 
x0=[0.07, 0.6]; 
options=optimset('Display','iter'); 
 
%% run optimizer 
optPar=fminsearch(@runwest_MC_opt,x0,options) 
runwest_MC_opt	  
function RMSE=runwest_MC_opt(param)     
%clear all 
 
global dataToCal 
 
%name of the WEST model to be run 
nameFile='Fra_bunden.Dynamic.ObjEval.Exp.xml';

M.Sc.	  Thesis	  
May	  2016	  
	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
xi  
% create a structure with the info about the parameters you want 
to change. 
%  
par(1).name='.Combined.k'; 
par(1).value=param(1); % k [d-1] 
%  
%  
par(2).name='.Combined.PerviousFraction'; 
par(2).value=param(2); %  [] 
 
%% run the WEST model (this part should not be changed)  
% create string with command 
dummyString='"'; 
for i=1:size(par,2) 
    dummyString=[dummyString 
par(i).name 
'=' 
num2str(par(i).value) ';']; 
end 
dummyString=[dummyString '"']; 
 
% commandString=['!texec ' nameFile ' -sp -i '  dummyString]; 
commandString=['!texec ' nameFile ' -q -i '  dummyString]; 
 
% run model 
eval(commandString) 
 
%read output file from WEST  
D=dlmread('Viby.out.txt','\t',2,0); 
D(:,2)=D(:,2)*1000/86400; 
 
%% calculate RMSE 
Err=nan(length(D),1); % create empy vector - lenght of simulated 
values (which are usually shorter than meas values) 
 
% create index vector to find out which simulation step 
corresponds to 
% which observation 
edges=bsxfun(@plus,dataToCal(1:end-1,1),dataToCal(2:end,1))/2; % 
create points between obs steps 
edges=[-Inf;edges;Inf]; %add extremes to interval 
[~,simIdx]=histc(D(:,1),edges); % find to which interval each 
simulation step belongs 
% if simIdx(end)>length(D) 
%     simIdx(end)=length(D); 
% end 
 
% 
ii=find(dataToCal(:,1)>D(end,1),1)-1; 
% 
find 
the 
last 
measurement corresponding to the last simulated values 
for i=1:length(simIdx);% size(D,1) % calculate residuals 
    if  dataToCal(simIdx(i),2)>250 
    Err(i,1)=(dataToCal(simIdx(i),2)-D(i,2))^2; 
    end 
end 
Err(isnan(Err),:)=[]; %remove empty places

	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  	  Valentina	  Poli	  
	  
	  
xii	  
RMSE=mean(Err)^.5; 
 
%% plot data -- (comment this part to speed up simulations)  
hold on 
plot(dataToCal(:,1),dataToCal(:,2),'r') 
hold on 
plot(D(:,1),D(:,2),'b') 
ylabel('Flow (l/s)') 
xlabel('Time (d)') 
legend('meas','simul') 
 
pause(.5) 
%%  
 
end 
• 
For	  the	  water	  quality	  part:	  ammonia	  
The	  scripts	  here	  presented	  refers	  to	  the	  long	  term	  period,	  since	  it	  is	  the	  only	  period	  
which	  has	  been	  investigated	  through	  the	  use	  of	  an	  fminsearch	  calibration	  approach	  for

objFun=NaN(nSim,1);

Intersection	  
% INTERSECTION.m is a file that evaluates the intesction between 
the uncertainty bounds of available measurements and the 
simulated prediction bounds.  
% The results are provided as fraction of the observation where 
intersection is observed: 
% The score is calculated as 1 - there is intersection 
      %                            0 - there is no intersection 
% Input: obs_min,obs_max,sim_min,sim_max 
% 
function out=intersection(obs_min,obs_max,sim_min,sim_max) 
 
n=length(obs_min); % number of observations 
 
score=zeros(n,1); % vector of scores 
 
for i=1:n 
    if obs_min(i)>sim_max(i) 
        score(i)=0; % simulated bounds are below the min obs 
interval 
    elseif obs_max(i)<sim_min(i)  % simulated bounds are above 
the obs interval 
        score(i)=0; 
    else 
        score(i)=1; 
    end 
 
end 
==PAGE:115==
DTU	  Environment

xxii  
 
out=sum(score)/n; 
Intersection	  1	  
% INTERSECTION1.m is a file that evaluates the intersection 
between the uncertainty bounds of available measurements and the 
simulated prediction bounds. The results are provided as 
fraction of the observation where intersection is observed. 
% the score is calculated as:  
% 1 - there is complete intersection (obs_min<sim_min & 
sim_max<obs_max) 
% 0 - there is no intersection 
% 0<x<1 - one between sim_min and sim_max is inside the bounds 
of the measured data 
% Input: obs_min,obs_max,sim_min,sim_max, obsVal 
% 
 
function 
out=intersection1(obs_min,obs_max,sim_min,sim_max,obsVal) 
 
n=length(obs_min); % number of observations 
 
score=zeros(n,1); % vector of scores 
 
for i=1:n 
    if obs_min(i)>sim_max(i) 
        score(i)=0; % simulated bounds are below the min obs 
interval 
    elseif obs_max(i)<sim_min(i)  % simulated bounds are above 
the obs interval 
        score(i)=0; 
    elseif sim_max(i)>obs_min(i) && sim_max(i)<obs_max(i) && 
sim_min(i)<obs_min(i) 
        score(i)=(sim_max(i)-obs_min(i))/(sim_max(i)-
sim_min(i)); 
    elseif sim_min(i)>obs_min(i) && sim_min(i)<obs_max(i) && 
sim_max(i)>obs_max(i) 
        score(i)=(obs_max(i)-sim_min(i))/(sim_max(i)-
sim_min(i)); 
    else 
        score(i)=1; 
    end 
 
end 
 
out=sum(score)/n;

function [h]=OverlappingPlot(data1,data2) 
 
% this function creates an area plot fo two bounds, changing 
colors when the two input datasets are overlapping

% INPUTS  data1 -> array with 3 rows (x, lower bound, upper 
bound) 
%                 data2 -> array with 3 rows (x, lower bound, 
upper bound) 
% OBS data1 and data2 should have the same x-values 
 
if size(data1,1)>size(data1,2) 
    data1=data1'; 
    data2=data2'; 
end 
 
if ~isequal(data1(1,:),data2(1,:)) 
    disp('the two datasets do not have the same x-values') 
    return 
end 
 
figure; 
h=gca; 
hold on 
 
% store the data 
AllData=[data1(2:3,:);data2(2:3,:)]; 
x=data1(1,:); 
 
newData=zeros(size(AllData)); 
idx=zeros(size(AllData)); 
% re-order the data 
for i=1:length(AllData) 
    [newData(:,i) idx(:,i)]=sort(AllData(:,i),'descend'); 
end 
 
flex=zeros(length(idx),1); 
for i=2:length(idx) 
    flex(i,1)=sum(abs(idx(:,i)-idx(:,i-1))); 
end 
flexPt=find(flex>0); 
flexPt(2:end+1)=flexPt(1:end); 
flexPt(1)=1; 
flexPt(end+1)=length(flex); 
 
% p=zeros(length(flexPt),1); 
for i=1:length(flexPt)-1 
    p(i,1)=patch([x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)) 
fliplr(x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)))], ... 
        [newData(1,flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)) 
fliplr(newData(2,flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)))],... 
        [.9 .9 .9]); 
    p(i,2)=patch([x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)) 
fliplr(x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)))], ... 
        [newData(2,flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)) 
fliplr(newData(3,flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)))],... 
        [.9 .9 .9]); 
    p(i,3)=patch([x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)) 
fliplr(x(flexPt(i):flexPt(i+1)))], ... 
==PAGE:117==

        [.9 .9 .9]);     
 
end 
% define colors 
set(p,'EdgeColor','none') 
cmap=[1 .6 .6; %red (data #1) 
      1 .2 .8; %violet (intersection of data#1 and data#2) 
      .4 .8 1; %blue (data #2) 
      1 1 1]; % white (no intersection) 
%loop to define colors based on cases 
 
for i=1:length(flexPt)-1 
    if isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[4 3 2 1]') %data #1 - white - 
data #2 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(4,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
    elseif isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[4 2 3 1]') %data #1 - 
overlapping - data #2 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(2,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
    elseif isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[4 2 1 3]') %data #1 - data 
#2 (overlapping) - data#1 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(2,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
    elseif isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[2 4 3 1]') %data #2 -  data 
#1 (overlapping) - data#2 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(2,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
    elseif isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[2 4 1 3]') %data #2 - 
overlapping - data#1 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(2,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
    elseif isequal(idx(:,flexPt(i)),[2 1 4 3]') %data #2 - white 
- data #1 
        set(p(i,1),'FaceColor',cmap(3,:)) 
        set(p(i,2),'FaceColor',cmap(4,:)) 
        set(p(i,3),'FaceColor',cmap(1,:)) 
    end 
 
end 
 
end

Ø RMSE	  calculation	  
Comparison1	  
measData=[XM_AM_UF707_PV(:,1)-datenum('01-Jan-
2013'),XM_AM_UF707_PV(:,2)]; %Ammonium inlet 
 
tStart=datenum('10-May-2014 00:00')-datenum('01-Jan-2013'); 
tStop=datenum('12-May-2014 00:00')-datenum('01-Jan-2013'); 
 
idxStart=find(measData(:,1)>tStart,1)-1; 
idxStop=find(measData(:,1)>tStop,1)-1;  
obsVal=measData(idxStart:idxStop,:); 
 
D=dlmread('LeaMC3amm.txt','\t',0,0); %file di output of Olesen 
 
%% calculate RMSE 
Err=nan(length(D),1); % create empy vector 
 
% create index vector to find out which simulation step 
corresponds to which observation 
edges=bsxfun(@plus,obsVal(1:end-1,1),obsVal(2:end,1))/2; 
% 
create points between obs steps 
edges=[-Inf;edges;Inf]; %add extremes to interval 
[~,simIdx]=histc(D(:,1),edges); % find to which interval each 
simulation step belongs 
 
 
for i=1:size(obsVal,1) % calculate residuals 
    Err(i,1)=(obsVal(i,2)-D(simIdx(i),2))^2; 
end 
 
Err(isnan(Err),:)=[]; %remove empty places 
 
RMSE=(mean(Err)^.5);

APPENDIX	  D	  
Here	   the	   scripts	   for	   the	   load	   calculation	   are	   presented,	   for	   both	   the	   measured	   data	  
(LoadCalcDataMeasured)	   and	   the	   modelled	   data	   resulting	   from	   the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	  
calibration	  (LoadCalcModMC).	  
LoadCalcDataMeasured	  
load XM_AM_UF707_PV %ammonium inlet 
load VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV %inflow Viby 
 
measDataAMM=[XM_AM_UF707_PV(:,1)-datenum('01-Jan-
2013'),XM_AM_UF707_PV(:,2)]; % vector of meas data with time converted 
to WEST 
measDataFLOW=[VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV(:,1)-datenum('01-Jan-
2013'),VIBY_XM_FT_FB702_PV(:,2)]; 
 
% define simulation period 
tStart=datenum('10-May-2014 00:00')-datenum('01-Jan-2013'); 
tStop=datenum('12-May-2014 00:00')-datenum('01-Jan-2013'); 
 
% create vector with observations for calibration 
idxStart=find(measDataAMM(:,1)>tStart,1)-1; 
idxStop=find(measDataAMM(:,1)>tStop,1)-1;  
obsValAMM=measDataAMM(idxStart:idxStop,:); 
 
% create vector with observations for calibration 
idxStart=find(measDataFLOW(:,1)>tStart,1)-1; 
idxStop=find(measDataFLOW(:,1)>tStop,1)-1;  
obsValFLOW=measDataFLOW(idxStart:idxStop,:); 
 
LoadMeas=obsValAMM(:,2).*obsValFLOW(:,2); 
% 
measured 
mass 
fluxes  
[mg/l] * [l/s] -> [mg/s] 
 
LoadMeas=LoadMeas * 1 * 60; % unit conversion [mg/s] * [min] * [s/min] 
-> [mg] 
 
LoadMeasTot=sum(LoadMeas)/1000000; % total mass  [kg] 
 
% we assume a fixed +/-30% error on all the measurements  
Err=0.3; 
LoadMeasTotMin=LoadMeasTot*(1-Err) 
LoadMeasTotMax=LoadMeasTot*(1+Err) 
 
%save simulation results 
M=[LoadMeas]; 
dlmwrite('Load_measData.txt',M,'delimiter','\t', 'precision',4) 
RMSE1=((LoadMeasTot- 877.34)^2)^0.5

xxviii  
LoadCalcModMC	  
LoadSim=zeros(k-1,1);  %initialize load vector  
dt= 1 /1440; % time resolution of output file  
D=dlmread('Viby.out.txt','\t',2,0); 
 
for i=1:k-1 %loop over number of behavioural parameter sets (index i) 
   for j=1:size((simData),2) % loop over timesteps (index j) 
   LoadSim(i)=LoadSim(i) + D(j,2)*simData(j).tStep(i)*dt/1000; %  flow 
* conc * timestep -> [m3/d] * [kg/m3] * [d] 
   end 
end 
 
L=median(LoadSim) 
MI=min(LoadSim) 
MA=max(LoadSim) 
 
% %save simulation results 
T=[LoadSim]; 
dlmwrite('LoadSim_modMC.txt',T,'delimiter','\t', 'precision',4) 
 
RMSE2=((LoadMeasTot-median(LoadSim))^2)^0.5

for	  the	  modified	  Monte	  Carlo	  method.	  The	  order	  of	  presentation	  of	  the	  results	  will	  follow	  the	  
one	  presented	  in	  Chapter	  6.2.

Ammonia	  calibration

the	   cases	   in	   which	   the	   graph	   ‘no	   of	   simulation	   –	   intersection	   λ	   (%)’	   turned	   out	   to	   have	   a	  
decreasing	   behaviour,	   only	   the	   case	   with	   k=1000	   (all	   the	   parameter	   sets	   are	   used	   for	   the	  
construction	  of	  the	  model	  prediction	  bounds)	  is	  here	  reported,	  since	  it	  has	  been	  explained	  in	  
Chapter	  6.2	  that	  a	  variation	  of	  the	  k	  number	  only	  corresponds	  to	  a	  variation	  of	  the	  bounds’	  
width.	  In	  the	  graphs	  for	  the	  long	  period	  calibration,	  the	  vertical	  green	  lines	  represent	  the	  time	  
in	  which	  the	  ammonium	  sensor	  has	  been	  calibrated.

Behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  
For	  the	  cases	  in	  which	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour	  of	  the	  plot	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’	  
was	  observed,	  the	  values	  of	  the	  minimum,	  median	  and	  maximum	  parameter	  sets	  have	  been	  
calculated	  by	  considering	  fixed	  parameter	  sets	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000).	  In	  
the	  following	  tables	  the	  different	  cases	  are	  presented	  for	  the	  events	  MC7,	  RF1,	  RF2,	  RS1	  and	  
RS3.

Behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  –	  Different	  intersection	  criterion	  
For	   the	   two	   events	   in	   which	   a	   different	   intersection	   criterion	   was	   used	   (MC3	   and	   RF1),	   a	  
decreasing	   behaviour	   of	   the	   plot	   ‘no	   of	   simulation	   –	   intersection	   λ	   (%)’	   is	   observed,	   and	  
therefore	   the	   values	   of	   the	   minimum,	   median	   and	   maximum	   parameter	   sets	   have	   been	  
calculated	  by	  considering	  different	  numbers	  of	  parameter	  sets	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  
750,	  1000).	  In	  the	  following	  tables	  the	  different	  cases	  are	  presented.	  
Table	  A.3:	  minimum,	  maximum,	  median	  values	  for	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  considered,	  obtained	  through	  the	  
application	  of	  the	  different	  intersection	  criterion,	  for	  the	  events	  MC3	  and	  RF1.	  The	  number	  of	  parameter	  sets	  for	  
each	  event	  is	  indicated	  in	  column	  2.

APPENDIX	  G	  
In	   this	   section	   the	   graphical	   results	   of	   the	   load	   calculation	   are	   presented	   for	   the	   measured	  
data,	  for	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  fminsearch	  calibration	  and	  for	  the	  data	  coming	  from	  the	  
modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration.	   All	   the	   load	   results	   are	   expressed	   in	   kilograms	   (kg).	   The	  
graphical	  comparison	  of	  the	  load	  results	  is	  given	  for	  all	  the	  analysed	  events	  (except	  for	  MC3	  
and	  RF1,	  whose	  results	  are	  presentend	  in	  Chapter	  6.3).	  For	  the	  events	  showing	  a	  decreasing	  
behaviour	  in	  the	  graphs	  ‘no	  of	  simulation	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’,	  only	  the	  case	  of	  k=1000	  (i.e.	  all	  
the	  parameter	  sets	  are	  considered	  as	  behavioural)	  is	  here	  presented.

ELECTRONIC	  APPENDIX	  1	  
This	  electronic	  appendix	  contains	  the	  values	  of	  the	  behavioural	  parameter	  sets	  for	  the	  water	  
quality	   module	   calibration	   (the	   modified	   Monte	   Carlo	   calibration)	   for	   all	   the	   analysed	   rain	  
events	   and	   for	   the	   long	   term	   event.	   The	   results	   are	   presented	   for	   both	   the	   considered	  
intersection	  methods	  and	  for	  different	  k	  values	  (k=5,	  10,	  25,	  50,	  100,	  250,	  500,	  750,	  1000)	  in	  
case	  of	  a	  decreasing	  behaviour	  of	  the	  plot	  ‘no	  of	  simulations	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’.

ELECTRONIC	  APPENDIX	  2	  
This	  electronic	  appendix	  contains	  the	  values	  of	  the	  load	  calculation,	  in	  the	  case	  of	  the	  modified	  
Monte	   Carlo	   calibration	   method,	   related	   to	   each	   of	   the	   different	   analysed	   behavioural	  
parameter	   sets	   for	   every	   rain	   event	   and	   for	   both	   the	   intersection	   criteria.	   The	   results	   are	  
presented	   for	   different	   k	   values	   (k=5,	   10,	   25,	   50,	   100,	   250,	   500,	   750,	   1000)	   in	   case	   of	  
decreasing	  behaviour	  of	  the	  plot	  ‘no	  of	  simulations	  –	  intersection	  λ	  (%)’.
